4016 =======
4017 [Enter Lady Capulet and Nurse.]
4018 
4019 
4020 LADY CAPULET
4021 Hold, take these keys, and fetch more spices, nurse.
4022 
4023 NURSE
4024 They call for dates and quinces in the pastry.
4025 
4026 [Enter old Capulet.]
4027 
4028 
4029 CAPULET
4030 Come, stir, stir, stir! The second cock hath crowed.
4031 The curfew bell hath rung. 'Tis three o'clock.--
4032 Look to the baked meats, good Angelica.
4033 Spare not for cost.
4034 
4035 NURSE  Go, you cot-quean, go,
4036 Get you to bed. Faith, you'll be sick tomorrow
4037 For this night's watching.
4038 
4039 CAPULET
4040 No, not a whit. What, I have watched ere now
4041 All night for lesser cause, and ne'er been sick.
4042 
4043 LADY CAPULET
4044 Ay, you have been a mouse-hunt in your time,
4045 But I will watch you from such watching now.
4046 [Lady Capulet and Nurse exit.]
4047 
4048 CAPULET
4049 A jealous hood, a jealous hood!
4050 
4051 [Enter three or four Servingmen with spits and logs
4052 and baskets.]
4053 
4054 Now fellow,
4055 What is there?
4056 
4057 FIRST SERVINGMAN
4058 Things for the cook, sir, but I know not what.
4059 
4060 CAPULET
4061 Make haste, make haste.	[First Servingman exits.]
4062 Sirrah, fetch drier logs.
4063 Call Peter. He will show thee where they are.
4064 
4065 SECOND SERVINGMAN
4066 I have a head, sir, that will find out logs
4067 And never trouble Peter for the matter.
4068 
4069 CAPULET
4070 Mass, and well said. A merry whoreson, ha!
4071 Thou shalt be loggerhead.
4072 [Second Servingman exits.]
4073 Good faith, 'tis day.
4074 The County will be here with music straight,
4075 [Play music.]
4076 For so he said he would. I hear him near.--
4077 Nurse!--Wife! What ho!--What, nurse, I say!
4078 
4079 [Enter Nurse.]
4080 
4081 Go waken Juliet. Go and trim her up.
4082 I'll go and chat with Paris. Hie, make haste,
4083 Make haste. The bridegroom he is come already.
4084 Make haste, I say.
4085 [He exits.]
4086 
4087 Scene 5
4088 =======
4089 
4090 NURSE, [approaching the bed]
4091 Mistress! What, mistress! Juliet!--Fast, I warrant
4092 her, she--
4093 Why, lamb, why, lady! Fie, you slugabed!
4094 Why, love, I say! Madam! Sweetheart! Why, bride!--
4095 What, not a word?--You take your pennyworths
4096 now.
4097 Sleep for a week, for the next night, I warrant,
4098 The County Paris hath set up his rest
4099 That you shall rest but little.--God forgive me,
4100 Marry, and amen! How sound is she asleep!
4101 I needs must wake her.--Madam, madam, madam!
4102 Ay, let the County take you in your bed,
4103 He'll fright you up, i' faith.--Will it not be?
4104 [She opens the bed's curtains.]
4105 What, dressed, and in your clothes, and down
4106 again?
4107 I must needs wake you. Lady, lady, lady!--
4108 Alas, alas! Help, help! My lady's dead.--
4109 O, weraday, that ever I was born!--
4110 Some aqua vitae, ho!--My lord! My lady!
4111 
4112 [Enter Lady Capulet.]
4113 
4114 
4115 LADY CAPULET
4116 What noise is here?
4117 
4118 NURSE  O lamentable day!
4119 
4120 LADY CAPULET
4121 What is the matter?
4122 
4123 NURSE  Look, look!--O heavy day!
4124 
4125 LADY CAPULET
4126 O me! O me! My child, my only life,
4127 Revive, look up, or I will die with thee.
4128 Help, help! Call help.
4129 
4130 [Enter Capulet.]
4131 
4132 
4133 CAPULET
4134 For shame, bring Juliet forth. Her lord is come.
4135 
4136 NURSE
4137 She's dead, deceased. She's dead, alack the day!
4138 
4139 LADY CAPULET
4140 Alack the day, she's dead, she's dead, she's dead.
4141 
4142 CAPULET
4143 Ha, let me see her! Out, alas, she's cold.
4144 Her blood is settled, and her joints are stiff.
4145 Life and these lips have long been separated.
4146 Death lies on her like an untimely frost
4147 Upon the sweetest flower of all the field.
4148 
4149 NURSE
4150 O lamentable day!
4151 
4152 LADY CAPULET  O woeful time!
4153 
4154 CAPULET
4155 Death, that hath ta'en her hence to make me wail,
4156 Ties up my tongue and will not let me speak.
4157 
4158 [Enter Friar Lawrence and the County Paris, with
4159 Musicians.]
4160 
4161 
4162 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4163 Come, is the bride ready to go to church?
4164 
4165 CAPULET
4166 Ready to go, but never to return.--
4167 O son, the night before thy wedding day
4168 Hath Death lain with thy wife. There she lies,
4169 Flower as she was, deflowered by him.
4170 Death is my son-in-law; Death is my heir.
4171 My daughter he hath wedded. I will die
4172 And leave him all. Life, living, all is Death's.
4173 
4174 PARIS
4175 Have I thought long to see this morning's face,
4176 And doth it give me such a sight as this?
4177 
4178 LADY CAPULET
4179 Accursed, unhappy, wretched, hateful day!
4180 Most miserable hour that e'er time saw
4181 In lasting labor of his pilgrimage!
4182 But one, poor one, one poor and loving child,
4183 But one thing to rejoice and solace in,
4184 And cruel death hath catched it from my sight!
4185 
4186 NURSE
4187 O woe, O woeful, woeful, woeful day!
4188 Most lamentable day, most woeful day
4189 That ever, ever I did yet behold!
4190 O day, O day, O day, O hateful day!
4191 Never was seen so black a day as this!
4192 O woeful day, O woeful day!
4193 
4194 PARIS
4195 Beguiled, divorced, wronged, spited, slain!
4196 Most detestable death, by thee beguiled,
4197 By cruel, cruel thee quite overthrown!
4198 O love! O life! Not life, but love in death!
4199 
4200 CAPULET
4201 Despised, distressed, hated, martyred, killed!
4202 Uncomfortable time, why cam'st thou now
4203 To murder, murder our solemnity?
4204 O child! O child! My soul and not my child!
4205 Dead art thou! Alack, my child is dead,
4206 And with my child my joys are buried.
4207 
4208 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4209 Peace, ho, for shame! Confusion's cure lives not
4210 In these confusions. Heaven and yourself
4211 Had part in this fair maid. Now heaven hath all,
4212 And all the better is it for the maid.
4213 Your part in her you could not keep from death,
4214 But heaven keeps his part in eternal life.
4215 The most you sought was her promotion,
4216 For 'twas your heaven she should be advanced;
4217 And weep you now, seeing she is advanced
4218 Above the clouds, as high as heaven itself?
4219 O, in this love you love your child so ill
4220 That you run mad, seeing that she is well.
4221 She's not well married that lives married long,
4222 But she's best married that dies married young.
4223 Dry up your tears, and stick your rosemary
4224 On this fair corse, and, as the custom is,
4225 And in her best array, bear her to church,
4226 For though fond nature bids us all lament,
4227 Yet nature's tears are reason's merriment.
4228 
4229 CAPULET
4230 All things that we ordained festival
4231 Turn from their office to black funeral:
4232 Our instruments to melancholy bells,
4233 Our wedding cheer to a sad burial feast,
4234 Our solemn hymns to sullen dirges change,
4235 Our bridal flowers serve for a buried corse,
4236 And all things change them to the contrary.
4237 
4238 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4239 Sir, go you in, and, madam, go with him,
4240 And go, Sir Paris. Everyone prepare
4241 To follow this fair corse unto her grave.
4242 The heavens do lour upon you for some ill.
4243 Move them no more by crossing their high will.
4244 [All but the Nurse and the Musicians exit.]
4245 
4246 FIRST MUSICIAN
4247 Faith, we may put up our pipes and be gone.
4248 
4249 NURSE
4250 Honest good fellows, ah, put up, put up,
4251 For, well you know, this is a pitiful case.
4252 
4253 FIRST MUSICIAN
4254 Ay, by my troth, the case may be amended.
4255 [Nurse exits.]
4256 
4257 [Enter Peter.]
4258 
4259 
4260 PETER  Musicians, O musicians, "Heart's ease,"
4261 "Heart's ease." O, an you will have me live, play
4262 "Heart's ease."
4263 
4264 FIRST MUSICIAN  Why "Heart's ease?"
4265 
4266 PETER  O musicians, because my heart itself plays "My
4267 heart is full." O, play me some merry dump to
4268 comfort me.
4269 
4270 FIRST MUSICIAN  Not a dump, we. 'Tis no time to play
4271 now.
4272 
4273 PETER  You will not then?
4274 
4275 FIRST MUSICIAN  No.
4276 
4277 PETER  I will then give it you soundly.
4278 
4279 FIRST MUSICIAN  What will you give us?
4280 
4281 PETER  No money, on my faith, but the gleek. I will give
4282 you the minstrel.
4283 
4284 FIRST MUSICIAN  Then will I give you the
4285 serving-creature.
4286 
4287 PETER  Then will I lay the serving-creature's dagger on
4288 your pate. I will carry no crochets. I'll re you, I'll fa
4289 you. Do you note me?
4290 
4291 FIRST MUSICIAN  An you re us and fa us, you note us.
4292 
4293 SECOND MUSICIAN  Pray you, put up your dagger and
4294 put out your wit.
4295 
4296 PETER  Then have at you with my wit. I will dry-beat
4297 you with an iron wit, and put up my iron dagger.
4298 Answer me like men.
4299 [Sings.]	When griping griefs the heart doth wound
4300 	And doleful dumps the mind oppress,
4301 	Then music with her silver sound--
4302 Why "silver sound"? Why "music with her silver
4303 sound"? What say you, Simon Catling?
4304 
4305 FIRST MUSICIAN  Marry, sir, because silver hath a
4306 sweet sound.
4307 
4308 PETER  Prates.--What say you, Hugh Rebeck?
4309 
4310 SECOND MUSICIAN  I say "silver sound" because musicians
4311 sound for silver.
4312 
4313 PETER  Prates too.--What say you, James Soundpost?
4314 
4315 THIRD MUSICIAN  Faith, I know not what to say.
4316 
4317 PETER  O, I cry you mercy. You are the singer. I will say
4318 for you. It is "music with her silver sound" because
4319 musicians have no gold for sounding:
4320 [Sings.]	Then music with her silver sound
4321 	With speedy help doth lend redress.
4322 [He exits.]
4323 
4324 FIRST MUSICIAN  What a pestilent knave is this same!
4325 
4326 SECOND MUSICIAN  Hang him, Jack. Come, we'll in
4327 here, tarry for the mourners, and stay dinner.
4328 [They exit.]
4329 
4330 
4331 ACT 5
4332 =====
4333 
4334 Scene 1
4335 =======
4336 [Enter Romeo.]
4337 
4338 
4339 ROMEO
4340 If I may trust the flattering truth of sleep,
4341 My dreams presage some joyful news at hand.
4342 My bosom's lord sits lightly in his throne,
4343 And all this day an unaccustomed spirit
4344 Lifts me above the ground with cheerful thoughts.
4345 I dreamt my lady came and found me dead
4346 (Strange dream that gives a dead man leave to
4347 think!)
4348 And breathed such life with kisses in my lips
4349 That I revived and was an emperor.
4350 Ah me, how sweet is love itself possessed
4351 When but love's shadows are so rich in joy!
4352 
4353 [Enter Romeo's man Balthasar, in riding boots.]
4354 
4355 News from Verona!--How now, Balthasar?
4356 Dost thou not bring me letters from the Friar?
4357 How doth my lady? Is my father well?
4358 How doth my Juliet? That I ask again,
4359 For nothing can be ill if she be well.
4360 
4361 BALTHASAR
4362 Then she is well and nothing can be ill.
4363 Her body sleeps in Capels' monument,
4364 And her immortal part with angels lives.
4365 I saw her laid low in her kindred's vault
4366 And presently took post to tell it you.
4367 O, pardon me for bringing these ill news,
4368 Since you did leave it for my office, sir.
4369 
4370 ROMEO
4371 Is it e'en so?--Then I deny you, stars!--
4372 Thou knowest my lodging. Get me ink and paper,
4373 And hire post-horses. I will hence tonight.
4374 
4375 BALTHASAR
4376 I do beseech you, sir, have patience.
4377 Your looks are pale and wild and do import
4378 Some misadventure.
4379 
4380 ROMEO  Tush, thou art deceived.
4381 Leave me, and do the thing I bid thee do.
4382 Hast thou no letters to me from the Friar?
4383 
4384 BALTHASAR
4385 No, my good lord.
4386 
4387 ROMEO  No matter. Get thee gone,
4388 And hire those horses. I'll be with thee straight.
4389 [Balthasar exits.]
4390 Well, Juliet, I will lie with thee tonight.
4391 Let's see for means. O mischief, thou art swift
4392 To enter in the thoughts of desperate men.
4393 I do remember an apothecary
4394 (And hereabouts he dwells) which late I noted
4395 In tattered weeds, with overwhelming brows,
4396 Culling of simples. Meager were his looks.
4397 Sharp misery had worn him to the bones.
4398 And in his needy shop a tortoise hung,
4399 An alligator stuffed, and other skins
4400 Of ill-shaped fishes; and about his shelves,
4401 A beggarly account of empty boxes,
4402 Green earthen pots, bladders, and musty seeds,
4403 Remnants of packthread, and old cakes of roses
4404 Were thinly scattered to make up a show.
4405 Noting this penury, to myself I said
4406 "An if a man did need a poison now,
4407 Whose sale is present death in Mantua,
4408 Here lives a caitiff wretch would sell it him."
4409 O, this same thought did but forerun my need,
4410 And this same needy man must sell it me.
4411 As I remember, this should be the house.
4412 Being holiday, the beggar's shop is shut.--
4413 What ho, Apothecary!
4414 
4415 [Enter Apothecary.]
4416 
4417 
4418 APOTHECARY  Who calls so loud?
4419 
4420 ROMEO
4421 Come hither, man. I see that thou art poor.
4422 [He offers money.]
4423 Hold, there is forty ducats. Let me have
4424 A dram of poison, such soon-speeding gear
4425 As will disperse itself through all the veins,
4426 That the life-weary taker may fall dead,
4427 And that the trunk may be discharged of breath
4428 As violently as hasty powder fired
4429 Doth hurry from the fatal cannon's womb.
4430 
4431 APOTHECARY
4432 Such mortal drugs I have, but Mantua's law
4433 Is death to any he that utters them.
4434 
4435 ROMEO
4436 Art thou so bare and full of wretchedness,
4437 And fearest to die? Famine is in thy cheeks,
4438 Need and oppression starveth in thy eyes,
4439 Contempt and beggary hangs upon thy back.
4440 The world is not thy friend, nor the world's law.
4441 The world affords no law to make thee rich.
4442 Then be not poor, but break it, and take this.
4443 
4444 APOTHECARY
4445 My poverty, but not my will, consents.
4446 
4447 ROMEO
4448 I pay thy poverty and not thy will.
4449 
4450 APOTHECARY, [giving him the poison]
4451 Put this in any liquid thing you will
4452 And drink it off, and if you had the strength
4453 Of twenty men, it would dispatch you straight.
4454 
4455 ROMEO, [handing him the money]
4456 There is thy gold, worse poison to men's souls,
4457 Doing more murder in this loathsome world
4458 Than these poor compounds that thou mayst not
4459 sell.
4460 I sell thee poison; thou hast sold me none.
4461 Farewell, buy food, and get thyself in flesh.
4462 [Apothecary exits.]
4463 Come, cordial and not poison, go with me
4464 To Juliet's grave, for there must I use thee.
4465 [He exits.]
4466 
4467 Scene 2
4468 =======
4469 [Enter Friar John.]
4470 
4471 
4472 FRIAR JOHN
4473 Holy Franciscan friar, brother, ho!
4474 
4475 [Enter Friar Lawrence.]
4476 
4477 
4478 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4479 This same should be the voice of Friar John.--
4480 Welcome from Mantua. What says Romeo?
4481 Or, if his mind be writ, give me his letter.
4482 
4483 FRIAR JOHN
4484 Going to find a barefoot brother out,
4485 One of our order, to associate me,
4486 Here in this city visiting the sick,
4487 And finding him, the searchers of the town,
4488 Suspecting that we both were in a house
4489 Where the infectious pestilence did reign,
4490 Sealed up the doors and would not let us forth,
4491 So that my speed to Mantua there was stayed.
4492 
4493 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4494 Who bare my letter, then, to Romeo?
4495 
4496 FRIAR JOHN
4497 I could not send it--here it is again--
4498 [Returning the letter.]
4499 Nor get a messenger to bring it thee,
4500 So fearful were they of infection.
4501 
4502 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4503 Unhappy fortune! By my brotherhood,
4504 The letter was not nice but full of charge,
4505 Of dear import, and the neglecting it
4506 May do much danger. Friar John, go hence.
4507 Get me an iron crow and bring it straight
4508 Unto my cell.
4509 
4510 FRIAR JOHN
4511 Brother, I'll go and bring it thee.	[He exits.]
4512 
4513 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4514 Now must I to the monument alone.
4515 Within this three hours will fair Juliet wake.
4516 She will beshrew me much that Romeo
4517 Hath had no notice of these accidents.
4518 But I will write again to Mantua,
4519 And keep her at my cell till Romeo come.
4520 Poor living corse, closed in a dead man's tomb!
4521 [He exits.]
4522 
4523 Scene 3
4524 =======
4525 [Enter Paris and his Page.]
4526 
4527 
4528 PARIS
4529 Give me thy torch, boy. Hence and stand aloof.
4530 Yet put it out, for I would not be seen.
4531 Under yond yew trees lay thee all along,
4532 Holding thy ear close to the hollow ground.
4533 So shall no foot upon the churchyard tread
4534 (Being loose, unfirm, with digging up of graves)
4535 But thou shalt hear it. Whistle then to me
4536 As signal that thou hearest something approach.
4537 Give me those flowers. Do as I bid thee. Go.
4538 
4539 PAGE, [aside]
4540 I am almost afraid to stand alone
4541 Here in the churchyard. Yet I will adventure.
4542 [He moves away from Paris.]
4543 
4544 PARIS, [scattering flowers]
4545 Sweet flower, with flowers thy bridal bed I strew
4546 (O woe, thy canopy is dust and stones!)
4547 Which with sweet water nightly I will dew,
4548 Or, wanting that, with tears distilled by moans.
4549 The obsequies that I for thee will keep
4550 Nightly shall be to strew thy grave and weep.
4551 [Page whistles.]
4552 The boy gives warning something doth approach.
4553 What cursed foot wanders this way tonight,
4554 To cross my obsequies and true love's rite?
4555 What, with a torch? Muffle me, night, awhile.
4556 [He steps aside.]
4557 
4558 [Enter Romeo and Balthasar.]
4559 
4560 
4561 ROMEO
4562 Give me that mattock and the wrenching iron.
4563 Hold, take this letter. Early in the morning
4564 See thou deliver it to my lord and father.
4565 Give me the light. Upon thy life I charge thee,
4566 Whate'er thou hearest or seest, stand all aloof
4567 And do not interrupt me in my course.
4568 Why I descend into this bed of death
4569 Is partly to behold my lady's face,
4570 But chiefly to take thence from her dead finger
4571 A precious ring, a ring that I must use
4572 In dear employment. Therefore hence, begone.
4573 But, if thou, jealous, dost return to pry
4574 In what I farther shall intend to do,
4575 By heaven, I will tear thee joint by joint
4576 And strew this hungry churchyard with thy limbs.
4577 The time and my intents are savage-wild,
4578 More fierce and more inexorable far
4579 Than empty tigers or the roaring sea.
4580 
4581 BALTHASAR
4582 I will be gone, sir, and not trouble you.
4583 
4584 ROMEO
4585 So shalt thou show me friendship. Take thou that.
4586 [Giving money.]
4587 Live and be prosperous, and farewell, good fellow.
4588 
4589 BALTHASAR, [aside]
4590 For all this same, I'll hide me hereabout.
4591 His looks I fear, and his intents I doubt.
4592 [He steps aside.]
4593 
4594 ROMEO, [beginning to force open the tomb]
4595 Thou detestable maw, thou womb of death,
4596 Gorged with the dearest morsel of the earth,
4597 Thus I enforce thy rotten jaws to open,
4598 And in despite I'll cram thee with more food.
4599 
4600 PARIS
4601 This is that banished haughty Montague
4602 That murdered my love's cousin, with which grief
4603 It is supposed the fair creature died,
4604 And here is come to do some villainous shame
4605 To the dead bodies. I will apprehend him.
4606 [Stepping forward.]
4607 Stop thy unhallowed toil, vile Montague.
4608 Can vengeance be pursued further than death?
4609 Condemned villain, I do apprehend thee.
4610 Obey and go with me, for thou must die.
4611 
4612 ROMEO
4613 I must indeed, and therefore came I hither.
4614 Good gentle youth, tempt not a desp'rate man.
4615 Fly hence and leave me. Think upon these gone.
4616 Let them affright thee. I beseech thee, youth,
4617 Put not another sin upon my head
4618 By urging me to fury. O, begone!
4619 By heaven, I love thee better than myself,
4620 For I come hither armed against myself.
4621 Stay not, begone, live, and hereafter say
4622 A madman's mercy bid thee run away.
4623 
4624 PARIS
4625 I do defy thy commination
4626 And apprehend thee for a felon here.
4627 
4628 ROMEO
4629 Wilt thou provoke me? Then have at thee, boy!
4630 [They draw and fight.]
4631 
4632 PAGE
4633 O Lord, they fight! I will go call the watch.
4634 [He exits.]
4635 
4636 PARIS
4637 O, I am slain! If thou be merciful,
4638 Open the tomb; lay me with Juliet.	[He dies.]
4639 
4640 ROMEO
4641 In faith, I will.--Let me peruse this face.
4642 Mercutio's kinsman, noble County Paris!
4643 What said my man when my betossed soul
4644 Did not attend him as we rode? I think
4645 He told me Paris should have married Juliet.
4646 Said he not so? Or did I dream it so?
4647 Or am I mad, hearing him talk of Juliet,
4648 To think it was so?--O, give me thy hand,
4649 One writ with me in sour misfortune's book!
4650 I'll bury thee in a triumphant grave.--
4651 [He opens the tomb.]
4652 A grave? O, no. A lantern, slaughtered youth,
4653 For here lies Juliet, and her beauty makes
4654 This vault a feasting presence full of light.--
4655 Death, lie thou there, by a dead man interred.
4656 [Laying Paris in the tomb.]
4657 How oft when men are at the point of death
4658 Have they been merry, which their keepers call
4659 A light'ning before death! O, how may I
4660 Call this a light'ning?--O my love, my wife,
4661 Death, that hath sucked the honey of thy breath,
4662 Hath had no power yet upon thy beauty.
4663 Thou art not conquered. Beauty's ensign yet
4664 Is crimson in thy lips and in thy cheeks,
4665 And death's pale flag is not advanced there.--
4666 Tybalt, liest thou there in thy bloody sheet?
4667 O, what more favor can I do to thee
4668 Than with that hand that cut thy youth in twain
4669 To sunder his that was thine enemy?
4670 Forgive me, cousin.--Ah, dear Juliet,
4671 Why art thou yet so fair? Shall I believe
4672 That unsubstantial death is amorous,
4673 And that the lean abhorred monster keeps
4674 Thee here in dark to be his paramour?
4675 For fear of that I still will stay with thee
4676 And never from this palace of dim night
4677 Depart again. Here, here will I remain
4678 With worms that are thy chambermaids. O, here
4679 Will I set up my everlasting rest
4680 And shake the yoke of inauspicious stars
4681 From this world-wearied flesh! Eyes, look your last.
4682 Arms, take your last embrace. And, lips, O, you
4683 The doors of breath, seal with a righteous kiss
4684 A dateless bargain to engrossing death.
4685 [Kissing Juliet.]
4686 Come, bitter conduct, come, unsavory guide!
4687 Thou desperate pilot, now at once run on
4688 The dashing rocks thy seasick weary bark!
4689 Here's to my love. [Drinking.] O true apothecary,
4690 Thy drugs are quick. Thus with a kiss I die.
4691 [He dies.]
4692 
4693 [Enter Friar Lawrence with lantern, crow, and spade.]
4694 
4695 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4696 Saint Francis be my speed! How oft tonight
4697 Have my old feet stumbled at graves!--Who's there?
4698 
4699 BALTHASAR
4700 Here's one, a friend, and one that knows you well.
4701 
4702 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4703 Bliss be upon you. Tell me, good my friend,
4704 What torch is yond that vainly lends his light
4705 To grubs and eyeless skulls? As I discern,
4706 It burneth in the Capels' monument.
4707 
4708 BALTHASAR
4709 It doth so, holy sir, and there's my master,
4710 One that you love.
4711 
4712 FRIAR LAWRENCE  Who is it?
4713 
4714 BALTHASAR  Romeo.
4715 
4716 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4717 How long hath he been there?
4718 
4719 BALTHASAR  Full half an hour.
4720 
4721 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4722 Go with me to the vault.
4723 
4724 BALTHASAR  I dare not, sir.
4725 My master knows not but I am gone hence,
4726 And fearfully did menace me with death
4727 If I did stay to look on his intents.
4728 
4729 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4730 Stay, then. I'll go alone. Fear comes upon me.
4731 O, much I fear some ill unthrifty thing.
4732 
4733 BALTHASAR
4734 As I did sleep under this yew tree here,
4735 I dreamt my master and another fought,
4736 And that my master slew him.
4737 
4738 FRIAR LAWRENCE, [moving toward the tomb]
4739 Romeo!--
4740 Alack, alack, what blood is this which stains
4741 The stony entrance of this sepulcher?
4742 What mean these masterless and gory swords
4743 To lie discolored by this place of peace?
4744 Romeo! O, pale! Who else? What, Paris too?
4745 And steeped in blood? Ah, what an unkind hour
4746 Is guilty of this lamentable chance!
4747 The lady stirs.
4748 
4749 JULIET
4750 O comfortable friar, where is my lord?
4751 I do remember well where I should be,
4752 And there I am. Where is my Romeo?
4753 
4754 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4755 I hear some noise.--Lady, come from that nest
4756 Of death, contagion, and unnatural sleep.
4757 A greater power than we can contradict
4758 Hath thwarted our intents. Come, come away.
4759 Thy husband in thy bosom there lies dead,
4760 And Paris, too. Come, I'll dispose of thee
4761 Among a sisterhood of holy nuns.
4762 Stay not to question, for the watch is coming.
4763 Come, go, good Juliet. I dare no longer stay.
4764 
4765 JULIET
4766 Go, get thee hence, for I will not away.
4767 [He exits.]
4768 What's here? A cup closed in my true love's hand?
4769 Poison, I see, hath been his timeless end.--
4770 O churl, drunk all, and left no friendly drop
4771 To help me after! I will kiss thy lips.
4772 Haply some poison yet doth hang on them,
4773 To make me die with a restorative.	[She kisses him.]
4774 Thy lips are warm!
4775 
4776 [Enter Paris's Page and Watch.]
4777 
4778 
4779 FIRST WATCH  Lead, boy. Which way?
4780 
4781 JULIET
4782 Yea, noise? Then I'll be brief. O, happy dagger,
4783 This is thy sheath. There rust, and let me die.
4784 [She takes Romeo's dagger, stabs herself, and dies.]
4785 
4786 PAGE
4787 This is the place, there where the torch doth burn.
4788 
4789 FIRST WATCH
4790 The ground is bloody.--Search about the
4791 churchyard.
4792 Go, some of you; whoe'er you find, attach.
4793 [Some watchmen exit.]
4794 Pitiful sight! Here lies the County slain,
4795 And Juliet bleeding, warm, and newly dead,
4796 Who here hath lain this two days buried.--
4797 Go, tell the Prince. Run to the Capulets.
4798 Raise up the Montagues. Some others search.
4799 [Others exit.]
4800 We see the ground whereon these woes do lie,
4801 But the true ground of all these piteous woes
4802 We cannot without circumstance descry.
4803 
4804 [Enter Watchmen with Romeo's man Balthasar.]
4805 
4806 
4807 SECOND WATCH
4808 Here's Romeo's man. We found him in the
4809 churchyard.
4810 
4811 FIRST WATCH
4812 Hold him in safety till the Prince come hither.
4813 
4814 [Enter Friar Lawrence and another Watchman.]
4815 
4816 
4817 THIRD WATCH
4818 Here is a friar that trembles, sighs, and weeps.
4819 We took this mattock and this spade from him
4820 As he was coming from this churchyard's side.
4821 
4822 FIRST WATCH
4823 A great suspicion. Stay the Friar too.
4824 
4825 [Enter the Prince with Attendants.]
4826 
4827 
4828 PRINCE
4829 What misadventure is so early up
4830 That calls our person from our morning rest?
4831 
4832 [Enter Capulet and Lady Capulet.]
4833 
4834 
4835 CAPULET
4836 What should it be that is so shrieked abroad?
4837 
4838 LADY CAPULET
4839 O, the people in the street cry "Romeo,"
4840 Some "Juliet," and some "Paris," and all run
4841 With open outcry toward our monument.
4842 
4843 PRINCE
4844 What fear is this which startles in our ears?
4845 
4846 FIRST WATCH
4847 Sovereign, here lies the County Paris slain,
4848 And Romeo dead, and Juliet, dead before,
4849 Warm and new killed.
4850 
4851 PRINCE
4852 Search, seek, and know how this foul murder
4853 comes.
4854 
4855 FIRST WATCH
4856 Here is a friar, and slaughtered Romeo's man,
4857 With instruments upon them fit to open
4858 These dead men's tombs.
4859 
4860 CAPULET
4861 O heavens! O wife, look how our daughter bleeds!
4862 This dagger hath mista'en, for, lo, his house
4863 Is empty on the back of Montague,
4864 And it mis-sheathed in my daughter's bosom.
4865 
4866 LADY CAPULET
4867 O me, this sight of death is as a bell
4868 That warns my old age to a sepulcher.
4869 
4870 [Enter Montague.]
4871 
4872 
4873 PRINCE
4874 Come, Montague, for thou art early up
4875 To see thy son and heir now early down.
4876 
4877 MONTAGUE
4878 Alas, my liege, my wife is dead tonight.
4879 Grief of my son's exile hath stopped her breath.
4880 What further woe conspires against mine age?
4881 
4882 PRINCE  Look, and thou shalt see.
4883 
4884 MONTAGUE, [seeing Romeo dead]
4885 O thou untaught! What manners is in this,
4886 To press before thy father to a grave?
4887 
4888 PRINCE
4889 Seal up the mouth of outrage for awhile,
4890 Till we can clear these ambiguities
4891 And know their spring, their head, their true
4892 descent,
4893 And then will I be general of your woes
4894 And lead you even to death. Meantime forbear,
4895 And let mischance be slave to patience.--
4896 Bring forth the parties of suspicion.
4897 
4898 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4899 I am the greatest, able to do least,
4900 Yet most suspected, as the time and place
4901 Doth make against me, of this direful murder.
4902 And here I stand, both to impeach and purge
4903 Myself condemned and myself excused.
4904 
4905 PRINCE
4906 Then say at once what thou dost know in this.
4907 
4908 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4909 I will be brief, for my short date of breath
4910 Is not so long as is a tedious tale.
4911 Romeo, there dead, was husband to that Juliet,
4912 And she, there dead, that Romeo's faithful wife.
4913 I married them, and their stol'n marriage day
4914 Was Tybalt's doomsday, whose untimely death
4915 Banished the new-made bridegroom from this city,
4916 For whom, and not for Tybalt, Juliet pined.
4917 You, to remove that siege of grief from her,
4918 Betrothed and would have married her perforce
4919 To County Paris. Then comes she to me,
4920 And with wild looks bid me devise some mean
4921 To rid her from this second marriage,
4922 Or in my cell there would she kill herself.
4923 Then gave I her (so tutored by my art)
4924 A sleeping potion, which so took effect
4925 As I intended, for it wrought on her
4926 The form of death. Meantime I writ to Romeo
4927 That he should hither come as this dire night
4928 To help to take her from her borrowed grave,
4929 Being the time the potion's force should cease.
4930 But he which bore my letter, Friar John,
4931 Was stayed by accident, and yesternight
4932 Returned my letter back. Then all alone
4933 At the prefixed hour of her waking
4934 Came I to take her from her kindred's vault,
4935 Meaning to keep her closely at my cell
4936 Till I conveniently could send to Romeo.
4937 But when I came, some minute ere the time
4938 Of her awakening, here untimely lay
4939 The noble Paris and true Romeo dead.
4940 She wakes, and I entreated her come forth
4941 And bear this work of heaven with patience.
4942 But then a noise did scare me from the tomb,
4943 And she, too desperate, would not go with me
4944 But, as it seems, did violence on herself.
4945 All this I know, and to the marriage
4946 Her nurse is privy. And if aught in this
4947 Miscarried by my fault, let my old life
4948 Be sacrificed some hour before his time
4949 Unto the rigor of severest law.
4950 
4951 PRINCE
4952 We still have known thee for a holy man.--
4953 Where's Romeo's man? What can he say to this?
4954 
4955 BALTHASAR
4956 I brought my master news of Juliet's death,
4957 And then in post he came from Mantua
4958 To this same place, to this same monument.
4959 This letter he early bid me give his father
4960 And threatened me with death, going in the vault,
4961 If I departed not and left him there.
4962 
4963 PRINCE
4964 Give me the letter. I will look on it.--
4965 [He takes Romeo's letter.]
4966 Where is the County's page, that raised the
4967 watch?--
4968 Sirrah, what made your master in this place?
4969 
4970 PAGE
4971 He came with flowers to strew his lady's grave
4972 And bid me stand aloof, and so I did.
4973 Anon comes one with light to ope the tomb,
4974 And by and by my master drew on him,
4975 And then I ran away to call the watch.
4976 
4977 PRINCE
4978 This letter doth make good the Friar's words,
4979 Their course of love, the tidings of her death;
4980 And here he writes that he did buy a poison
4981 Of a poor 'pothecary, and therewithal
4982 Came to this vault to die and lie with Juliet.
4983 Where be these enemies?--Capulet, Montague,
4984 See what a scourge is laid upon your hate,
4985 That heaven finds means to kill your joys with love,
4986 And I, for winking at your discords too,
4987 Have lost a brace of kinsmen. All are punished.
4988 
4989 CAPULET
4990 O brother Montague, give me thy hand.
4991 This is my daughter's jointure, for no more
4992 Can I demand.
4993 
4994 MONTAGUE  But I can give thee more,
4995 For I will ray her statue in pure gold,
4996 That whiles Verona by that name is known,
4997 There shall no figure at such rate be set
4998 As that of true and faithful Juliet.
4999 
5000 CAPULET
5001 As rich shall Romeo's by his lady's lie,
5002 Poor sacrifices of our enmity.
5003 
5004 PRINCE
5005 A glooming peace this morning with it brings.
5006 The sun for sorrow will not show his head.
5007 Go hence to have more talk of these sad things.
5008 Some shall be pardoned, and some punished.
5009 For never was a story of more woe
5010 Than this of Juliet and her Romeo.
5011 [All exit.]

43   THE PROLOGUE
44   ============
45   [Enter Chorus.]
46   
47   
48   Two households, both alike in dignity
49   (In fair Verona, where we lay our scene),
50   From ancient grudge break to new mutiny,
51   Where civil blood makes civil hands unclean.
52   From forth the fatal loins of these two foes
53   A pair of star-crossed lovers take their life;
54   Whose misadventured piteous overthrows
55   Doth with their death bury their parents' strife.
56   The fearful passage of their death-marked love
57   And the continuance of their parents' rage,
58   Which, but their children's end, naught could remove,
59   Is now the two hours' traffic of our stage;
60   The which, if you with patient ears attend,
61   What here shall miss, our toil shall strive to mend.
62   [Chorus exits.]
63   
64   
65   ACT 1
66   =====
67   
68   Scene 1
69   =======
70   [Enter Sampson and Gregory, with swords and bucklers,
71   of the house of Capulet.]
72   
73   
74   SAMPSON  Gregory, on my word we'll not carry coals.
75   
76   GREGORY  No, for then we should be colliers.
77   
78   SAMPSON  I mean, an we be in choler, we'll draw.
79   
80   GREGORY  Ay, while you live, draw your neck out of
81   collar.
82   
83   SAMPSON  I strike quickly, being moved.
84   
85   GREGORY  But thou art not quickly moved to strike.
86   
87   SAMPSON  A dog of the house of Montague moves me.
88   
89   GREGORY  To move is to stir, and to be valiant is to
90   stand. Therefore if thou art moved thou runn'st
91   away.
92   
93   SAMPSON  A dog of that house shall move me to stand. I
94   will take the wall of any man or maid of Montague's.
95   
96   GREGORY  That shows thee a weak slave, for the weakest
97   goes to the wall.
98   
99   SAMPSON  'Tis true, and therefore women, being the
100  weaker vessels, are ever thrust to the wall. Therefore
101  I will push Montague's men from the wall and
102  thrust his maids to the wall.
103  
104  GREGORY  The quarrel is between our masters and us
105  their men.
106  
107  SAMPSON  'Tis all one. I will show myself a tyrant.
108  When I have fought with the men, I will be civil
109  with the maids; I will cut off their heads.
110  
111  GREGORY  The heads of the maids?
112  
113  SAMPSON  Ay, the heads of the maids, or their maidenheads.
114  Take it in what sense thou wilt.
115  
116  GREGORY  They must take it in sense that feel it.
117  
118  SAMPSON  Me they shall feel while I am able to stand,
119  and 'tis known I am a pretty piece of flesh.
120  
121  GREGORY  'Tis well thou art not fish; if thou hadst, thou
122  hadst been poor-john. Draw thy tool. Here comes
123  of the house of Montagues.
124  
125  [Enter Abram with another Servingman.]
126  
127  
128  SAMPSON  My naked weapon is out. Quarrel, I will back
129  thee.
130  
131  GREGORY  How? Turn thy back and run?
132  
133  SAMPSON  Fear me not.
134  
135  GREGORY  No, marry. I fear thee!
136  
137  SAMPSON  Let us take the law of our sides; let them
138  begin.
139  
140  GREGORY  I will frown as I pass by, and let them take it
141  as they list.
142  
143  SAMPSON  Nay, as they dare. I will bite my thumb at
144  them, which is disgrace to them if they bear it.
145  [He bites his thumb.]
146  
147  ABRAM  Do you bite your thumb at us, sir?
148  
149  SAMPSON  I do bite my thumb, sir.
150  
151  ABRAM  Do you bite your thumb at us, sir?
152  
153  SAMPSON, [aside to Gregory]  Is the law of our side if I
154  say "Ay"?
155  
156  GREGORY, [aside to Sampson]  No.
157  
158  SAMPSON  No, sir, I do not bite my thumb at you, sir,
159  but I bite my thumb, sir.
160  
161  GREGORY  Do you quarrel, sir?
162  
163  ABRAM  Quarrel, sir? No, sir.
164  
165  SAMPSON  But if you do, sir, I am for you. I serve as
166  good a man as you.
167  
168  ABRAM  No better.
169  
170  SAMPSON  Well, sir.
171  
172  [Enter Benvolio.]
173  
174  
175  GREGORY, [aside to Sampson]  Say "better"; here comes
176  one of my master's kinsmen.
177  
178  SAMPSON  Yes, better, sir.
179  
180  ABRAM  You lie.
181  
182  SAMPSON  Draw if you be men.--Gregory, remember
183  thy washing blow.	[They fight.]
184  
185  BENVOLIO  Part, fools!	[Drawing his sword.]
186  Put up your swords. You know not what you do.
187  
188  [Enter Tybalt, drawing his sword.]
189  
190  
191  TYBALT
192  What, art thou drawn among these heartless hinds?
193  Turn thee, Benvolio; look upon thy death.
194  
195  BENVOLIO
196  I do but keep the peace. Put up thy sword,
197  Or manage it to part these men with me.
198  
199  TYBALT
200  What, drawn and talk of peace? I hate the word
201  As I hate hell, all Montagues, and thee.
202  Have at thee, coward!	[They fight.]
203  
204  [Enter three or four Citizens with clubs or partisans.]
205  
206  
207  CITIZENS
208  Clubs, bills, and partisans! Strike! Beat them down!
209  Down with the Capulets! Down with the Montagues!
210  
211  [Enter old Capulet in his gown, and his Wife.]
212  
213  
214  CAPULET
215  What noise is this? Give me my long sword, ho!
216  
217  LADY CAPULET
218  A crutch, a crutch! Why call you for a
219  sword?
220  
221  [Enter old Montague and his Wife.]
222  
223  CAPULET
224  My sword, I say. Old Montague is come
225  And flourishes his blade in spite of me.
226  
227  MONTAGUE
228  Thou villain Capulet!--Hold me not; let me go.
229  
230  LADY MONTAGUE
231  Thou shalt not stir one foot to seek a foe.
232  
233  [Enter Prince Escalus with his train.]
234  
235  
236  PRINCE
237  Rebellious subjects, enemies to peace,
238  Profaners of this neighbor-stained steel--
239  Will they not hear?--What ho! You men, you beasts,
240  That quench the fire of your pernicious rage
241  With purple fountains issuing from your veins:
242  On pain of torture, from those bloody hands
243  Throw your mistempered weapons to the ground,
244  And hear the sentence of your moved prince.
245  Three civil brawls bred of an airy word
246  By thee, old Capulet, and Montague,
247  Have thrice disturbed the quiet of our streets
248  And made Verona's ancient citizens
249  Cast by their grave-beseeming ornaments
250  To wield old partisans in hands as old,
251  Cankered with peace, to part your cankered hate.
252  If ever you disturb our streets again,
253  Your lives shall pay the forfeit of the peace.
254  For this time all the rest depart away.
255  You, Capulet, shall go along with me,
256  And, Montague, come you this afternoon
257  To know our farther pleasure in this case,
258  To old Free-town, our common judgment-place.
259  Once more, on pain of death, all men depart.
260  [All but Montague, Lady Montague,
261  and Benvolio exit.]
262  
263  MONTAGUE, [to Benvolio]
264  Who set this ancient quarrel new abroach?
265  Speak, nephew, were you by when it began?
266  
267  BENVOLIO
268  Here were the servants of your adversary,
269  And yours, close fighting ere I did approach.
270  I drew to part them. In the instant came
271  The fiery Tybalt with his sword prepared,
272  Which, as he breathed defiance to my ears,
273  He swung about his head and cut the winds,
274  Who, nothing hurt withal, hissed him in scorn.
275  While we were interchanging thrusts and blows
276  Came more and more and fought on part and part,
277  Till the Prince came, who parted either part.
278  
279  LADY MONTAGUE
280  O, where is Romeo? Saw you him today?
281  Right glad I am he was not at this fray.
282  
283  BENVOLIO
284  Madam, an hour before the worshiped sun
285  Peered forth the golden window of the east,
286  A troubled mind drove me to walk abroad,
287  Where underneath the grove of sycamore
288  That westward rooteth from this city side,
289  So early walking did I see your son.
290  Towards him I made, but he was 'ware of me
291  And stole into the covert of the wood.
292  I, measuring his affections by my own
293  (Which then most sought where most might not be
294  found,
295  Being one too many by my weary self),
296  Pursued my humor, not pursuing his,
297  And gladly shunned who gladly fled from me.
298  
299  MONTAGUE
300  Many a morning hath he there been seen,
301  With tears augmenting the fresh morning's dew,
302  Adding to clouds more clouds with his deep sighs.
303  But all so soon as the all-cheering sun
304  Should in the farthest east begin to draw
305  The shady curtains from Aurora's bed,
306  Away from light steals home my heavy son
307  And private in his chamber pens himself,
308  Shuts up his windows, locks fair daylight out,
309  And makes himself an artificial night.
310  Black and portentous must this humor prove,
311  Unless good counsel may the cause remove.
312  
313  BENVOLIO
314  My noble uncle, do you know the cause?
315  
316  MONTAGUE
317  I neither know it nor can learn of him.
318  
319  BENVOLIO
320  Have you importuned him by any means?
321  
322  MONTAGUE
323  Both by myself and many other friends.
324  But he, his own affections' counselor,
325  Is to himself--I will not say how true,
326  But to himself so secret and so close,
327  So far from sounding and discovery,
328  As is the bud bit with an envious worm
329  Ere he can spread his sweet leaves to the air
330  Or dedicate his beauty to the same.
331  Could we but learn from whence his sorrows grow,
332  We would as willingly give cure as know.
333  
334  [Enter Romeo.]
335  
336  
337  BENVOLIO
338  See where he comes. So please you, step aside.
339  I'll know his grievance or be much denied.
340  
341  MONTAGUE
342  I would thou wert so happy by thy stay
343  To hear true shrift.--Come, madam, let's away.
344  [Montague and Lady Montague exit.]
345  
346  BENVOLIO
347  Good morrow, cousin.
348  
349  ROMEO  Is the day so young?
350  
351  BENVOLIO
352  But new struck nine.
353  
354  ROMEO  Ay me, sad hours seem long.
355  Was that my father that went hence so fast?
356  
357  BENVOLIO
358  It was. What sadness lengthens Romeo's hours?
359  
360  ROMEO
361  Not having that which, having, makes them short.
362  
363  BENVOLIO  In love?
364  
365  ROMEO  Out--
366  
367  BENVOLIO  Of love?
368  
369  ROMEO
370  Out of her favor where I am in love.
371  
372  BENVOLIO
373  Alas that love, so gentle in his view,
374  Should be so tyrannous and rough in proof!
375  
376  ROMEO
377  Alas that love, whose view is muffled still,
378  Should without eyes see pathways to his will!
379  Where shall we dine?--O me! What fray was here?
380  Yet tell me not, for I have heard it all.
381  Here's much to do with hate, but more with love.
382  Why then, O brawling love, O loving hate,
383  O anything of nothing first create!
384  O heavy lightness, serious vanity,
385  Misshapen chaos of well-seeming forms,
386  Feather of lead, bright smoke, cold fire, sick health,
387  Still-waking sleep that is not what it is!
388  This love feel I, that feel no love in this.
389  Dost thou not laugh?
390  
391  BENVOLIO  No, coz, I rather weep.
392  
393  ROMEO
394  Good heart, at what?
395  
396  BENVOLIO  At thy good heart's oppression.
397  
398  ROMEO  Why, such is love's transgression.
399  Griefs of mine own lie heavy in my breast,
400  Which thou wilt propagate to have it pressed
401  With more of thine. This love that thou hast shown
402  Doth add more grief to too much of mine own.
403  Love is a smoke made with the fume of sighs;
404  Being purged, a fire sparkling in lovers' eyes;
405  Being vexed, a sea nourished with loving tears.
406  What is it else? A madness most discreet,
407  A choking gall, and a preserving sweet.
408  Farewell, my coz.
409  
410  BENVOLIO  Soft, I will go along.
411  An if you leave me so, you do me wrong.
412  
413  ROMEO
414  Tut, I have lost myself. I am not here.
415  This is not Romeo. He's some other where.
416  
417  BENVOLIO
418  Tell me in sadness, who is that you love?
419  
420  ROMEO  What, shall I groan and tell thee?
421  
422  BENVOLIO
423  Groan? Why, no. But sadly tell me who.
424  
425  ROMEO
426  A sick man in sadness makes his will--
427  A word ill urged to one that is so ill.
428  In sadness, cousin, I do love a woman.
429  
430  BENVOLIO
431  I aimed so near when I supposed you loved.
432  
433  ROMEO
434  A right good markman! And she's fair I love.
435  
436  BENVOLIO
437  A right fair mark, fair coz, is soonest hit.
438  
439  ROMEO
440  Well in that hit you miss. She'll not be hit
441  With Cupid's arrow. She hath Dian's wit,
442  And, in strong proof of chastity well armed,
443  From love's weak childish bow she lives uncharmed.
444  She will not stay the siege of loving terms,
445  Nor bide th' encounter of assailing eyes,
446  Nor ope her lap to saint-seducing gold.
447  O, she is rich in beauty, only poor
448  That, when she dies, with beauty dies her store.
449  
450  BENVOLIO
451  Then she hath sworn that she will still live chaste?
452  
453  ROMEO
454  She hath, and in that sparing makes huge waste;
455  For beauty, starved with her severity,
456  Cuts beauty off from all posterity.
457  She is too fair, too wise, wisely too fair,
458  To merit bliss by making me despair.
459  She hath forsworn to love, and in that vow
460  Do I live dead, that live to tell it now.
461  
462  BENVOLIO
463  Be ruled by me. Forget to think of her.
464  
465  ROMEO
466  O, teach me how I should forget to think!
467  
468  BENVOLIO
469  By giving liberty unto thine eyes.
470  Examine other beauties.
471  
472  ROMEO  'Tis the way
473  To call hers, exquisite, in question more.
474  These happy masks that kiss fair ladies' brows,
475  Being black, puts us in mind they hide the fair.
476  He that is strucken blind cannot forget
477  The precious treasure of his eyesight lost.
478  Show me a mistress that is passing fair;
479  What doth her beauty serve but as a note
480  Where I may read who passed that passing fair?
481  Farewell. Thou canst not teach me to forget.
482  
483  BENVOLIO
484  I'll pay that doctrine or else die in debt.
485  [They exit.]
486  
487  Scene 2
488  =======
489  [Enter Capulet, County Paris, and a Servingman.]
490  
491  
492  CAPULET
493  But Montague is bound as well as I,
494  In penalty alike, and 'tis not hard, I think,
495  For men so old as we to keep the peace.
496  
497  PARIS
498  Of honorable reckoning are you both,
499  And pity 'tis you lived at odds so long.
500  But now, my lord, what say you to my suit?
501  
502  CAPULET
503  But saying o'er what I have said before.
504  My child is yet a stranger in the world.
505  She hath not seen the change of fourteen years.
506  Let two more summers wither in their pride
507  Ere we may think her ripe to be a bride.
508  
509  PARIS
510  Younger than she are happy mothers made.
511  
512  CAPULET
513  And too soon marred are those so early made.
514  Earth hath swallowed all my hopes but she;
515  She's the hopeful lady of my earth.
516  But woo her, gentle Paris, get her heart;
517  My will to her consent is but a part.
518  And, she agreed, within her scope of choice
519  Lies my consent and fair according voice.
520  This night I hold an old accustomed feast,
521  Whereto I have invited many a guest
522  Such as I love; and you among the store,
523  One more, most welcome, makes my number more.
524  At my poor house look to behold this night
525  Earth-treading stars that make dark heaven light.
526  Such comfort as do lusty young men feel
527  When well-appareled April on the heel
528  Of limping winter treads, even such delight
529  Among fresh fennel buds shall you this night
530  Inherit at my house. Hear all, all see,
531  And like her most whose merit most shall be;
532  Which, on more view of many, mine, being one,
533  May stand in number, though in reck'ning none.
534  Come go with me.	[To Servingman, giving him a list.]
535  Go, sirrah, trudge about
536  Through fair Verona, find those persons out
537  Whose names are written there, and to them say
538  My house and welcome on their pleasure stay.
539  [Capulet and Paris exit.]
540  
541  SERVINGMAN  Find them out whose names are written
542  here! It is written that the shoemaker should
543  meddle with his yard and the tailor with his last, the
544  fisher with his pencil and the painter with his nets.
545  But I am sent to find those persons whose names
546  are here writ, and can never find what names the
547  writing person hath here writ. I must to the learned.
548  In good time!
549  
550  [Enter Benvolio and Romeo.]
551  
552  
553  BENVOLIO, [to Romeo]
554  Tut, man, one fire burns out another's burning;
555  One pain is lessened by another's anguish.
556  Turn giddy, and be helped by backward turning.
557  One desperate grief cures with another's languish.
558  Take thou some new infection to thy eye,
559  And the rank poison of the old will die.
560  
561  ROMEO
562  Your plantain leaf is excellent for that.
563  
564  BENVOLIO
565  For what, I pray thee?
566  
567  ROMEO  For your broken shin.
568  
569  BENVOLIO  Why Romeo, art thou mad?
570  
571  ROMEO
572  Not mad, but bound more than a madman is,
573  Shut up in prison, kept without my food,
574  Whipped and tormented, and--good e'en, good
575  fellow.
576  
577  SERVINGMAN  God gi' good e'en. I pray, sir, can you
578  read?
579  
580  ROMEO
581  Ay, mine own fortune in my misery.
582  
583  SERVINGMAN  Perhaps you have learned it without
584  book. But I pray, can you read anything you see?
585  
586  ROMEO
587  Ay, if I know the letters and the language.
588  
589  SERVINGMAN  You say honestly. Rest you merry.
590  
591  ROMEO  Stay, fellow. I can read.	[(He reads the letter.)]
592  Signior Martino and his wife and daughters,
593  County Anselme and his beauteous sisters,
594  The lady widow of Vitruvio,
595  Signior Placentio and his lovely nieces,
596  Mercutio and his brother Valentine,
597  Mine Uncle Capulet, his wife and daughters,
598  My fair niece Rosaline and Livia,
599  Signior Valentio and his cousin Tybalt,
600  Lucio and the lively Helena.
601  A fair assembly. Whither should they come?
602  
603  SERVINGMAN  Up.
604  
605  ROMEO  Whither? To supper?
606  
607  SERVINGMAN  To our house.
608  
609  ROMEO  Whose house?
610  
611  SERVINGMAN  My master's.
612  
613  ROMEO
614  Indeed I should have asked thee that before.
615  
616  SERVINGMAN  Now I'll tell you without asking. My
617  master is the great rich Capulet, and, if you be not
618  of the house of Montagues, I pray come and crush a
619  cup of wine. Rest you merry.	[He exits.]
620  
621  BENVOLIO
622  At this same ancient feast of Capulet's
623  Sups the fair Rosaline whom thou so loves,
624  With all the admired beauties of Verona.
625  Go thither, and with unattainted eye
626  Compare her face with some that I shall show,
627  And I will make thee think thy swan a crow.
628  
629  ROMEO
630  When the devout religion of mine eye
631  Maintains such falsehood, then turn tears to fire;
632  And these who, often drowned, could never die,
633  Transparent heretics, be burnt for liars.
634  One fairer than my love? The all-seeing sun
635  Ne'er saw her match since first the world begun.
636  
637  BENVOLIO
638  Tut, you saw her fair, none else being by,
639  Herself poised with herself in either eye;
640  But in that crystal scales let there be weighed
641  Your lady's love against some other maid
642  That I will show you shining at this feast,
643  And she shall scant show well that now seems best.
644  
645  ROMEO
646  I'll go along, no such sight to be shown,
647  But to rejoice in splendor of mine own.
648  [They exit.]
649  
650  Scene 3
651  =======
652  [Enter Lady Capulet and Nurse.]
653  
654  
655  LADY CAPULET
656  Nurse, where's my daughter? Call her forth to me.
657  
658  NURSE
659  Now, by my maidenhead at twelve year old,
660  I bade her come.--What, lamb! What, ladybird!
661  God forbid. Where's this girl? What, Juliet!
662  
663  [Enter Juliet.]
664  
665  
666  JULIET  How now, who calls?
667  
668  NURSE  Your mother.
669  
670  JULIET
671  Madam, I am here. What is your will?
672  
673  LADY CAPULET
674  This is the matter.--Nurse, give leave awhile.
675  We must talk in secret.--Nurse, come back again.
676  I have remembered me, thou 's hear our counsel.
677  Thou knowest my daughter's of a pretty age.
678  
679  NURSE
680  Faith, I can tell her age unto an hour.
681  
682  LADY CAPULET  She's not fourteen.
683  
684  NURSE  I'll lay fourteen of my teeth (and yet, to my teen
685  be it spoken, I have but four) she's not fourteen.
686  How long is it now to Lammastide?
687  
688  LADY CAPULET  A fortnight and odd days.
689  
690  NURSE
691  Even or odd, of all days in the year,
692  Come Lammas Eve at night shall she be fourteen.
693  Susan and she (God rest all Christian souls!)
694  Were of an age. Well, Susan is with God;
695  She was too good for me. But, as I said,
696  On Lammas Eve at night shall she be fourteen.
697  That shall she. Marry, I remember it well.
698  'Tis since the earthquake now eleven years,
699  And she was weaned (I never shall forget it)
700  Of all the days of the year, upon that day.
701  For I had then laid wormwood to my dug,
702  Sitting in the sun under the dovehouse wall.
703  My lord and you were then at Mantua.
704  Nay, I do bear a brain. But, as I said,
705  When it did taste the wormwood on the nipple
706  Of my dug and felt it bitter, pretty fool,
707  To see it tetchy and fall out with the dug.
708  "Shake," quoth the dovehouse. 'Twas no need, I
709  trow,
710  To bid me trudge.
711  And since that time it is eleven years.
712  For then she could stand high-lone. Nay, by th'
713  rood,
714  She could have run and waddled all about,
715  For even the day before, she broke her brow,
716  And then my husband (God be with his soul,
717  He was a merry man) took up the child.
718  "Yea," quoth he, "Dost thou fall upon thy face?
719  Thou wilt fall backward when thou hast more wit,
720  Wilt thou not, Jule?" And, by my holidam,
721  The pretty wretch left crying and said "Ay."
722  To see now how a jest shall come about!
723  I warrant, an I should live a thousand years,
724  I never should forget it. "Wilt thou not, Jule?"
725  quoth he.
726  And, pretty fool, it stinted and said "Ay."
727  
728  LADY CAPULET
729  Enough of this. I pray thee, hold thy peace.
730  
731  NURSE
732  Yes, madam, yet I cannot choose but laugh
733  To think it should leave crying and say "Ay."
734  And yet, I warrant, it had upon its brow
735  A bump as big as a young cock'rel's stone,
736  A perilous knock, and it cried bitterly.
737  "Yea," quoth my husband. "Fall'st upon thy face?
738  Thou wilt fall backward when thou comest to age,
739  Wilt thou not, Jule?" It stinted and said "Ay."
740  
741  JULIET
742  And stint thou, too, I pray thee, nurse, say I.
743  
744  NURSE
745  Peace. I have done. God mark thee to his grace,
746  Thou wast the prettiest babe that e'er I nursed.
747  An I might live to see thee married once,
748  I have my wish.
749  
750  LADY CAPULET
751  Marry, that "marry" is the very theme
752  I came to talk of.--Tell me, daughter Juliet,
753  How stands your disposition to be married?
754  
755  JULIET
756  It is an honor that I dream not of.
757  
758  NURSE
759  An honor? Were not I thine only nurse,
760  I would say thou hadst sucked wisdom from thy
761  teat.
762  
763  LADY CAPULET
764  Well, think of marriage now. Younger than you
765  Here in Verona, ladies of esteem,
766  Are made already mothers. By my count
767  I was your mother much upon these years
768  That you are now a maid. Thus, then, in brief:
769  The valiant Paris seeks you for his love.
770  
771  NURSE
772  A man, young lady--lady, such a man
773  As all the world--why, he's a man of wax.
774  
775  LADY CAPULET
776  Verona's summer hath not such a flower.
777  
778  NURSE
779  Nay, he's a flower, in faith, a very flower.
780  
781  LADY CAPULET
782  What say you? Can you love the gentleman?
783  This night you shall behold him at our feast.
784  Read o'er the volume of young Paris' face,
785  And find delight writ there with beauty's pen.
786  Examine every married lineament
787  And see how one another lends content,
788  And what obscured in this fair volume lies
789  Find written in the margent of his eyes.
790  This precious book of love, this unbound lover,
791  To beautify him only lacks a cover.
792  The fish lives in the sea, and 'tis much pride
793  For fair without the fair within to hide.
794  That book in many's eyes doth share the glory
795  That in gold clasps locks in the golden story.
796  So shall you share all that he doth possess
797  By having him, making yourself no less.
798  
799  NURSE
800  No less? Nay, bigger. Women grow by men.
801  
802  LADY CAPULET
803  Speak briefly. Can you like of Paris' love?
804  
805  JULIET
806  I'll look to like, if looking liking move.
807  But no more deep will I endart mine eye
808  Than your consent gives strength to make it fly.
809  
810  [Enter Servingman.]
811  
812  
813  SERVINGMAN  Madam, the guests are come, supper
814  served up, you called, my young lady asked for, the
815  Nurse cursed in the pantry, and everything in
816  extremity. I must hence to wait. I beseech you,
817  follow straight.
818  
819  LADY CAPULET
820  We follow thee.	[Servingman exits.]
821  Juliet, the County stays.
822  
823  NURSE
824  Go, girl, seek happy nights to happy days.
825  [They exit.]
826  
827  Scene 4
828  =======
829  [Enter Romeo, Mercutio, Benvolio, with five or six other
830  Maskers, Torchbearers, and a Boy with a drum.]
831  
832  
833  ROMEO
834  What, shall this speech be spoke for our excuse?
835  Or shall we on without apology?
836  
837  BENVOLIO
838  The date is out of such prolixity.
839  We'll have no Cupid hoodwinked with a scarf,
840  Bearing a Tartar's painted bow of lath,
841  Scaring the ladies like a crowkeeper,
842  Nor no without-book prologue, faintly spoke
843  After the prompter, for our entrance.
844  But let them measure us by what they will.
845  We'll measure them a measure and be gone.
846  
847  ROMEO
848  Give me a torch. I am not for this ambling.
849  Being but heavy I will bear the light.
850  
851  MERCUTIO
852  Nay, gentle Romeo, we must have you dance.
853  
854  ROMEO
855  Not I, believe me. You have dancing shoes
856  With nimble soles. I have a soul of lead
857  So stakes me to the ground I cannot move.
858  
859  MERCUTIO
860  You are a lover. Borrow Cupid's wings
861  And soar with them above a common bound.
862  
863  ROMEO
864  I am too sore enpierced with his shaft
865  To soar with his light feathers, and so bound
866  I cannot bound a pitch above dull woe.
867  Under love's heavy burden do I sink.
868  
869  MERCUTIO
870  And to sink in it should you burden love--
871  Too great oppression for a tender thing.
872  
873  ROMEO
874  Is love a tender thing? It is too rough,
875  Too rude, too boist'rous, and it pricks like thorn.
876  
877  MERCUTIO
878  If love be rough with you, be rough with love.
879  Prick love for pricking, and you beat love down.--
880  Give me a case to put my visage in.--
881  A visor for a visor. What care I
882  What curious eye doth cote deformities?
883  Here are the beetle brows shall blush for me.
884  
885  BENVOLIO
886  Come, knock and enter, and no sooner in
887  But every man betake him to his legs.
888  
889  ROMEO
890  A torch for me. Let wantons light of heart
891  Tickle the senseless rushes with their heels,
892  For I am proverbed with a grandsire phrase:
893  I'll be a candle holder and look on;
894  The game was ne'er so fair, and I am done.
895  
896  MERCUTIO
897  Tut, dun's the mouse, the constable's own word.
898  If thou art dun, we'll draw thee from the mire--
899  Or, save your reverence, love--wherein thou
900  stickest
901  Up to the ears. Come, we burn daylight, ho!
902  
903  ROMEO
904  Nay, that's not so.
905  
906  MERCUTIO  I mean, sir, in delay
907  We waste our lights; in vain, light lights by day.
908  Take our good meaning, for our judgment sits
909  Five times in that ere once in our five wits.
910  
911  ROMEO
912  And we mean well in going to this masque,
913  But 'tis no wit to go.
914  
915  MERCUTIO  Why, may one ask?
916  
917  ROMEO
918  I dreamt a dream tonight.
919  
920  MERCUTIO  And so did I.
921  
922  ROMEO
923  Well, what was yours?
924  
925  MERCUTIO  That dreamers often lie.
926  
927  ROMEO
928  In bed asleep while they do dream things true.
929  
930  MERCUTIO
931  O, then I see Queen Mab hath been with you.
932  She is the fairies' midwife, and she comes
933  In shape no bigger than an agate stone
934  On the forefinger of an alderman,
935  Drawn with a team of little atomi
936  Over men's noses as they lie asleep.
937  Her wagon spokes made of long spinners' legs,
938  The cover of the wings of grasshoppers,
939  Her traces of the smallest spider web,
940  Her collars of the moonshine's wat'ry beams,
941  Her whip of cricket's bone, the lash of film,
942  Her wagoner a small gray-coated gnat,
943  Not half so big as a round little worm
944  Pricked from the lazy finger of a maid.
945  Her chariot is an empty hazelnut,
946  Made by the joiner squirrel or old grub,
947  Time out o' mind the fairies' coachmakers.
948  And in this state she gallops night by night
949  Through lovers' brains, and then they dream of love;
950  On courtiers' knees, that dream on cur'sies straight;
951  O'er lawyers' fingers, who straight dream on fees;
952  O'er ladies' lips, who straight on kisses dream,
953  Which oft the angry Mab with blisters plagues
954  Because their breaths with sweetmeats tainted are.
955  Sometime she gallops o'er a courtier's nose,
956  And then dreams he of smelling out a suit.
957  And sometime comes she with a tithe-pig's tail,
958  Tickling a parson's nose as he lies asleep;
959  Then he dreams of another benefice.
960  Sometime she driveth o'er a soldier's neck,
961  And then dreams he of cutting foreign throats,
962  Of breaches, ambuscadoes, Spanish blades,
963  Of healths five fathom deep, and then anon
964  Drums in his ear, at which he starts and wakes
965  And, being thus frighted, swears a prayer or two
966  And sleeps again. This is that very Mab
967  That plats the manes of horses in the night
968  And bakes the elflocks in foul sluttish hairs,
969  Which once untangled much misfortune bodes.
970  This is the hag, when maids lie on their backs,
971  That presses them and learns them first to bear,
972  Making them women of good carriage.
973  This is she--
974  
975  ROMEO  Peace, peace, Mercutio, peace.
976  Thou talk'st of nothing.
977  
978  MERCUTIO  True, I talk of dreams,
979  Which are the children of an idle brain,
980  Begot of nothing but vain fantasy,
981  Which is as thin of substance as the air
982  And more inconstant than the wind, who woos
983  Even now the frozen bosom of the north
984  And, being angered, puffs away from thence,
985  Turning his side to the dew-dropping south.
986  
987  BENVOLIO
988  This wind you talk of blows us from ourselves.
989  Supper is done, and we shall come too late.
990  
991  ROMEO
992  I fear too early, for my mind misgives
993  Some consequence yet hanging in the stars
994  Shall bitterly begin his fearful date
995  With this night's revels, and expire the term
996  Of a despised life closed in my breast
997  By some vile forfeit of untimely death.
998  But he that hath the steerage of my course
999  Direct my sail. On, lusty gentlemen.
1000 
1001 BENVOLIO  Strike, drum.
1002 [They march about the stage
1003 and then withdraw to the side.]
1004 
1005 Scene 5
1006 =======
1007 [Servingmen come forth with napkins.]
1008 
1009 
1010 FIRST SERVINGMAN  Where's Potpan that he helps not
1011 to take away? He shift a trencher? He scrape a
1012 trencher?
1013 
1014 SECOND SERVINGMAN  When good manners shall lie
1015 all in one or two men's hands, and they unwashed
1016 too, 'tis a foul thing.
1017 
1018 FIRST SERVINGMAN  Away with the joint stools, remove
1019 the court cupboard, look to the plate.--
1020 Good thou, save me a piece of marchpane, and, as
1021 thou loves me, let the porter let in Susan Grindstone
1022 and Nell.--Anthony and Potpan!
1023 
1024 THIRD SERVINGMAN  Ay, boy, ready.
1025 
1026 FIRST SERVINGMAN  You are looked for and called for,
1027 asked for and sought for, in the great chamber.
1028 
1029 THIRD SERVINGMAN  We cannot be here and there too.
1030 Cheerly, boys! Be brisk awhile, and the longer liver
1031 take all.	[They move aside.]
1032 
1033 [Enter Capulet and his household, all the guests and
1034 gentlewomen to Romeo, Mercutio, Benvolio, and the
1035 other Maskers.]
1036 
1037 
1038 CAPULET
1039 Welcome, gentlemen. Ladies that have their toes
1040 Unplagued with corns will walk a bout with
1041 you.--
1042 Ah, my mistresses, which of you all
1043 Will now deny to dance? She that makes dainty,
1044 She, I'll swear, hath corns. Am I come near you
1045 now?--
1046 Welcome, gentlemen. I have seen the day
1047 That I have worn a visor and could tell
1048 A whispering tale in a fair lady's ear,
1049 Such as would please. 'Tis gone, 'tis gone, 'tis gone.
1050 You are welcome, gentlemen.--Come, musicians,
1051 play.	[Music plays and they dance.]
1052 A hall, a hall, give room!--And foot it, girls.--
1053 More light, you knaves, and turn the tables up,
1054 And quench the fire; the room is grown too hot.--
1055 Ah, sirrah, this unlooked-for sport comes well.--
1056 Nay, sit, nay, sit, good cousin Capulet,
1057 For you and I are past our dancing days.
1058 How long is 't now since last yourself and I
1059 Were in a mask?
1060 
1061 CAPULET'S COUSIN  By 'r Lady, thirty years.
1062 
1063 CAPULET
1064 What, man, 'tis not so much, 'tis not so much.
1065 'Tis since the nuptial of Lucentio,
1066 Come Pentecost as quickly as it will,
1067 Some five and twenty years, and then we masked.
1068 
1069 CAPULET'S COUSIN
1070 'Tis more, 'tis more. His son is elder, sir.
1071 His son is thirty.
1072 
1073 CAPULET  Will you tell me that?
1074 His son was but a ward two years ago.
1075 
1076 ROMEO, [to a Servingman]
1077 What lady's that which doth enrich the hand
1078 Of yonder knight?
1079 
1080 SERVINGMAN  I know not, sir.
1081 
1082 ROMEO
1083 O, she doth teach the torches to burn bright!
1084 It seems she hangs upon the cheek of night
1085 As a rich jewel in an Ethiop's ear--
1086 Beauty too rich for use, for Earth too dear.
1087 So shows a snowy dove trooping with crows
1088 As yonder lady o'er her fellows shows.
1089 The measure done, I'll watch her place of stand
1090 And, touching hers, make blessed my rude hand.
1091 Did my heart love till now? Forswear it, sight,
1092 For I ne'er saw true beauty till this night.
1093 
1094 TYBALT
1095 This, by his voice, should be a Montague.--
1096 Fetch me my rapier, boy.	[Page exits.]
1097 What, dares the slave
1098 Come hither covered with an antic face
1099 To fleer and scorn at our solemnity?
1100 Now, by the stock and honor of my kin,
1101 To strike him dead I hold it not a sin.
1102 
1103 CAPULET
1104 Why, how now, kinsman? Wherefore storm you so?
1105 
1106 TYBALT
1107 Uncle, this is a Montague, our foe,
1108 A villain that is hither come in spite
1109 To scorn at our solemnity this night.
1110 
1111 CAPULET
1112 Young Romeo is it?
1113 
1114 TYBALT  'Tis he, that villain Romeo.
1115 
1116 CAPULET
1117 Content thee, gentle coz. Let him alone.
1118 He bears him like a portly gentleman,
1119 And, to say truth, Verona brags of him
1120 To be a virtuous and well-governed youth.
1121 I would not for the wealth of all this town
1122 Here in my house do him disparagement.
1123 Therefore be patient. Take no note of him.
1124 It is my will, the which if thou respect,
1125 Show a fair presence and put off these frowns,
1126 An ill-beseeming semblance for a feast.
1127 
1128 TYBALT
1129 It fits when such a villain is a guest.
1130 I'll not endure him.
1131 
1132 CAPULET  He shall be endured.
1133 What, goodman boy? I say he shall. Go to.
1134 Am I the master here or you? Go to.
1135 You'll not endure him! God shall mend my soul,
1136 You'll make a mutiny among my guests,
1137 You will set cock-a-hoop, you'll be the man!
1138 
1139 TYBALT
1140 Why, uncle, 'tis a shame.
1141 
1142 CAPULET  Go to, go to.
1143 You are a saucy boy. Is 't so indeed?
1144 This trick may chance to scathe you. I know what.
1145 You must contrary me. Marry, 'tis time--
1146 Well said, my hearts.--You are a princox, go.
1147 Be quiet, or--More light, more light!--for shame,
1148 I'll make you quiet.--What, cheerly, my hearts!
1149 
1150 TYBALT
1151 Patience perforce with willful choler meeting
1152 Makes my flesh tremble in their different greeting.
1153 I will withdraw, but this intrusion shall,
1154 Now seeming sweet, convert to bitt'rest gall.
1155 [He exits.]
1156 
1157 ROMEO, [taking Juliet's hand]
1158 If I profane with my unworthiest hand
1159 This holy shrine, the gentle sin is this:
1160 My lips, two blushing pilgrims, ready stand
1161 To smooth that rough touch with a tender kiss.
1162 
1163 JULIET
1164 Good pilgrim, you do wrong your hand too much,
1165 Which mannerly devotion shows in this;
1166 For saints have hands that pilgrims' hands do touch,
1167 And palm to palm is holy palmers' kiss.
1168 
1169 ROMEO
1170 Have not saints lips, and holy palmers too?
1171 
1172 JULIET
1173 Ay, pilgrim, lips that they must use in prayer.
1174 
1175 ROMEO
1176 O then, dear saint, let lips do what hands do.
1177 They pray: grant thou, lest faith turn to despair.
1178 
1179 JULIET
1180 Saints do not move, though grant for prayers' sake.
1181 
1182 ROMEO
1183 Then move not while my prayer's effect I take.
1184 [He kisses her.]
1185 Thus from my lips, by thine, my sin is purged.
1186 
1187 JULIET
1188 Then have my lips the sin that they have took.
1189 
1190 ROMEO
1191 Sin from my lips? O trespass sweetly urged!
1192 Give me my sin again.	[He kisses her.]
1193 
1194 JULIET  You kiss by th' book.
1195 
1196 NURSE
1197 Madam, your mother craves a word with you.
1198 [Juliet moves toward her mother.]
1199 
1200 ROMEO
1201 What is her mother?
1202 
1203 NURSE  Marry, bachelor,
1204 Her mother is the lady of the house,
1205 And a good lady, and a wise and virtuous.
1206 I nursed her daughter that you talked withal.
1207 I tell you, he that can lay hold of her
1208 Shall have the chinks.	[Nurse moves away.]
1209 
1210 ROMEO, [aside]  Is she a Capulet?
1211 O dear account! My life is my foe's debt.
1212 
1213 BENVOLIO
1214 Away, begone. The sport is at the best.
1215 
1216 ROMEO
1217 Ay, so I fear. The more is my unrest.
1218 
1219 CAPULET
1220 Nay, gentlemen, prepare not to be gone.
1221 We have a trifling foolish banquet towards.--
1222 Is it e'en so? Why then, I thank you all.
1223 I thank you, honest gentlemen. Good night.--
1224 More torches here.--Come on then, let's to bed.--
1225 Ah, sirrah, by my fay, it waxes late.
1226 I'll to my rest.
1227 [All but Juliet and the Nurse begin to exit.]
1228 
1229 JULIET
1230 Come hither, nurse. What is yond gentleman?
1231 
1232 NURSE
1233 The son and heir of old Tiberio.
1234 
1235 JULIET
1236 What's he that now is going out of door?
1237 
1238 NURSE
1239 Marry, that, I think, be young Petruchio.
1240 
1241 JULIET
1242 What's he that follows here, that would not dance?
1243 
1244 NURSE  I know not.
1245 
1246 JULIET
1247 Go ask his name. [The Nurse goes.] If he be married,
1248 My grave is like to be my wedding bed.
1249 
1250 NURSE, [returning]
1251 His name is Romeo, and a Montague,
1252 The only son of your great enemy.
1253 
1254 JULIET
1255 My only love sprung from my only hate!
1256 Too early seen unknown, and known too late!
1257 Prodigious birth of love it is to me
1258 That I must love a loathed enemy.
1259 
1260 NURSE
1261 What's this? What's this?
1262 
1263 JULIET  A rhyme I learned even now
1264 Of one I danced withal.
1265 [One calls within "Juliet."]
1266 
1267 NURSE  Anon, anon.
1268 Come, let's away. The strangers all are gone.
1269 [They exit.]
1270 
1271 
1272 ACT 2
1273 =====
1274 
1275 
1276 
1277 [Enter Chorus.]
1278 
1279 
1280 Now old desire doth in his deathbed lie,
1281 And young affection gapes to be his heir.
1282 That fair for which love groaned for and would die,
1283 With tender Juliet matched, is now not fair.
1284 Now Romeo is beloved and loves again,
1285 Alike bewitched by the charm of looks,
1286 But to his foe supposed he must complain,
1287 And she steal love's sweet bait from fearful hooks.
1288 Being held a foe, he may not have access
1289 To breathe such vows as lovers use to swear,
1290 And she as much in love, her means much less
1291 To meet her new beloved anywhere.
1292 But passion lends them power, time means, to meet,
1293 Temp'ring extremities with extreme sweet.
1294 [Chorus exits.]
1295 
1296 Scene 1
1297 =======
1298 [Enter Romeo alone.]
1299 
1300 
1301 ROMEO
1302 Can I go forward when my heart is here?
1303 Turn back, dull earth, and find thy center out.
1304 [He withdraws.]
1305 
1306 [Enter Benvolio with Mercutio.]
1307 
1308 BENVOLIO
1309 Romeo, my cousin Romeo, Romeo!
1310 
1311 MERCUTIO  He is wise
1312 And, on my life, hath stol'n him home to bed.
1313 
1314 BENVOLIO
1315 He ran this way and leapt this orchard wall.
1316 Call, good Mercutio.
1317 
1318 MERCUTIO  Nay, I'll conjure too.
1319 Romeo! Humors! Madman! Passion! Lover!
1320 Appear thou in the likeness of a sigh.
1321 Speak but one rhyme and I am satisfied.
1322 Cry but "Ay me," pronounce but "love" and
1323 "dove."
1324 Speak to my gossip Venus one fair word,
1325 One nickname for her purblind son and heir,
1326 Young Abraham Cupid, he that shot so trim
1327 When King Cophetua loved the beggar maid.--
1328 He heareth not, he stirreth not, he moveth not.
1329 The ape is dead, and I must conjure him.--
1330 I conjure thee by Rosaline's bright eyes,
1331 By her high forehead, and her scarlet lip,
1332 By her fine foot, straight leg, and quivering thigh,
1333 And the demesnes that there adjacent lie,
1334 That in thy likeness thou appear to us.
1335 
1336 BENVOLIO
1337 An if he hear thee, thou wilt anger him.
1338 
1339 MERCUTIO
1340 This cannot anger him. 'Twould anger him
1341 To raise a spirit in his mistress' circle
1342 Of some strange nature, letting it there stand
1343 Till she had laid it and conjured it down.
1344 That were some spite. My invocation
1345 Is fair and honest. In his mistress' name,
1346 I conjure only but to raise up him.
1347 
1348 BENVOLIO
1349 Come, he hath hid himself among these trees
1350 To be consorted with the humorous night.
1351 Blind is his love and best befits the dark.
1352 
1353 MERCUTIO
1354 If love be blind, love cannot hit the mark.
1355 Now will he sit under a medlar tree
1356 And wish his mistress were that kind of fruit
1357 As maids call medlars when they laugh alone.--
1358 O Romeo, that she were, O, that she were
1359 An open-arse, thou a pop'rin pear.
1360 Romeo, good night. I'll to my truckle bed;
1361 This field-bed is too cold for me to sleep.--
1362 Come, shall we go?
1363 
1364 BENVOLIO  Go, then, for 'tis in vain
1365 To seek him here that means not to be found.
1366 [They exit.]
1367 
1368 Scene 2
1369 =======
1370 [Romeo comes forward.]
1371 
1372 
1373 
1374 ROMEO
1375 He jests at scars that never felt a wound.
1376 
1377 [Enter Juliet above.]
1378 
1379 But soft, what light through yonder window breaks?
1380 It is the East, and Juliet is the sun.
1381 Arise, fair sun, and kill the envious moon,
1382 Who is already sick and pale with grief
1383 That thou, her maid, art far more fair than she.
1384 Be not her maid since she is envious.
1385 Her vestal livery is but sick and green,
1386 And none but fools do wear it. Cast it off.
1387 It is my lady. O, it is my love!
1388 O, that she knew she were!
1389 She speaks, yet she says nothing. What of that?
1390 Her eye discourses; I will answer it.
1391 I am too bold. 'Tis not to me she speaks.
1392 Two of the fairest stars in all the heaven,
1393 Having some business, do entreat her eyes
1394 To twinkle in their spheres till they return.
1395 What if her eyes were there, they in her head?
1396 The brightness of her cheek would shame those
1397 stars
1398 As daylight doth a lamp; her eye in heaven
1399 Would through the airy region stream so bright
1400 That birds would sing and think it were not night.
1401 See how she leans her cheek upon her hand.
1402 O, that I were a glove upon that hand,
1403 That I might touch that cheek!
1404
