1405 JULIET  Ay me.
1406 
1407 ROMEO, [aside]  She speaks.
1408 O, speak again, bright angel, for thou art
1409 As glorious to this night, being o'er my head,
1410 As is a winged messenger of heaven
1411 Unto the white-upturned wond'ring eyes
1412 Of mortals that fall back to gaze on him
1413 When he bestrides the lazy puffing clouds
1414 And sails upon the bosom of the air.
1415 
1416 JULIET
1417 O Romeo, Romeo, wherefore art thou Romeo?
1418 Deny thy father and refuse thy name,
1419 Or, if thou wilt not, be but sworn my love,
1420 And I'll no longer be a Capulet.
1421 
1422 ROMEO, [aside]
1423 Shall I hear more, or shall I speak at this?
1424 
1425 JULIET
1426 'Tis but thy name that is my enemy.
1427 Thou art thyself, though not a Montague.
1428 What's Montague? It is nor hand, nor foot,
1429 Nor arm, nor face. O, be some other name
1430 Belonging to a man.
1431 What's in a name? That which we call a rose
1432 By any other word would smell as sweet.
1433 So Romeo would, were he not Romeo called,
1434 Retain that dear perfection which he owes
1435 Without that title. Romeo, doff thy name,
1436 And, for thy name, which is no part of thee,
1437 Take all myself.
1438 
1439 ROMEO  I take thee at thy word.
1440 Call me but love, and I'll be new baptized.
1441 Henceforth I never will be Romeo.
1442 
1443 JULIET
1444 What man art thou that, thus bescreened in night,
1445 So stumblest on my counsel?
1446 
1447 ROMEO  By a name
1448 I know not how to tell thee who I am.
1449 My name, dear saint, is hateful to myself
1450 Because it is an enemy to thee.
1451 Had I it written, I would tear the word.
1452 
1453 JULIET
1454 My ears have yet not drunk a hundred words
1455 Of thy tongue's uttering, yet I know the sound.
1456 Art thou not Romeo, and a Montague?
1457 
1458 ROMEO
1459 Neither, fair maid, if either thee dislike.
1460 
1461 JULIET
1462 How camest thou hither, tell me, and wherefore?
1463 The orchard walls are high and hard to climb,
1464 And the place death, considering who thou art,
1465 If any of my kinsmen find thee here.
1466 
1467 ROMEO
1468 With love's light wings did I o'erperch these walls,
1469 For stony limits cannot hold love out,
1470 And what love can do, that dares love attempt.
1471 Therefore thy kinsmen are no stop to me.
1472 
1473 JULIET
1474 If they do see thee, they will murder thee.
1475 
1476 ROMEO
1477 Alack, there lies more peril in thine eye
1478 Than twenty of their swords. Look thou but sweet,
1479 And I am proof against their enmity.
1480 
1481 JULIET
1482 I would not for the world they saw thee here.
1483 
1484 ROMEO
1485 I have night's cloak to hide me from their eyes,
1486 And, but thou love me, let them find me here.
1487 My life were better ended by their hate
1488 Than death prorogued, wanting of thy love.
1489 
1490 JULIET
1491 By whose direction found'st thou out this place?
1492 
1493 ROMEO
1494 By love, that first did prompt me to inquire.
1495 He lent me counsel, and I lent him eyes.
1496 I am no pilot; yet, wert thou as far
1497 As that vast shore washed with the farthest sea,
1498 I should adventure for such merchandise.
1499 
1500 JULIET
1501 Thou knowest the mask of night is on my face,
1502 Else would a maiden blush bepaint my cheek
1503 For that which thou hast heard me speak tonight.
1504 Fain would I dwell on form; fain, fain deny
1505 What I have spoke. But farewell compliment.
1506 Dost thou love me? I know thou wilt say "Ay,"
1507 And I will take thy word. Yet, if thou swear'st,
1508 Thou mayst prove false. At lovers' perjuries,
1509 They say, Jove laughs. O gentle Romeo,
1510 If thou dost love, pronounce it faithfully.
1511 Or, if thou thinkest I am too quickly won,
1512 I'll frown and be perverse and say thee nay,
1513 So thou wilt woo, but else not for the world.
1514 In truth, fair Montague, I am too fond,
1515 And therefore thou mayst think my havior light.
1516 But trust me, gentleman, I'll prove more true
1517 Than those that have more coying to be strange.
1518 I should have been more strange, I must confess,
1519 But that thou overheard'st ere I was ware
1520 My true-love passion. Therefore pardon me,
1521 And not impute this yielding to light love,
1522 Which the dark night hath so discovered.
1523 
1524 ROMEO
1525 Lady, by yonder blessed moon I vow,
1526 That tips with silver all these fruit-tree tops--
1527 
1528 JULIET
1529 O, swear not by the moon, th' inconstant moon,
1530 That monthly changes in her circled orb,
1531 Lest that thy love prove likewise variable.
1532 
1533 ROMEO
1534 What shall I swear by?
1535 
1536 JULIET  Do not swear at all.
1537 Or, if thou wilt, swear by thy gracious self,
1538 Which is the god of my idolatry,
1539 And I'll believe thee.
1540 
1541 ROMEO  If my heart's dear love--
1542 
1543 JULIET
1544 Well, do not swear. Although I joy in thee,
1545 I have no joy of this contract tonight.
1546 It is too rash, too unadvised, too sudden,
1547 Too like the lightning, which doth cease to be
1548 Ere one can say "It lightens." Sweet, good night.
1549 This bud of love, by summer's ripening breath,
1550 May prove a beauteous flower when next we meet.
1551 Good night, good night. As sweet repose and rest
1552 Come to thy heart as that within my breast.
1553 
1554 ROMEO
1555 O, wilt thou leave me so unsatisfied?
1556 
1557 JULIET
1558 What satisfaction canst thou have tonight?
1559 
1560 ROMEO
1561 Th' exchange of thy love's faithful vow for mine.
1562 
1563 JULIET
1564 I gave thee mine before thou didst request it,
1565 And yet I would it were to give again.
1566 
1567 ROMEO
1568 Wouldst thou withdraw it? For what purpose, love?
1569 
1570 JULIET
1571 But to be frank and give it thee again.
1572 And yet I wish but for the thing I have.
1573 My bounty is as boundless as the sea,
1574 My love as deep. The more I give to thee,
1575 The more I have, for both are infinite.
1576 [Nurse calls from within.]
1577 I hear some noise within. Dear love, adieu.--
1578 Anon, good nurse.--Sweet Montague, be true.
1579 Stay but a little; I will come again.	[She exits.]
1580 
1581 ROMEO
1582 O blessed, blessed night! I am afeard,
1583 Being in night, all this is but a dream,
1584 Too flattering sweet to be substantial.
1585 
1586 [Reenter Juliet above.]
1587 
1588 
1589 JULIET
1590 Three words, dear Romeo, and good night indeed.
1591 If that thy bent of love be honorable,
1592 Thy purpose marriage, send me word tomorrow,
1593 By one that I'll procure to come to thee,
1594 Where and what time thou wilt perform the rite,
1595 And all my fortunes at thy foot I'll lay
1596 And follow thee my lord throughout the world.
1597 
1598 NURSE, [within]  Madam.
1599 
1600 JULIET
1601 I come anon.--But if thou meanest not well,
1602 I do beseech thee--
1603 
1604 NURSE, [within]  Madam.
1605 
1606 JULIET  By and by, I come.--
1607 To cease thy strife and leave me to my grief.
1608 Tomorrow will I send.
1609 
1610 ROMEO  So thrive my soul--
1611 
1612 JULIET  A thousand times good night.	[She exits.]
1613 
1614 ROMEO
1615 A thousand times the worse to want thy light.
1616 Love goes toward love as schoolboys from their
1617 books,
1618 But love from love, toward school with heavy looks.
1619 [Going.]
1620 
1621 [Enter Juliet above again.]
1622 
1623 
1624 JULIET
1625 Hist, Romeo, hist! O, for a falc'ner's voice
1626 To lure this tassel-gentle back again!
1627 Bondage is hoarse and may not speak aloud,
1628 Else would I tear the cave where Echo lies
1629 And make her airy tongue more hoarse than mine
1630 With repetition of "My Romeo!"
1631 
1632 ROMEO
1633 It is my soul that calls upon my name.
1634 How silver-sweet sound lovers' tongues by night,
1635 Like softest music to attending ears.
1636 
1637 JULIET
1638 Romeo.
1639 
1640 ROMEO  My dear.
1641 
1642 JULIET  What o'clock tomorrow
1643 Shall I send to thee?
1644 
1645 ROMEO  By the hour of nine.
1646 
1647 JULIET
1648 I will not fail. 'Tis twenty year till then.
1649 I have forgot why I did call thee back.
1650 
1651 ROMEO
1652 Let me stand here till thou remember it.
1653 
1654 JULIET
1655 I shall forget, to have thee still stand there,
1656 Rememb'ring how I love thy company.
1657 
1658 ROMEO
1659 And I'll still stay, to have thee still forget,
1660 Forgetting any other home but this.
1661 
1662 JULIET
1663 'Tis almost morning. I would have thee gone,
1664 And yet no farther than a wanton's bird,
1665 That lets it hop a little from his hand,
1666 Like a poor prisoner in his twisted gyves,
1667 And with a silken thread plucks it back again,
1668 So loving-jealous of his liberty.
1669 
1670 ROMEO
1671 I would I were thy bird.
1672 
1673 JULIET  Sweet, so would I.
1674 Yet I should kill thee with much cherishing.
1675 Good night, good night. Parting is such sweet
1676 sorrow
1677 That I shall say "Good night" till it be morrow.
1678 [She exits.]
1679 
1680 ROMEO
1681 Sleep dwell upon thine eyes, peace in thy breast.
1682 Would I were sleep and peace so sweet to rest.
1683 Hence will I to my ghostly friar's close cell,
1684 His help to crave, and my dear hap to tell.
1685 [He exits.]
1686 
1687 Scene 3
1688 =======
1689 [Enter Friar Lawrence alone with a basket.]
1690 
1691 
1692 FRIAR LAWRENCE
1693 The gray-eyed morn smiles on the frowning night,
1694 Check'ring the eastern clouds with streaks of light,
1695 And fleckled darkness like a drunkard reels
1696 From forth day's path and Titan's fiery wheels.
1697 Now, ere the sun advance his burning eye,
1698 The day to cheer and night's dank dew to dry,
1699 I must upfill this osier cage of ours
1700 With baleful weeds and precious-juiced flowers.
1701 The Earth that's nature's mother is her tomb;
1702 What is her burying grave, that is her womb;
1703 And from her womb children of divers kind
1704 We sucking on her natural bosom find,
1705 Many for many virtues excellent,
1706 None but for some, and yet all different.
1707 O, mickle is the powerful grace that lies
1708 In plants, herbs, stones, and their true qualities.
1709 For naught so vile that on the Earth doth live
1710 But to the Earth some special good doth give;
1711 Nor aught so good but, strained from that fair use,
1712 Revolts from true birth, stumbling on abuse.
1713 Virtue itself turns vice, being misapplied,
1714 And vice sometime by action dignified.
1715 
1716 [Enter Romeo.]
1717 
1718 Within the infant rind of this weak flower
1719 Poison hath residence and medicine power:
1720 For this, being smelt, with that part cheers each
1721 part;
1722 Being tasted, stays all senses with the heart.
1723 Two such opposed kings encamp them still
1724 In man as well as herbs--grace and rude will;
1725 And where the worser is predominant,
1726 Full soon the canker death eats up that plant.
1727 
1728 ROMEO
1729 Good morrow, father.
1730 
1731 FRIAR LAWRENCE  Benedicite.
1732 What early tongue so sweet saluteth me?
1733 Young son, it argues a distempered head
1734 So soon to bid "Good morrow" to thy bed.
1735 Care keeps his watch in every old man's eye,
1736 And, where care lodges, sleep will never lie;
1737 But where unbruised youth with unstuffed brain
1738 Doth couch his limbs, there golden sleep doth
1739 reign.
1740 Therefore thy earliness doth me assure
1741 Thou art uproused with some distemp'rature,
1742 Or, if not so, then here I hit it right:
1743 Our Romeo hath not been in bed tonight.
1744 
1745 ROMEO
1746 That last is true. The sweeter rest was mine.
1747 
1748 FRIAR LAWRENCE
1749 God pardon sin! Wast thou with Rosaline?
1750 
1751 ROMEO
1752 With Rosaline, my ghostly father? No.
1753 I have forgot that name and that name's woe.
1754 
1755 FRIAR LAWRENCE
1756 That's my good son. But where hast thou been
1757 then?
1758 
1759 ROMEO
1760 I'll tell thee ere thou ask it me again.
1761 I have been feasting with mine enemy,
1762 Where on a sudden one hath wounded me
1763 That's by me wounded. Both our remedies
1764 Within thy help and holy physic lies.
1765 I bear no hatred, blessed man, for, lo,
1766 My intercession likewise steads my foe.
1767 
1768 FRIAR LAWRENCE
1769 Be plain, good son, and homely in thy drift.
1770 Riddling confession finds but riddling shrift.
1771 
1772 ROMEO
1773 Then plainly know my heart's dear love is set
1774 On the fair daughter of rich Capulet.
1775 As mine on hers, so hers is set on mine,
1776 And all combined, save what thou must combine
1777 By holy marriage. When and where and how
1778 We met, we wooed, and made exchange of vow
1779 I'll tell thee as we pass, but this I pray,
1780 That thou consent to marry us today.
1781 
1782 FRIAR LAWRENCE
1783 Holy Saint Francis, what a change is here!
1784 Is Rosaline, that thou didst love so dear,
1785 So soon forsaken? Young men's love then lies
1786 Not truly in their hearts, but in their eyes.
1787 Jesu Maria, what a deal of brine
1788 Hath washed thy sallow cheeks for Rosaline!
1789 How much salt water thrown away in waste
1790 To season love, that of it doth not taste!
1791 The sun not yet thy sighs from heaven clears,
1792 Thy old groans yet ringing in mine ancient ears.
1793 Lo, here upon thy cheek the stain doth sit
1794 Of an old tear that is not washed off yet.
1795 If e'er thou wast thyself, and these woes thine,
1796 Thou and these woes were all for Rosaline.
1797 And art thou changed? Pronounce this sentence
1798 then:
1799 Women may fall when there's no strength in men.
1800 
1801 ROMEO
1802 Thou chid'st me oft for loving Rosaline.
1803 
1804 FRIAR LAWRENCE
1805 For doting, not for loving, pupil mine.
1806 
1807 ROMEO
1808 And bad'st me bury love.
1809 
1810 FRIAR LAWRENCE  Not in a grave
1811 To lay one in, another out to have.
1812 
1813 ROMEO
1814 I pray thee, chide me not. Her I love now
1815 Doth grace for grace and love for love allow.
1816 The other did not so.
1817 
1818 FRIAR LAWRENCE  O, she knew well
1819 Thy love did read by rote, that could not spell.
1820 But come, young waverer, come, go with me.
1821 In one respect I'll thy assistant be,
1822 For this alliance may so happy prove
1823 To turn your households' rancor to pure love.
1824 
1825 ROMEO
1826 O, let us hence. I stand on sudden haste.
1827 
1828 FRIAR LAWRENCE
1829 Wisely and slow. They stumble that run fast.
1830 [They exit.]
1831 
1832 Scene 4
1833 =======
1834 [Enter Benvolio and Mercutio.]
1835 
1836 
1837 MERCUTIO
1838 Where the devil should this Romeo be?
1839 Came he not home tonight?
1840 
1841 BENVOLIO
1842 Not to his father's. I spoke with his man.
1843 
1844 MERCUTIO
1845 Why, that same pale hard-hearted wench, that
1846 Rosaline,
1847 Torments him so that he will sure run mad.
1848 
1849 BENVOLIO
1850 Tybalt, the kinsman to old Capulet,
1851 Hath sent a letter to his father's house.
1852 
1853 MERCUTIO  A challenge, on my life.
1854 
1855 BENVOLIO  Romeo will answer it.
1856 
1857 MERCUTIO  Any man that can write may answer a letter.
1858 
1859 BENVOLIO  Nay, he will answer the letter's master, how
1860 he dares, being dared.
1861 
1862 MERCUTIO  Alas, poor Romeo, he is already dead,
1863 stabbed with a white wench's black eye, run
1864 through the ear with a love-song, the very pin of his
1865 heart cleft with the blind bow-boy's butt shaft. And
1866 is he a man to encounter Tybalt?
1867 
1868 BENVOLIO  Why, what is Tybalt?
1869 
1870 MERCUTIO  More than prince of cats. O, he's the courageous
1871 captain of compliments. He fights as you sing
1872 prick-song, keeps time, distance, and proportion.
1873 He rests his minim rests, one, two, and the third in
1874 your bosom--the very butcher of a silk button, a
1875 duelist, a duelist, a gentleman of the very first house
1876 of the first and second cause. Ah, the immortal
1877 passado, the punto reverso, the hay!
1878 
1879 BENVOLIO  The what?
1880 
1881 MERCUTIO  The pox of such antic, lisping, affecting
1882 phantasimes, these new tuners of accent: "By
1883 Jesu, a very good blade! A very tall man! A very good
1884 whore!" Why, is not this a lamentable thing, grandsire,
1885 that we should be thus afflicted with these
1886 strange flies, these fashion-mongers, these "pardon-me" 's,
1887 who stand so much on the new form
1888 that they cannot sit at ease on the old bench? O their
1889 bones, their bones!
1890 
1891 [Enter Romeo.]
1892 
1893 
1894 BENVOLIO  Here comes Romeo, here comes Romeo.
1895 
1896 MERCUTIO  Without his roe, like a dried herring. O
1897 flesh, flesh, how art thou fishified! Now is he for the
1898 numbers that Petrarch flowed in. Laura to his lady
1899 was a kitchen wench (marry, she had a better love
1900 to berhyme her), Dido a dowdy, Cleopatra a gypsy,
1901 Helen and Hero hildings and harlots, Thisbe a gray
1902 eye or so, but not to the purpose.--Signior Romeo,
1903 bonjour. There's a French salutation to your French
1904 slop. You gave us the counterfeit fairly last night.
1905 
1906 ROMEO  Good morrow to you both. What counterfeit
1907 did I give you?
1908 
1909 MERCUTIO  The slip, sir, the slip. Can you not conceive?
1910 
1911 ROMEO  Pardon, good Mercutio, my business was
1912 great, and in such a case as mine a man may strain
1913 courtesy.
1914 
1915 MERCUTIO  That's as much as to say such a case as
1916 yours constrains a man to bow in the hams.
1917 
1918 ROMEO  Meaning, to curtsy.
1919 
1920 MERCUTIO  Thou hast most kindly hit it.
1921 
1922 ROMEO  A most courteous exposition.
1923 
1924 MERCUTIO  Nay, I am the very pink of courtesy.
1925 
1926 ROMEO  "Pink" for flower.
1927 
1928 MERCUTIO  Right.
1929 
1930 ROMEO  Why, then is my pump well flowered.
1931 
1932 MERCUTIO  Sure wit, follow me this jest now till thou
1933 hast worn out thy pump, that when the single sole
1934 of it is worn, the jest may remain, after the wearing,
1935 solely singular.
1936 
1937 ROMEO  O single-soled jest, solely singular for the
1938 singleness.
1939 
1940 MERCUTIO  Come between us, good Benvolio. My wits
1941 faints.
1942 
1943 ROMEO  Switch and spurs, switch and spurs, or I'll cry
1944 a match.
1945 
1946 MERCUTIO  Nay, if our wits run the wild-goose chase, I
1947 am done, for thou hast more of the wild goose in
1948 one of thy wits than, I am sure, I have in my whole
1949 five. Was I with you there for the goose?
1950 
1951 ROMEO  Thou wast never with me for anything when
1952 thou wast not there for the goose.
1953 
1954 MERCUTIO  I will bite thee by the ear for that jest.
1955 
1956 ROMEO  Nay, good goose, bite not.
1957 
1958 MERCUTIO  Thy wit is a very bitter sweeting; it is a most
1959 sharp sauce.
1960 
1961 ROMEO  And is it not, then, well served into a sweet
1962 goose?
1963 
1964 MERCUTIO  O, here's a wit of cheveril that stretches
1965 from an inch narrow to an ell broad.
1966 
1967 ROMEO  I stretch it out for that word "broad," which
1968 added to the goose, proves thee far and wide a
1969 broad goose.
1970 
1971 MERCUTIO  Why, is not this better now than groaning
1972 for love? Now art thou sociable, now art thou
1973 Romeo, now art thou what thou art, by art as well as
1974 by nature. For this driveling love is like a great
1975 natural that runs lolling up and down to hide his
1976 bauble in a hole.
1977 
1978 BENVOLIO  Stop there, stop there.
1979 
1980 MERCUTIO  Thou desirest me to stop in my tale against
1981 the hair.
1982 
1983 BENVOLIO  Thou wouldst else have made thy tale large.
1984 
1985 MERCUTIO  O, thou art deceived. I would have made it
1986 short, for I was come to the whole depth of my tale
1987 and meant indeed to occupy the argument no
1988 longer.
1989 
1990 [Enter Nurse and her man Peter.]
1991 
1992 
1993 ROMEO  Here's goodly gear. A sail, a sail!
1994 
1995 MERCUTIO  Two, two--a shirt and a smock.
1996 
1997 NURSE  Peter.
1998 
1999 PETER  Anon.
2000 
2001 NURSE  My fan, Peter.
2002 
2003 MERCUTIO  Good Peter, to hide her face, for her fan's
2004 the fairer face.
2005 
2006 NURSE  God you good morrow, gentlemen.
2007 
2008 MERCUTIO  God you good e'en, fair gentlewoman.
2009 
2010 NURSE  Is it good e'en?
2011 
2012 MERCUTIO  'Tis no less, I tell you, for the bawdy hand of
2013 the dial is now upon the prick of noon.
2014 
2015 NURSE  Out upon you! What a man are you?
2016 
2017 ROMEO  One, gentlewoman, that God hath made, himself
2018 to mar.
2019 
2020 NURSE  By my troth, it is well said: "for himself to
2021 mar," quoth he? Gentlemen, can any of you tell me
2022 where I may find the young Romeo?
2023 
2024 ROMEO  I can tell you, but young Romeo will be older
2025 when you have found him than he was when you
2026 sought him. I am the youngest of that name, for
2027 fault of a worse.
2028 
2029 NURSE  You say well.
2030 
2031 MERCUTIO  Yea, is the worst well? Very well took, i'
2032 faith, wisely, wisely.
2033 
2034 NURSE  If you be he, sir, I desire some confidence with
2035 you.
2036 
2037 BENVOLIO  She will indite him to some supper.
2038 
2039 MERCUTIO  A bawd, a bawd, a bawd. So ho!
2040 
2041 ROMEO  What hast thou found?
2042 
2043 MERCUTIO  No hare, sir, unless a hare, sir, in a Lenten
2044 pie that is something stale and hoar ere it be spent.
2045 [Singing.]	An old hare hoar,
2046 	And an old hare hoar,
2047 	   Is very good meat in Lent.
2048 	But a hare that is hoar
2049 	Is too much for a score
2050 	   When it hoars ere it be spent.
2051 Romeo, will you come to your father's? We'll to
2052 dinner thither.
2053 
2054 ROMEO  I will follow you.
2055 
2056 MERCUTIO  Farewell, ancient lady. Farewell, lady, lady,
2057 lady.	[Mercutio and Benvolio exit.]
2058 
2059 NURSE  I pray you, sir, what saucy merchant was this
2060 that was so full of his ropery?
2061 
2062 ROMEO  A gentleman, nurse, that loves to hear himself
2063 talk and will speak more in a minute than he will
2064 stand to in a month.
2065 
2066 NURSE  An he speak anything against me, I'll take him
2067 down, an he were lustier than he is, and twenty
2068 such jacks. An if I cannot, I'll find those that shall.
2069 Scurvy knave, I am none of his flirt-gills; I am none
2070 of his skains-mates. [To Peter.] And thou must stand
2071 by too and suffer every knave to use me at his
2072 pleasure.
2073 
2074 PETER  I saw no man use you at his pleasure. If I had,
2075 my weapon should quickly have been out. I warrant
2076 you, I dare draw as soon as another man, if I
2077 see occasion in a good quarrel, and the law on my
2078 side.
2079 
2080 NURSE  Now, afore God, I am so vexed that every part
2081 about me quivers. Scurvy knave! [To Romeo.] Pray
2082 you, sir, a word. And, as I told you, my young lady
2083 bid me inquire you out. What she bid me say, I will
2084 keep to myself. But first let me tell you, if you
2085 should lead her in a fool's paradise, as they say, it
2086 were a very gross kind of behavior, as they say. For
2087 the gentlewoman is young; and therefore, if you
2088 should deal double with her, truly it were an ill
2089 thing to be offered to any gentlewoman, and very
2090 weak dealing.
2091 
2092 ROMEO  Nurse, commend me to thy lady and mistress.
2093 I protest unto thee--
2094 
2095 NURSE  Good heart, and i' faith I will tell her as much.
2096 Lord, Lord, she will be a joyful woman.
2097 
2098 ROMEO  What wilt thou tell her, nurse? Thou dost not
2099 mark me.
2100 
2101 NURSE  I will tell her, sir, that you do protest, which, as
2102 I take it, is a gentlemanlike offer.
2103 
2104 ROMEO  Bid her devise
2105 Some means to come to shrift this afternoon,
2106 And there she shall at Friar Lawrence' cell
2107 Be shrived and married. Here is for thy pains.
2108 [Offering her money.]
2109 
2110 NURSE  No, truly, sir, not a penny.
2111 
2112 ROMEO  Go to, I say you shall.
2113 
2114 NURSE
2115 This afternoon, sir? Well, she shall be there.
2116 
2117 ROMEO
2118 And stay, good nurse, behind the abbey wall.
2119 Within this hour my man shall be with thee
2120 And bring thee cords made like a tackled stair,
2121 Which to the high topgallant of my joy
2122 Must be my convoy in the secret night.
2123 Farewell. Be trusty, and I'll quit thy pains.
2124 Farewell. Commend me to thy mistress.
2125 
2126 NURSE
2127 Now, God in heaven bless thee! Hark you, sir.
2128 
2129 ROMEO  What sayst thou, my dear nurse?
2130 
2131 NURSE
2132 Is your man secret? Did you ne'er hear say
2133 "Two may keep counsel, putting one away"?
2134 
2135 ROMEO
2136 Warrant thee, my man's as true as steel.
2137 
2138 NURSE  Well, sir, my mistress is the sweetest lady. Lord,
2139 Lord, when 'twas a little prating thing--O, there is
2140 a nobleman in town, one Paris, that would fain lay
2141 knife aboard, but she, good soul, had as lief see a
2142 toad, a very toad, as see him. I anger her sometimes
2143 and tell her that Paris is the properer man, but I'll
2144 warrant you, when I say so, she looks as pale as any
2145 clout in the versal world. Doth not rosemary and
2146 Romeo begin both with a letter?
2147 
2148 ROMEO  Ay, nurse, what of that? Both with an R.
2149 
2150 NURSE  Ah, mocker, that's the dog's name. R is for
2151 the--No, I know it begins with some other letter,
2152 and she hath the prettiest sententious of it, of you
2153 and rosemary, that it would do you good to hear it.
2154 
2155 ROMEO  Commend me to thy lady.
2156 
2157 NURSE  Ay, a thousand times.--Peter.
2158 
2159 PETER  Anon.
2160 
2161 NURSE  Before and apace.
2162 [They exit.]
2163 
2164 Scene 5
2165 =======
2166 [Enter Juliet.]
2167 
2168 
2169 JULIET
2170 The clock struck nine when I did send the Nurse.
2171 In half an hour she promised to return.
2172 Perchance she cannot meet him. That's not so.
2173 O, she is lame! Love's heralds should be thoughts,
2174 Which ten times faster glides than the sun's beams,
2175 Driving back shadows over louring hills.
2176 Therefore do nimble-pinioned doves draw Love,
2177 And therefore hath the wind-swift Cupid wings.
2178 Now is the sun upon the highmost hill
2179 Of this day's journey, and from nine till twelve
2180 Is three long hours, yet she is not come.
2181 Had she affections and warm youthful blood,
2182 She would be as swift in motion as a ball;
2183 My words would bandy her to my sweet love,
2184 And his to me.
2185 But old folks, many feign as they were dead,
2186 Unwieldy, slow, heavy, and pale as lead.
2187 
2188 [Enter Nurse and Peter.]
2189 
2190 O God, she comes!--O, honey nurse, what news?
2191 Hast thou met with him? Send thy man away.
2192 
2193 NURSE  Peter, stay at the gate.	[Peter exits.]
2194 
2195 JULIET
2196 Now, good sweet nurse--O Lord, why lookest thou
2197 sad?
2198 Though news be sad, yet tell them merrily.
2199 If good, thou shamest the music of sweet news
2200 By playing it to me with so sour a face.
2201 
2202 NURSE
2203 I am aweary. Give me leave awhile.
2204 Fie, how my bones ache! What a jaunt have I!
2205 
2206 JULIET
2207 I would thou hadst my bones, and I thy news.
2208 Nay, come, I pray thee, speak. Good, good nurse,
2209 speak.
2210 
2211 NURSE
2212 Jesu, what haste! Can you not stay awhile?
2213 Do you not see that I am out of breath?
2214 
2215 JULIET
2216 How art thou out of breath, when thou hast breath
2217 To say to me that thou art out of breath?
2218 The excuse that thou dost make in this delay
2219 Is longer than the tale thou dost excuse.
2220 Is thy news good or bad? Answer to that.
2221 Say either, and I'll stay the circumstance.
2222 Let me be satisfied; is 't good or bad?
2223 
2224 NURSE  Well, you have made a simple choice. You know
2225 not how to choose a man. Romeo? No, not he.
2226 Though his face be better than any man's, yet his leg
2227 excels all men's, and for a hand and a foot and a
2228 body, though they be not to be talked on, yet they
2229 are past compare. He is not the flower of courtesy,
2230 but I'll warrant him as gentle as a lamb. Go thy
2231 ways, wench. Serve God. What, have you dined at
2232 home?
2233 
2234 JULIET
2235 No, no. But all this did I know before.
2236 What says he of our marriage? What of that?
2237 
2238 NURSE
2239 Lord, how my head aches! What a head have I!
2240 It beats as it would fall in twenty pieces.
2241 My back o' t' other side! Ah, my back, my back!
2242 Beshrew your heart for sending me about
2243 To catch my death with jaunting up and down.
2244 
2245 JULIET
2246 I' faith, I am sorry that thou art not well.
2247 Sweet, sweet, sweet nurse, tell me, what says my
2248 love?
2249 
2250 NURSE  Your love says, like an honest gentleman, and a
2251 courteous, and a kind, and a handsome, and, I
2252 warrant, a virtuous--Where is your mother?
2253 
2254 JULIET
2255 Where is my mother? Why, she is within.
2256 Where should she be? How oddly thou repliest:
2257 "Your love says, like an honest gentleman,
2258 Where is your mother?"
2259 
2260 NURSE  O God's lady dear,
2261 Are you so hot? Marry, come up, I trow.
2262 Is this the poultice for my aching bones?
2263 Henceforward do your messages yourself.
2264 
2265 JULIET
2266 Here's such a coil. Come, what says Romeo?
2267 
2268 NURSE
2269 Have you got leave to go to shrift today?
2270 
2271 JULIET  I have.
2272 
2273 NURSE
2274 Then hie you hence to Friar Lawrence' cell.
2275 There stays a husband to make you a wife.
2276 Now comes the wanton blood up in your cheeks;
2277 They'll be in scarlet straight at any news.
2278 Hie you to church. I must another way,
2279 To fetch a ladder by the which your love
2280 Must climb a bird's nest soon when it is dark.
2281 I am the drudge and toil in your delight,
2282 But you shall bear the burden soon at night.
2283 Go. I'll to dinner. Hie you to the cell.
2284 
2285 JULIET
2286 Hie to high fortune! Honest nurse, farewell.
2287 [They exit.]
2288 
2289 Scene 6
2290 =======
2291 [Enter Friar Lawrence and Romeo.]
2292 
2293 
2294 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2295 So smile the heavens upon this holy act
2296 That after-hours with sorrow chide us not.
2297 
2298 ROMEO
2299 Amen, amen. But come what sorrow can,
2300 It cannot countervail the exchange of joy
2301 That one short minute gives me in her sight.
2302 Do thou but close our hands with holy words,
2303 Then love-devouring death do what he dare,
2304 It is enough I may but call her mine.
2305 
2306 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2307 These violent delights have violent ends
2308 And in their triumph die, like fire and powder,
2309 Which, as they kiss, consume. The sweetest honey
2310 Is loathsome in his own deliciousness
2311 And in the taste confounds the appetite.
2312 Therefore love moderately. Long love doth so.
2313 Too swift arrives as tardy as too slow.
2314 
2315 [Enter Juliet.]
2316 
2317 Here comes the lady. O, so light a foot
2318 Will ne'er wear out the everlasting flint.
2319 A lover may bestride the gossamers
2320 That idles in the wanton summer air,
2321 And yet not fall, so light is vanity.
2322 
2323 JULIET
2324 Good even to my ghostly confessor.
2325 
2326 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2327 Romeo shall thank thee, daughter, for us both.
2328 
2329 JULIET
2330 As much to him, else is his thanks too much.
2331 
2332 ROMEO
2333 Ah, Juliet, if the measure of thy joy
2334 Be heaped like mine, and that thy skill be more
2335 To blazon it, then sweeten with thy breath
2336 This neighbor air, and let rich music's tongue
2337 Unfold the imagined happiness that both
2338 Receive in either by this dear encounter.
2339 
2340 JULIET
2341 Conceit, more rich in matter than in words,
2342 Brags of his substance, not of ornament.
2343 They are but beggars that can count their worth,
2344 But my true love is grown to such excess
2345 I cannot sum up sum of half my wealth.
2346 
2347 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2348 Come, come with me, and we will make short work,
2349 For, by your leaves, you shall not stay alone
2350 Till Holy Church incorporate two in one.
2351 [They exit.]
2352 
2353 
2354 ACT 3
2355 =====
2356 
2357 Scene 1
2358 =======
2359 [Enter Mercutio, Benvolio, and their men.]
2360 
2361 
2362 BENVOLIO
2363 I pray thee, good Mercutio, let's retire.
2364 The day is hot, the Capels are abroad,
2365 And if we meet we shall not 'scape a brawl,
2366 For now, these hot days, is the mad blood stirring.
2367 
2368 MERCUTIO  Thou art like one of these fellows that, when
2369 he enters the confines of a tavern, claps me his
2370 sword upon the table and says "God send me no
2371 need of thee" and, by the operation of the second
2372 cup, draws him on the drawer when indeed there is
2373 no need.
2374 
2375 BENVOLIO  Am I like such a fellow?
2376 
2377 MERCUTIO  Come, come, thou art as hot a jack in thy
2378 mood as any in Italy, and as soon moved to be
2379 moody, and as soon moody to be moved.
2380 
2381 BENVOLIO  And what to?
2382 
2383 MERCUTIO  Nay, an there were two such, we should
2384 have none shortly, for one would kill the other.
2385 Thou--why, thou wilt quarrel with a man that
2386 hath a hair more or a hair less in his beard than
2387 thou hast. Thou wilt quarrel with a man for cracking
2388 nuts, having no other reason but because thou
2389 hast hazel eyes. What eye but such an eye would spy
2390 out such a quarrel? Thy head is as full of quarrels as
2391 an egg is full of meat, and yet thy head hath been
2392 beaten as addle as an egg for quarreling. Thou hast
2393 quarreled with a man for coughing in the street
2394 because he hath wakened thy dog that hath lain
2395 asleep in the sun. Didst thou not fall out with a tailor
2396 for wearing his new doublet before Easter? With
2397 another, for tying his new shoes with old ribbon?
2398 And yet thou wilt tutor me from quarreling?
2399 
2400 BENVOLIO  An I were so apt to quarrel as thou art, any
2401 man should buy the fee simple of my life for an
2402 hour and a quarter.
2403 
2404 MERCUTIO  The fee simple? O simple!
2405 
2406 [Enter Tybalt, Petruchio, and others.]
2407 
2408 
2409 BENVOLIO  By my head, here comes the Capulets.
2410 
2411 MERCUTIO  By my heel, I care not.
2412 
2413 TYBALT, [to his companions]
2414 Follow me close, for I will speak to them.--
2415 Gentlemen, good e'en. A word with one of you.
2416 
2417 MERCUTIO  And but one word with one of us? Couple it
2418 with something. Make it a word and a blow.
2419 
2420 TYBALT  You shall find me apt enough to that, sir, an
2421 you will give me occasion.
2422 
2423 MERCUTIO  Could you not take some occasion without
2424 giving?
2425 
2426 TYBALT  Mercutio, thou consortest with Romeo.
2427 
2428 MERCUTIO  Consort? What, dost thou make us minstrels?
2429 An thou make minstrels of us, look to hear
2430 nothing but discords. Here's my fiddlestick; here's
2431 that shall make you dance. Zounds, consort!
2432 
2433 BENVOLIO
2434 We talk here in the public haunt of men.
2435 Either withdraw unto some private place,
2436 Or reason coldly of your grievances,
2437 Or else depart. Here all eyes gaze on us.
2438 
2439 MERCUTIO
2440 Men's eyes were made to look, and let them gaze.
2441 I will not budge for no man's pleasure, I.
2442 
2443 [Enter Romeo.]
2444 
2445 
2446 TYBALT
2447 Well, peace be with you, sir. Here comes my man.
2448 
2449 MERCUTIO
2450 But I'll be hanged, sir, if he wear your livery.
2451 Marry, go before to field, he'll be your follower.
2452 Your Worship in that sense may call him "man."
2453 
2454 TYBALT
2455 Romeo, the love I bear thee can afford
2456 No better term than this: thou art a villain.
2457 
2458 ROMEO
2459 Tybalt, the reason that I have to love thee
2460 Doth much excuse the appertaining rage
2461 To such a greeting. Villain am I none.
2462 Therefore farewell. I see thou knowest me not.
2463 
2464 TYBALT
2465 Boy, this shall not excuse the injuries
2466 That thou hast done me. Therefore turn and draw.
2467 
2468 ROMEO
2469 I do protest I never injured thee
2470 But love thee better than thou canst devise
2471 Till thou shalt know the reason of my love.
2472 And so, good Capulet, which name I tender
2473 As dearly as mine own, be satisfied.
2474 
2475 MERCUTIO
2476 O calm, dishonorable, vile submission!
2477 Alla stoccato carries it away.	[He draws.]
2478 Tybalt, you ratcatcher, will you walk?
2479 
2480 TYBALT  What wouldst thou have with me?
2481 
2482 MERCUTIO  Good king of cats, nothing but one of your
2483 nine lives, that I mean to make bold withal, and, as
2484 you shall use me hereafter, dry-beat the rest of the
2485 eight. Will you pluck your sword out of his pilcher
2486 by the ears? Make haste, lest mine be about your
2487 ears ere it be out.
2488 
2489 TYBALT  I am for you.	[He draws.]
2490 
2491 ROMEO
2492 Gentle Mercutio, put thy rapier up.
2493 
2494 MERCUTIO  Come, sir, your passado.	[They fight.]
2495 
2496 ROMEO
2497 Draw, Benvolio, beat down their weapons.
2498 [Romeo draws.]
2499 Gentlemen, for shame forbear this outrage!
2500 Tybalt! Mercutio! The Prince expressly hath
2501 Forbid this bandying in Verona streets.
2502 Hold, Tybalt! Good Mercutio!
2503 [Romeo attempts to beat down their rapiers.
2504 Tybalt stabs Mercutio.]
2505 
2506 PETRUCHIO  Away, Tybalt!
2507 [Tybalt, Petruchio, and their followers exit.]
2508 
2509 MERCUTIO  I am hurt.
2510 A plague o' both houses! I am sped.
2511 Is he gone and hath nothing?
2512 
2513 BENVOLIO  What, art thou hurt?
2514 
2515 MERCUTIO
2516 Ay, ay, a scratch, a scratch. Marry, 'tis enough.
2517 Where is my page?--Go, villain, fetch a surgeon.
2518 [Page exits.]
2519 
2520 ROMEO
2521 Courage, man, the hurt cannot be much.
2522 
2523 MERCUTIO  No, 'tis not so deep as a well, nor so wide as
2524 a church door, but 'tis enough. 'Twill serve. Ask for
2525 me tomorrow, and you shall find me a grave man. I
2526 am peppered, I warrant, for this world. A plague o'
2527 both your houses! Zounds, a dog, a rat, a mouse, a
2528 cat, to scratch a man to death! A braggart, a rogue, a
2529 villain that fights by the book of arithmetic! Why the
2530 devil came you between us? I was hurt under your
2531 arm.
2532 
2533 ROMEO  I thought all for the best.
2534 
2535 MERCUTIO
2536 Help me into some house, Benvolio,
2537 Or I shall faint. A plague o' both your houses!
2538 They have made worms' meat of me.
2539 I have it, and soundly, too. Your houses!
2540 [All but Romeo exit.]
2541 
2542 ROMEO
2543 This gentleman, the Prince's near ally,
2544 My very friend, hath got this mortal hurt
2545 In my behalf. My reputation stained
2546 With Tybalt's slander--Tybalt, that an hour
2547 Hath been my cousin! O sweet Juliet,
2548 Thy beauty hath made me effeminate
2549 And in my temper softened valor's steel.
2550 
2551 [Enter Benvolio.]
2552 
2553 
2554 BENVOLIO
2555 O Romeo, Romeo, brave Mercutio is dead.
2556 That gallant spirit hath aspired the clouds,
2557 Which too untimely here did scorn the earth.
2558 
2559 ROMEO
2560 This day's black fate on more days doth depend.
2561 This but begins the woe others must end.
2562 
2563 [Enter Tybalt.]
2564 
2565 
2566 BENVOLIO
2567 Here comes the furious Tybalt back again.
2568 
2569 ROMEO
2570 Alive in triumph, and Mercutio slain!
2571 Away to heaven, respective lenity,
2572 And fire-eyed fury be my conduct now.--
2573 Now, Tybalt, take the "villain" back again
2574 That late thou gavest me, for Mercutio's soul
2575 Is but a little way above our heads,
2576 Staying for thine to keep him company.
2577 Either thou or I, or both, must go with him.
2578 
2579 TYBALT
2580 Thou wretched boy that didst consort him here
2581 Shalt with him hence.
2582 
2583 ROMEO  This shall determine that.
2584 [They fight. Tybalt falls.]
2585 
2586 BENVOLIO
2587 Romeo, away, begone!
2588 The citizens are up, and Tybalt slain.
2589 Stand not amazed. The Prince will doom thee death
2590 If thou art taken. Hence, be gone, away.
2591 
2592 ROMEO
2593 O, I am Fortune's fool!
2594 
2595 BENVOLIO  Why dost thou stay?
2596 [Romeo exits.]
2597 
2598 [Enter Citizens.]
2599 
2600 
2601 CITIZEN
2602 Which way ran he that killed Mercutio?
2603 Tybalt, that murderer, which way ran he?
2604 
2605 BENVOLIO
2606 There lies that Tybalt.
2607 
2608 CITIZEN, [to Tybalt]  Up, sir, go with me.
2609 I charge thee in the Prince's name, obey.
2610 
2611 [Enter Prince, old Montague, Capulet, their Wives and all.]
2612 
2613 
2614 PRINCE
2615 Where are the vile beginners of this fray?
2616 
2617 BENVOLIO
2618 O noble prince, I can discover all
2619 The unlucky manage of this fatal brawl.
2620 There lies the man, slain by young Romeo,
2621 That slew thy kinsman, brave Mercutio.
2622 
2623 LADY CAPULET
2624 Tybalt, my cousin, O my brother's child!
2625 O prince! O cousin! Husband! O, the blood is spilled
2626 Of my dear kinsman! Prince, as thou art true,
2627 For blood of ours, shed blood of Montague.
2628 O cousin, cousin!
2629 
2630 PRINCE
2631 Benvolio, who began this bloody fray?
2632 
2633 BENVOLIO
2634 Tybalt, here slain, whom Romeo's hand did slay--
2635 Romeo, that spoke him fair, bid him bethink
2636 How nice the quarrel was, and urged withal
2637 Your high displeasure. All this uttered
2638 With gentle breath, calm look, knees humbly bowed
2639 Could not take truce with the unruly spleen
2640 Of Tybalt, deaf to peace, but that he tilts
2641 With piercing steel at bold Mercutio's breast,
2642 Who, all as hot, turns deadly point to point
2643 And, with a martial scorn, with one hand beats
2644 Cold death aside and with the other sends
2645 It back to Tybalt, whose dexterity
2646 Retorts it. Romeo he cries aloud
2647 "Hold, friends! Friends, part!" and swifter than his
2648 tongue
2649 His agile arm beats down their fatal points,
2650 And 'twixt them rushes; underneath whose arm
2651 An envious thrust from Tybalt hit the life
2652 Of stout Mercutio, and then Tybalt fled.
2653 But by and by comes back to Romeo,
2654 Who had but newly entertained revenge,
2655 And to 't they go like lightning, for ere I
2656 Could draw to part them was stout Tybalt slain,
2657 And, as he fell, did Romeo turn and fly.
2658 This is the truth, or let Benvolio die.
2659 
2660 LADY CAPULET
2661 He is a kinsman to the Montague.
2662 Affection makes him false; he speaks not true.
2663 Some twenty of them fought in this black strife,
2664 And all those twenty could but kill one life.
2665 I beg for justice, which thou, prince, must give.
2666 Romeo slew Tybalt; Romeo must not live.
2667 
2668 PRINCE
2669 Romeo slew him; he slew Mercutio.
2670 Who now the price of his dear blood doth owe?
2671 
2672 MONTAGUE
2673 Not Romeo, Prince; he was Mercutio's friend.
2674 His fault concludes but what the law should end,
2675 The life of Tybalt.
2676 
2677 PRINCE  And for that offense
2678 Immediately we do exile him hence.
2679 I have an interest in your hearts' proceeding:
2680 My blood for your rude brawls doth lie a-bleeding.
2681 But I'll amerce you with so strong a fine
2682 That you shall all repent the loss of mine.
2683 I will be deaf to pleading and excuses.
2684 Nor tears nor prayers shall purchase out abuses.
2685 Therefore use none. Let Romeo hence in haste,
2686 Else, when he is found, that hour is his last.
2687 Bear hence this body and attend our will.
2688 Mercy but murders, pardoning those that kill.
2689 [They exit, the Capulet men
2690 bearing off Tybalt's body.]
2691 
2692 Scene 2
2693 =======
2694 [Enter Juliet alone.]
2695 
2696 
2697 JULIET
2698 Gallop apace, you fiery-footed steeds,
2699 Towards Phoebus' lodging. Such a wagoner
2700 As Phaeton would whip you to the west
2701 And bring in cloudy night immediately.
2702 Spread thy close curtain, love-performing night,
2703 That runaways' eyes may wink, and Romeo
2704 Leap to these arms, untalked of and unseen.
2705 Lovers can see to do their amorous rites
2706 By their own beauties, or, if love be blind,
2707 It best agrees with night. Come, civil night,
2708 Thou sober-suited matron all in black,
2709 And learn me how to lose a winning match
2710 Played for a pair of stainless maidenhoods.
2711 Hood my unmanned blood, bating in my cheeks,
2712 With thy black mantle till strange love grow bold,
2713 Think true love acted simple modesty.
2714 Come, night. Come, Romeo. Come, thou day in
2715 night,
2716 For thou wilt lie upon the wings of night
2717 Whiter than new snow upon a raven's back.
2718 Come, gentle night; come, loving black-browed
2719 night,
2720 Give me my Romeo, and when I shall die,
2721 Take him and cut him out in little stars,
2722 And he will make the face of heaven so fine
2723 That all the world will be in love with night
2724 And pay no worship to the garish sun.
2725 O, I have bought the mansion of a love
2726 But not possessed it, and, though I am sold,
2727 Not yet enjoyed. So tedious is this day
2728 As is the night before some festival
2729 To an impatient child that hath new robes
2730 And may not wear them.
2731 
2732 [Enter Nurse with cords.]
2733 
2734 O, here comes my nurse,
2735 And she brings news, and every tongue that speaks
2736 But Romeo's name speaks heavenly eloquence.--
2737 Now, nurse, what news? What hast thou there? The
2738 cords
2739 That Romeo bid thee fetch?
2740 
2741 NURSE  Ay, ay, the cords.
2742 [Dropping the rope ladder.]
2743 
2744 JULIET
2745 Ay me, what news? Why dost thou wring thy hands?
2746 
2747 NURSE
2748 Ah weraday, he's dead, he's dead, he's dead!
2749 We are undone, lady, we are undone.
2750 Alack the day, he's gone, he's killed, he's dead.
2751 
2752 JULIET
2753 Can heaven be so envious?
2754 
2755 NURSE  Romeo can,
2756 Though heaven cannot. O Romeo, Romeo,
2757 Whoever would have thought it? Romeo!
2758 
2759 JULIET
2760 What devil art thou that dost torment me thus?
2761 This torture should be roared in dismal hell.
2762 Hath Romeo slain himself? Say thou but "Ay,"
2763 And that bare vowel "I" shall poison more
2764 Than the death-darting eye of cockatrice.
2765 I am not I if there be such an "I,"
2766 Or those eyes shut that makes thee answer "Ay."
2767 If he be slain, say "Ay," or if not, "No."
2768 Brief sounds determine my weal or woe.
2769 
2770 NURSE
2771 I saw the wound. I saw it with mine eyes
2772 (God save the mark!) here on his manly breast--
2773 A piteous corse, a bloody piteous corse,
2774 Pale, pale as ashes, all bedaubed in blood,
2775 All in gore blood. I swooned at the sight.
2776 
2777 JULIET
2778 O break, my heart, poor bankrout, break at once!
2779 To prison, eyes; ne'er look on liberty.
2780 Vile earth to earth resign; end motion here,
2781 And thou and Romeo press one heavy bier.
2782 
2783 NURSE
2784 O Tybalt, Tybalt, the best friend I had!
2785 O courteous Tybalt, honest gentleman,
2786 That ever I should live to see thee dead!
2787 
2788 JULIET
2789 What storm is this that blows so contrary?
2790 Is Romeo slaughtered and is Tybalt dead?
2791 My dearest cousin, and my dearer lord?
2792 Then, dreadful trumpet, sound the general doom,
2793 For who is living if those two are gone?
2794 
2795 NURSE
2796 Tybalt is gone and Romeo banished.
2797 Romeo that killed him--he is banished.
2798 
2799 JULIET
2800 O God, did Romeo's hand shed Tybalt's blood?
2801 
2802 NURSE
2803 It did, it did, alas the day, it did.
2804 
2805 JULIET
2806 O serpent heart hid with a flow'ring face!
2807 Did ever dragon keep so fair a cave?
2808 Beautiful tyrant, fiend angelical!
2809 Dove-feathered raven, wolvish-ravening lamb!
2810 Despised substance of divinest show!
2811 Just opposite to what thou justly seem'st,
2812 A damned saint, an honorable villain.
2813 O nature, what hadst thou to do in hell
2814 When thou didst bower the spirit of a fiend
2815 In mortal paradise of such sweet flesh?
2816 Was ever book containing such vile matter
2817 So fairly bound? O, that deceit should dwell
2818 In such a gorgeous palace!
2819 
2820 NURSE  There's no trust,
2821 No faith, no honesty in men. All perjured,
2822 All forsworn, all naught, all dissemblers.
2823 Ah, where's my man? Give me some aqua vitae.
2824 These griefs, these woes, these sorrows make me
2825 old.
2826 Shame come to Romeo!
2827 
2828 JULIET  Blistered be thy tongue
2829 For such a wish! He was not born to shame.
2830 Upon his brow shame is ashamed to sit,
2831 For 'tis a throne where honor may be crowned
2832 Sole monarch of the universal Earth.
2833 O, what a beast was I to chide at him!
2834 
2835 NURSE
2836 Will you speak well of him that killed your cousin?
2837 
2838 JULIET
2839 Shall I speak ill of him that is my husband?
2840 Ah, poor my lord, what tongue shall smooth thy
2841 name
2842 When I, thy three-hours wife, have mangled it?
2843 But wherefore, villain, didst thou kill my cousin?
2844 That villain cousin would have killed my husband.
2845 Back, foolish tears, back to your native spring;
2846 Your tributary drops belong to woe,
2847 Which you, mistaking, offer up to joy.
2848 My husband lives, that Tybalt would have slain,
2849 And Tybalt's dead, that would have slain my
2850 husband.
2851 All this is comfort. Wherefore weep I then?
2852 Some word there was, worser than Tybalt's death,
2853 That murdered me. I would forget it fain,
2854 But, O, it presses to my memory
2855 Like damned guilty deeds to sinners' minds:
2856 "Tybalt is dead and Romeo banished."
2857 That "banished," that one word "banished,"
2858 Hath slain ten thousand Tybalts. Tybalt's death
2859 Was woe enough if it had ended there;
2860 Or, if sour woe delights in fellowship
2861 And needly will be ranked with other griefs,
2862 Why followed not, when she said "Tybalt's dead,"
2863 "Thy father" or "thy mother," nay, or both,
2864 Which modern lamentation might have moved?
2865 But with a rearward following Tybalt's death,
2866 "Romeo is banished." To speak that word
2867 Is father, mother, Tybalt, Romeo, Juliet,
2868 All slain, all dead. "Romeo is banished."
2869 There is no end, no limit, measure, bound,
2870 In that word's death. No words can that woe sound.
2871 Where is my father and my mother, nurse?
2872 
2873 NURSE
2874 Weeping and wailing over Tybalt's corse.
2875 Will you go to them? I will bring you thither.
2876 
2877 JULIET
2878 Wash they his wounds with tears? Mine shall be
2879 spent,
2880 When theirs are dry, for Romeo's banishment.--
2881 Take up those cords.
2882 [The Nurse picks up the rope ladder.]
2883 Poor ropes, you are beguiled,
2884 Both you and I, for Romeo is exiled.
2885 He made you for a highway to my bed,
2886 But I, a maid, die maiden-widowed.
2887 Come, cords--come, nurse. I'll to my wedding bed,
2888 And death, not Romeo, take my maidenhead!
2889 
2890 NURSE
2891 Hie to your chamber. I'll find Romeo
2892 To comfort you. I wot well where he is.
2893 Hark you, your Romeo will be here at night.
2894 I'll to him. He is hid at Lawrence' cell.
2895 
2896 JULIET
2897 O, find him!	[Giving the Nurse a ring.]
2898 Give this ring to my true knight
2899 And bid him come to take his last farewell.
2900 [They exit.]
2901 
2902 Scene 3
2903 =======
2904 [Enter Friar Lawrence.]
2905 
2906 
2907 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2908 Romeo, come forth; come forth, thou fearful man.
2909 Affliction is enamored of thy parts,
2910 And thou art wedded to calamity.
2911 
2912 [Enter Romeo.]
2913 
2914 
2915 ROMEO
2916 Father, what news? What is the Prince's doom?
2917 What sorrow craves acquaintance at my hand
2918 That I yet know not?
2919 
2920 FRIAR LAWRENCE  Too familiar
2921 Is my dear son with such sour company.
2922 I bring thee tidings of the Prince's doom.
2923 
2924 ROMEO
2925 What less than doomsday is the Prince's doom?
2926 
2927 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2928 A gentler judgment vanished from his lips:
2929 Not body's death, but body's banishment.
2930 
2931 ROMEO
2932 Ha, banishment? Be merciful, say "death,"
2933 For exile hath more terror in his look,
2934 Much more than death. Do not say "banishment."
2935 
2936 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2937 Here from Verona art thou banished.
2938 Be patient, for the world is broad and wide.
2939 
2940 ROMEO
2941 There is no world without Verona walls
2942 But purgatory, torture, hell itself.
2943 Hence "banished" is "banished from the world,"
2944 And world's exile is death. Then "banished"
2945 Is death mistermed. Calling death "banished,"
2946 Thou cutt'st my head off with a golden ax
2947 And smilest upon the stroke that murders me.
2948 
2949 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2950 O deadly sin, O rude unthankfulness!
2951 Thy fault our law calls death, but the kind prince,
2952 Taking thy part, hath rushed aside the law
2953 And turned that black word "death" to
2954 "banishment."
2955 This is dear mercy, and thou seest it not.
2956 
2957 ROMEO
2958 'Tis torture and not mercy. Heaven is here
2959 Where Juliet lives, and every cat and dog
2960 And little mouse, every unworthy thing,
2961 Live here in heaven and may look on her,
2962 But Romeo may not. More validity,
2963 More honorable state, more courtship lives
2964 In carrion flies than Romeo. They may seize
2965 On the white wonder of dear Juliet's hand
2966 And steal immortal blessing from her lips,
2967 Who even in pure and vestal modesty
2968 Still blush, as thinking their own kisses sin;
2969 But Romeo may not; he is banished.
2970 Flies may do this, but I from this must fly.
2971 They are free men, but I am banished.
2972 And sayest thou yet that exile is not death?
2973 Hadst thou no poison mixed, no sharp-ground
2974 knife,
2975 No sudden mean of death, though ne'er so mean,
2976 But "banished" to kill me? "Banished"?
2977 O friar, the damned use that word in hell.
2978 Howling attends it. How hast thou the heart,
2979 Being a divine, a ghostly confessor,
2980 A sin absolver, and my friend professed,
2981 To mangle me with that word "banished"?
2982 
2983 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2984 Thou fond mad man, hear me a little speak.
2985 
2986 ROMEO
2987 O, thou wilt speak again of banishment.
2988 
2989 FRIAR LAWRENCE
2990 I'll give thee armor to keep off that word,
2991 Adversity's sweet milk, philosophy,
2992 To comfort thee, though thou art banished.
2993 
2994 ROMEO
2995 Yet "banished"? Hang up philosophy.
2996 Unless philosophy can make a Juliet,
2997 Displant a town, reverse a prince's doom,
2998 It helps not, it prevails not. Talk no more.
2999 
3000 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3001 O, then I see that madmen have no ears.
3002 
3003 ROMEO
3004 How should they when that wise men have no eyes?
3005 
3006 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3007 Let me dispute with thee of thy estate.
3008 
3009 ROMEO
3010 Thou canst not speak of that thou dost not feel.
3011 Wert thou as young as I, Juliet thy love,
3012 An hour but married, Tybalt murdered,
3013 Doting like me, and like me banished,
3014 Then mightst thou speak, then mightst thou tear thy
3015 hair
3016 And fall upon the ground as I do now,
3017 [Romeo throws himself down.]
3018 Taking the measure of an unmade grave.
3019 [Knock within.]
3020 
3021 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3022 Arise. One knocks. Good Romeo, hide thyself.
3023 
3024 ROMEO
3025 Not I, unless the breath of heartsick groans,
3026 Mistlike, enfold me from the search of eyes.
3027 [Knock.]
3028 
3029 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3030 Hark, how they knock!--Who's there?--Romeo,
3031 arise.
3032 Thou wilt be taken.--Stay awhile.--Stand up.
3033 [Knock.]
3034 Run to my study.--By and by.--God's will,
3035 What simpleness is this?--I come, I come.
3036 [Knock.]
3037 Who knocks so hard? Whence come you? What's
3038 your will?
3039 
3040 NURSE, [within]
3041 Let me come in, and you shall know my errand.
3042 I come from Lady Juliet.
3043 
3044 FRIAR LAWRENCE, [admitting the Nurse]
3045 Welcome, then.
3046 
3047 [Enter Nurse.]
3048 
3049 
3050 NURSE
3051 O holy friar, O, tell me, holy friar,
3052 Where's my lady's lord? Where's Romeo?
3053 
3054 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3055 There on the ground, with his own tears made
3056 drunk.
3057 
3058 NURSE
3059 O, he is even in my mistress' case,
3060 Just in her case. O woeful sympathy!
3061 Piteous predicament! Even so lies she,
3062 Blubb'ring and weeping, weeping and blubb'ring.--
3063 Stand up, stand up. Stand an you be a man.
3064 For Juliet's sake, for her sake, rise and stand.
3065 Why should you fall into so deep an O?
3066 
3067 ROMEO  Nurse.
3068 
3069 NURSE
3070 Ah sir, ah sir, death's the end of all.
3071 
3072 ROMEO, [rising up]
3073 Spakest thou of Juliet? How is it with her?
3074 Doth not she think me an old murderer,
3075 Now I have stained the childhood of our joy
3076 With blood removed but little from her own?
3077 Where is she? And how doth she? And what says
3078 My concealed lady to our canceled love?
3079 
3080 NURSE
3081 O, she says nothing, sir, but weeps and weeps,
3082 And now falls on her bed, and then starts up,
3083 And "Tybalt" calls, and then on Romeo cries,
3084 And then down falls again.
3085 
3086 ROMEO  As if that name,
3087 Shot from the deadly level of a gun,
3088 Did murder her, as that name's cursed hand
3089 Murdered her kinsman.--O, tell me, friar, tell me,
3090 In what vile part of this anatomy
3091 Doth my name lodge? Tell me, that I may sack
3092 The hateful mansion.	[He draws his dagger.]
3093 
3094 FRIAR LAWRENCE  Hold thy desperate hand!
3095 Art thou a man? Thy form cries out thou art.
3096 Thy tears are womanish; thy wild acts denote
3097 The unreasonable fury of a beast.
3098 Unseemly woman in a seeming man,
3099 And ill-beseeming beast in seeming both!
3100 Thou hast amazed me. By my holy order,
3101 I thought thy disposition better tempered.
3102 Hast thou slain Tybalt? Wilt thou slay thyself,
3103 And slay thy lady that in thy life lives,
3104 By doing damned hate upon thyself?
3105 Why railest thou on thy birth, the heaven, and earth,
3106 Since birth and heaven and earth all three do meet
3107 In thee at once, which thou at once wouldst lose?
3108 Fie, fie, thou shamest thy shape, thy love, thy wit,
3109 Which, like a usurer, abound'st in all
3110 And usest none in that true use indeed
3111 Which should bedeck thy shape, thy love, thy wit.
3112 Thy noble shape is but a form of wax,
3113 Digressing from the valor of a man;
3114 Thy dear love sworn but hollow perjury,
3115 Killing that love which thou hast vowed to cherish;
3116 Thy wit, that ornament to shape and love,
3117 Misshapen in the conduct of them both,
3118 Like powder in a skilless soldier's flask,
3119 Is set afire by thine own ignorance,
3120 And thou dismembered with thine own defense.
3121 What, rouse thee, man! Thy Juliet is alive,
3122 For whose dear sake thou wast but lately dead:
3123 There art thou happy. Tybalt would kill thee,
3124 But thou slewest Tybalt: there art thou happy.
3125 The law that threatened death becomes thy friend
3126 And turns it to exile: there art thou happy.
3127 A pack of blessings light upon thy back;
3128 Happiness courts thee in her best array;
3129 But, like a misbehaved and sullen wench,
3130 Thou pouts upon thy fortune and thy love.
3131 Take heed, take heed, for such die miserable.
3132 Go, get thee to thy love, as was decreed.
3133 Ascend her chamber. Hence and comfort her.
3134 But look thou stay not till the watch be set,
3135 For then thou canst not pass to Mantua,
3136 Where thou shalt live till we can find a time
3137 To blaze your marriage, reconcile your friends,
3138 Beg pardon of the Prince, and call thee back
3139 With twenty hundred thousand times more joy
3140 Than thou went'st forth in lamentation.--
3141 Go before, nurse. Commend me to thy lady,
3142 And bid her hasten all the house to bed,
3143 Which heavy sorrow makes them apt unto.
3144 Romeo is coming.
3145 
3146 NURSE
3147 O Lord, I could have stayed here all the night
3148 To hear good counsel. O, what learning is!--
3149 My lord, I'll tell my lady you will come.
3150 
3151 ROMEO
3152 Do so, and bid my sweet prepare to chide.
3153 
3154 NURSE
3155 Here, sir, a ring she bid me give you, sir.
3156 [Nurse gives Romeo a ring.]
3157 Hie you, make haste, for it grows very late.
3158 [She exits.]
3159 
3160 ROMEO
3161 How well my comfort is revived by this!
3162 
3163 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3164 Go hence, good night--and here stands all your
3165 state:
3166 Either be gone before the watch be set
3167 Or by the break of day disguised from hence.
3168 Sojourn in Mantua. I'll find out your man,
3169 And he shall signify from time to time
3170 Every good hap to you that chances here.
3171 Give me thy hand. 'Tis late. Farewell. Good night.
3172 
3173 ROMEO
3174 But that a joy past joy calls out on me,
3175 It were a grief so brief to part with thee.
3176 Farewell.
3177 [They exit.]
3178 
3179 Scene 4
3180 =======
3181 [Enter old Capulet, his Wife, and Paris.]
3182 
3183 
3184 CAPULET
3185 Things have fallen out, sir, so unluckily
3186 That we have had no time to move our daughter.
3187 Look you, she loved her kinsman Tybalt dearly,
3188 And so did I. Well, we were born to die.
3189 'Tis very late. She'll not come down tonight.
3190 I promise you, but for your company,
3191 I would have been abed an hour ago.
3192 
3193 PARIS
3194 These times of woe afford no times to woo.--
3195 Madam, good night. Commend me to your
3196 daughter.
3197 
3198 LADY CAPULET
3199 I will, and know her mind early tomorrow.
3200 Tonight she's mewed up to her heaviness.
3201 
3202 CAPULET
3203 Sir Paris, I will make a desperate tender
3204 Of my child's love. I think she will be ruled
3205 In all respects by me. Nay, more, I doubt it not.--
3206 Wife, go you to her ere you go to bed.
3207 Acquaint her here of my son Paris' love,
3208 And bid her--mark you me?--on Wednesday
3209 next--
3210 But soft, what day is this?
3211 
3212 PARIS  Monday, my lord.
3213 
3214 CAPULET
3215 Monday, ha ha! Well, Wednesday is too soon.
3216 O' Thursday let it be.--O' Thursday, tell her,
3217 She shall be married to this noble earl.--
3218 Will you be ready? Do you like this haste?
3219 We'll keep no great ado: a friend or two.
3220 For hark you, Tybalt being slain so late,
3221 It may be thought we held him carelessly,
3222 Being our kinsman, if we revel much.
3223 Therefore we'll have some half a dozen friends,
3224 And there an end. But what say you to Thursday?
3225 
3226 PARIS
3227 My lord, I would that Thursday were tomorrow.
3228 
3229 CAPULET
3230 Well, get you gone. O' Thursday be it, then.
3231 [To Lady Capulet.] Go you to Juliet ere you go to bed.
3232 Prepare her, wife, against this wedding day.--
3233 Farewell, my lord.--Light to my chamber, ho!--
3234 Afore me, it is so very late that we
3235 May call it early by and by.--Good night.
3236 [They exit.]
3237 
3238 Scene 5
3239 =======
3240 [Enter Romeo and Juliet aloft.]
3241 
3242 
3243 JULIET
3244 Wilt thou be gone? It is not yet near day.
3245 It was the nightingale, and not the lark,
3246 That pierced the fearful hollow of thine ear.
3247 Nightly she sings on yond pomegranate tree.
3248 Believe me, love, it was the nightingale.
3249 
3250 ROMEO
3251 It was the lark, the herald of the morn,
3252 No nightingale. Look, love, what envious streaks
3253 Do lace the severing clouds in yonder east.
3254 Night's candles are burnt out, and jocund day
3255 Stands tiptoe on the misty mountain-tops.
3256 I must be gone and live, or stay and die.
3257 
3258 JULIET
3259 Yond light is not daylight, I know it, I.
3260 It is some meteor that the sun exhaled
3261 To be to thee this night a torchbearer
3262 And light thee on thy way to Mantua.
3263 Therefore stay yet. Thou need'st not to be gone.
3264 
3265 ROMEO
3266 Let me be ta'en; let me be put to death.
3267 I am content, so thou wilt have it so.
3268 I'll say yon gray is not the morning's eye;
3269 'Tis but the pale reflex of Cynthia's brow.
3270 Nor that is not the lark whose notes do beat
3271 The vaulty heaven so high above our heads.
3272 I have more care to stay than will to go.
3273 Come death and welcome. Juliet wills it so.
3274 How is 't, my soul? Let's talk. It is not day.
3275 
3276 JULIET
3277 It is, it is. Hie hence, begone, away!
3278 It is the lark that sings so out of tune,
3279 Straining harsh discords and unpleasing sharps.
3280 Some say the lark makes sweet division.
3281 This doth not so, for she divideth us.
3282 Some say the lark and loathed toad changed eyes.
3283 O, now I would they had changed voices too,
3284 Since arm from arm that voice doth us affray,
3285 Hunting thee hence with hunt's-up to the day.
3286 O, now begone. More light and light it grows.
3287 
3288 ROMEO
3289 More light and light, more dark and dark our woes.
3290 
3291 [Enter Nurse.]
3292 
3293 
3294 NURSE  Madam.
3295 
3296 JULIET  Nurse?
3297 
3298 NURSE
3299 Your lady mother is coming to your chamber.
3300 The day is broke; be wary; look about.	[She exits.]
3301 
3302 JULIET
3303 Then, window, let day in, and let life out.
3304 
3305 ROMEO
3306 Farewell, farewell. One kiss and I'll descend.
3307 [They kiss, and Romeo descends.]
3308 
3309 JULIET
3310 Art thou gone so? Love, lord, ay husband, friend!
3311 I must hear from thee every day in the hour,
3312 For in a minute there are many days.
3313 O, by this count I shall be much in years
3314 Ere I again behold my Romeo.
3315 
3316 ROMEO  Farewell.
3317 I will omit no opportunity
3318 That may convey my greetings, love, to thee.
3319 
3320 JULIET
3321 O, think'st thou we shall ever meet again?
3322 
3323 ROMEO
3324 I doubt it not; and all these woes shall serve
3325 For sweet discourses in our times to come.
3326 
3327 JULIET
3328 O God, I have an ill-divining soul!
3329 Methinks I see thee, now thou art so low,
3330 As one dead in the bottom of a tomb.
3331 Either my eyesight fails or thou lookest pale.
3332 
3333 ROMEO
3334 And trust me, love, in my eye so do you.
3335 Dry sorrow drinks our blood. Adieu, adieu.	[He exits.]
3336 
3337 JULIET
3338 O Fortune, Fortune, all men call thee fickle.
3339 If thou art fickle, what dost thou with him
3340 That is renowned for faith? Be fickle, Fortune,
3341 For then I hope thou wilt not keep him long,
3342 But send him back.
3343 
3344 [Enter Lady Capulet.]
3345 
3346 
3347 LADY CAPULET  Ho, daughter, are you up?
3348 
3349 JULIET
3350 Who is 't that calls? It is my lady mother.
3351 Is she not down so late or up so early?
3352 What unaccustomed cause procures her hither?
3353 [Juliet descends.]
3354 
3355 LADY CAPULET
3356 Why, how now, Juliet?
3357 
3358 JULIET  Madam, I am not well.
3359 
3360 LADY CAPULET
3361 Evermore weeping for your cousin's death?
3362 What, wilt thou wash him from his grave with tears?
3363 An if thou couldst, thou couldst not make him live.
3364 Therefore have done. Some grief shows much of
3365 love,
3366 But much of grief shows still some want of wit.
3367 
3368 JULIET
3369 Yet let me weep for such a feeling loss.
3370 
3371 LADY CAPULET
3372 So shall you feel the loss, but not the friend
3373 Which you weep for.
3374 
3375 JULIET  Feeling so the loss,
3376 I cannot choose but ever weep the friend.
3377 
3378 LADY CAPULET
3379 Well, girl, thou weep'st not so much for his death
3380 As that the villain lives which slaughtered him.
3381 
3382 JULIET
3383 What villain, madam?
3384 
3385 LADY CAPULET  That same villain, Romeo.
3386 
3387 JULIET, [aside]
3388 Villain and he be many miles asunder.--
3389 God pardon him. I do with all my heart,
3390 And yet no man like he doth grieve my heart.
3391 
3392 LADY CAPULET
3393 That is because the traitor murderer lives.
3394 
3395 JULIET
3396 Ay, madam, from the reach of these my hands.
3397 Would none but I might venge my cousin's death!
3398 
3399 LADY CAPULET
3400 We will have vengeance for it, fear thou not.
3401 Then weep no more. I'll send to one in Mantua,
3402 Where that same banished runagate doth live,
3403 Shall give him such an unaccustomed dram
3404 That he shall soon keep Tybalt company.
3405 And then, I hope, thou wilt be satisfied.
3406 
3407 JULIET
3408 Indeed, I never shall be satisfied
3409 With Romeo till I behold him--dead--
3410 Is my poor heart, so for a kinsman vexed.
3411 Madam, if you could find out but a man
3412 To bear a poison, I would temper it,
3413 That Romeo should, upon receipt thereof,
3414 Soon sleep in quiet. O, how my heart abhors
3415 To hear him named and cannot come to him
3416 To wreak the love I bore my cousin
3417 Upon his body that hath slaughtered him.
3418 
3419 LADY CAPULET
3420 Find thou the means, and I'll find such a man.
3421 But now I'll tell thee joyful tidings, girl.
3422 
3423 JULIET
3424 And joy comes well in such a needy time.
3425 What are they, beseech your Ladyship?
3426 
3427 LADY CAPULET
3428 Well, well, thou hast a careful father, child,
3429 One who, to put thee from thy heaviness,
3430 Hath sorted out a sudden day of joy
3431 That thou expects not, nor I looked not for.
3432 
3433 JULIET
3434 Madam, in happy time! What day is that?
3435 
3436 LADY CAPULET
3437 Marry, my child, early next Thursday morn
3438 The gallant, young, and noble gentleman,
3439 The County Paris, at Saint Peter's Church
3440 Shall happily make thee there a joyful bride.
3441 
3442 JULIET
3443 Now, by Saint Peter's Church, and Peter too,
3444 He shall not make me there a joyful bride!
3445 I wonder at this haste, that I must wed
3446 Ere he that should be husband comes to woo.
3447 I pray you, tell my lord and father, madam,
3448 I will not marry yet, and when I do I swear
3449 It shall be Romeo, whom you know I hate,
3450 Rather than Paris. These are news indeed!
3451 
3452 LADY CAPULET
3453 Here comes your father. Tell him so yourself,
3454 And see how he will take it at your hands.
3455 
3456 [Enter Capulet and Nurse.]
3457 
3458 
3459 CAPULET
3460 When the sun sets, the earth doth drizzle dew,
3461 But for the sunset of my brother's son
3462 It rains downright.
3463 How now, a conduit, girl? What, still in tears?
3464 Evermore show'ring? In one little body
3465 Thou counterfeits a bark, a sea, a wind.
3466 For still thy eyes, which I may call the sea,
3467 Do ebb and flow with tears; the bark thy body is,
3468 Sailing in this salt flood; the winds thy sighs,
3469 Who, raging with thy tears and they with them,
3470 Without a sudden calm, will overset
3471 Thy tempest-tossed body.--How now, wife?
3472 Have you delivered to her our decree?
3473 
3474 LADY CAPULET
3475 Ay, sir, but she will none, she gives you thanks.
3476 I would the fool were married to her grave.
3477 
3478 CAPULET
3479 Soft, take me with you, take me with you, wife.
3480 How, will she none? Doth she not give us thanks?
3481 Is she not proud? Doth she not count her blessed,
3482 Unworthy as she is, that we have wrought
3483 So worthy a gentleman to be her bride?
3484 
3485 JULIET
3486 Not proud you have, but thankful that you have.
3487 Proud can I never be of what I hate,
3488 But thankful even for hate that is meant love.
3489 
3490 CAPULET
3491 How, how, how, how? Chopped logic? What is this?
3492 "Proud," and "I thank you," and "I thank you not,"
3493 And yet "not proud"? Mistress minion you,
3494 Thank me no thankings, nor proud me no prouds,
3495 But fettle your fine joints 'gainst Thursday next
3496 To go with Paris to Saint Peter's Church,
3497 Or I will drag thee on a hurdle thither.
3498 Out, you green-sickness carrion! Out, you baggage!
3499 You tallow face!
3500 
3501 LADY CAPULET  Fie, fie, what, are you mad?
3502 
3503 JULIET, [kneeling]
3504 Good father, I beseech you on my knees,
3505 Hear me with patience but to speak a word.
3506 
3507 CAPULET
3508 Hang thee, young baggage, disobedient wretch!
3509 I tell thee what: get thee to church o' Thursday,
3510 Or never after look me in the face.
3511 Speak not; reply not; do not answer me.
3512 My fingers itch.--Wife, we scarce thought us
3513 blessed
3514 That God had lent us but this only child,
3515 But now I see this one is one too much,
3516 And that we have a curse in having her.
3517 Out on her, hilding.
3518 
3519 NURSE  God in heaven bless her!
3520 You are to blame, my lord, to rate her so.
3521 
3522 CAPULET
3523 And why, my Lady Wisdom? Hold your tongue.
3524 Good Prudence, smatter with your gossips, go.
3525 
3526 NURSE
3527 I speak no treason.
3528 
3529 CAPULET  O, God 'i' g' eden!
3530 
3531 NURSE
3532 May not one speak?
3533 
3534 CAPULET  Peace, you mumbling fool!
3535 Utter your gravity o'er a gossip's bowl,
3536 For here we need it not.
3537 
3538 LADY CAPULET  You are too hot.
3539 
3540 CAPULET  God's bread, it makes me mad.
3541 Day, night, hour, tide, time, work, play,
3542 Alone, in company, still my care hath been
3543 To have her matched. And having now provided
3544 A gentleman of noble parentage,
3545 Of fair demesnes, youthful, and nobly ligned,
3546 Stuffed, as they say, with honorable parts,
3547 Proportioned as one's thought would wish a man--
3548 And then to have a wretched puling fool,
3549 A whining mammet, in her fortune's tender,
3550 To answer "I'll not wed. I cannot love.
3551 I am too young. I pray you, pardon me."
3552 But, an you will not wed, I'll pardon you!
3553 Graze where you will, you shall not house with me.
3554 Look to 't; think on 't. I do not use to jest.
3555 Thursday is near. Lay hand on heart; advise.
3556 An you be mine, I'll give you to my friend.
3557 An you be not, hang, beg, starve, die in the streets,
3558 For, by my soul, I'll ne'er acknowledge thee,
3559 Nor what is mine shall never do thee good.
3560 Trust to 't; bethink you. I'll not be forsworn.
3561 [He exits.]
3562 
3563 JULIET
3564 Is there no pity sitting in the clouds
3565 That sees into the bottom of my grief?--
3566 O sweet my mother, cast me not away.
3567 Delay this marriage for a month, a week,
3568 Or, if you do not, make the bridal bed
3569 In that dim monument where Tybalt lies.
3570 
3571 LADY CAPULET
3572 Talk not to me, for I'll not speak a word.
3573 Do as thou wilt, for I have done with thee.
3574 [She exits.]
3575 
3576 JULIET, [rising]
3577 O God! O nurse, how shall this be prevented?
3578 My husband is on Earth, my faith in heaven.
3579 How shall that faith return again to Earth
3580 Unless that husband send it me from heaven
3581 By leaving Earth? Comfort me; counsel me.--
3582 Alack, alack, that heaven should practice stratagems
3583 Upon so soft a subject as myself.--
3584 What sayst thou? Hast thou not a word of joy?
3585 Some comfort, nurse.
3586 
3587 NURSE  Faith, here it is.
3588 Romeo is banished, and all the world to nothing
3589 That he dares ne'er come back to challenge you,
3590 Or, if he do, it needs must be by stealth.
3591 Then, since the case so stands as now it doth,
3592 I think it best you married with the County.
3593 O, he's a lovely gentleman!
3594 Romeo's a dishclout to him. An eagle, madam,
3595 Hath not so green, so quick, so fair an eye
3596 As Paris hath. Beshrew my very heart,
3597 I think you are happy in this second match,
3598 For it excels your first, or, if it did not,
3599 Your first is dead, or 'twere as good he were
3600 As living here and you no use of him.
3601 
3602 JULIET
3603 Speak'st thou from thy heart?
3604 
3605 NURSE
3606 And from my soul too, else beshrew them both.
3607 
3608 JULIET  Amen.
3609 
3610 NURSE  What?
3611 
3612 JULIET
3613 Well, thou hast comforted me marvelous much.
3614 Go in and tell my lady I am gone,
3615 Having displeased my father, to Lawrence' cell
3616 To make confession and to be absolved.
3617 
3618 NURSE
3619 Marry, I will; and this is wisely done.	[She exits.]
3620 
3621 JULIET
3622 Ancient damnation, O most wicked fiend!
3623 Is it more sin to wish me thus forsworn
3624 Or to dispraise my lord with that same tongue
3625 Which she hath praised him with above compare
3626 So many thousand times? Go, counselor.
3627 Thou and my bosom henceforth shall be twain.
3628 I'll to the Friar to know his remedy.
3629 If all else fail, myself have power to die.
3630 [She exits.]
3631 
3632 
3633 ACT 4
3634 =====
3635 
3636 Scene 1
3637 =======
3638 [Enter Friar Lawrence and County Paris.]
3639 
3640 
3641 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3642 On Thursday, sir? The time is very short.
3643 
3644 PARIS
3645 My father Capulet will have it so,
3646 And I am nothing slow to slack his haste.
3647 
3648 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3649 You say you do not know the lady's mind?
3650 Uneven is the course. I like it not.
3651 
3652 PARIS
3653 Immoderately she weeps for Tybalt's death,
3654 And therefore have I little talk of love,
3655 For Venus smiles not in a house of tears.
3656 Now, sir, her father counts it dangerous
3657 That she do give her sorrow so much sway,
3658 And in his wisdom hastes our marriage
3659 To stop the inundation of her tears,
3660 Which, too much minded by herself alone,
3661 May be put from her by society.
3662 Now do you know the reason of this haste.
3663 
3664 FRIAR LAWRENCE, [aside]
3665 I would I knew not why it should be slowed.--
3666 Look, sir, here comes the lady toward my cell.
3667 
3668 [Enter Juliet.]
3669 
3670 
3671 PARIS
3672 Happily met, my lady and my wife.
3673 
3674 JULIET
3675 That may be, sir, when I may be a wife.
3676 
3677 PARIS
3678 That "may be" must be, love, on Thursday next.
3679 
3680 JULIET
3681 What must be shall be.
3682 
3683 FRIAR LAWRENCE  That's a certain text.
3684 
3685 PARIS
3686 Come you to make confession to this father?
3687 
3688 JULIET
3689 To answer that, I should confess to you.
3690 
3691 PARIS
3692 Do not deny to him that you love me.
3693 
3694 JULIET
3695 I will confess to you that I love him.
3696 
3697 PARIS
3698 So will you, I am sure, that you love me.
3699 
3700 JULIET
3701 If I do so, it will be of more price
3702 Being spoke behind your back than to your face.
3703 
3704 PARIS
3705 Poor soul, thy face is much abused with tears.
3706 
3707 JULIET
3708 The tears have got small victory by that,
3709 For it was bad enough before their spite.
3710 
3711 PARIS
3712 Thou wrong'st it more than tears with that report.
3713 
3714 JULIET
3715 That is no slander, sir, which is a truth,
3716 And what I spake, I spake it to my face.
3717 
3718 PARIS
3719 Thy face is mine, and thou hast slandered it.
3720 
3721 JULIET
3722 It may be so, for it is not mine own.--
3723 Are you at leisure, holy father, now,
3724 Or shall I come to you at evening Mass?
3725 
3726 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3727 My leisure serves me, pensive daughter, now.--
3728 My lord, we must entreat the time alone.
3729 
3730 PARIS
3731 God shield I should disturb devotion!--
3732 Juliet, on Thursday early will I rouse you.
3733 Till then, adieu, and keep this holy kiss.	[He exits.]
3734 
3735 JULIET
