3736 O, shut the door, and when thou hast done so,
3737 Come weep with me, past hope, past care, past help.
3738 
3739 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3740 O Juliet, I already know thy grief.
3741 It strains me past the compass of my wits.
3742 I hear thou must, and nothing may prorogue it,
3743 On Thursday next be married to this County.
3744 
3745 JULIET
3746 Tell me not, friar, that thou hearest of this,
3747 Unless thou tell me how I may prevent it.
3748 If in thy wisdom thou canst give no help,
3749 Do thou but call my resolution wise,
3750 And with this knife I'll help it presently.
3751 [She shows him her knife.]
3752 God joined my heart and Romeo's, thou our hands;
3753 And ere this hand, by thee to Romeo's sealed,
3754 Shall be the label to another deed,
3755 Or my true heart with treacherous revolt
3756 Turn to another, this shall slay them both.
3757 Therefore out of thy long-experienced time
3758 Give me some present counsel, or, behold,
3759 'Twixt my extremes and me this bloody knife
3760 Shall play the umpire, arbitrating that
3761 Which the commission of thy years and art
3762 Could to no issue of true honor bring.
3763 Be not so long to speak. I long to die
3764 If what thou speak'st speak not of remedy.
3765 
3766 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3767 Hold, daughter, I do spy a kind of hope,
3768 Which craves as desperate an execution
3769 As that is desperate which we would prevent.
3770 If, rather than to marry County Paris,
3771 Thou hast the strength of will to slay thyself,
3772 Then is it likely thou wilt undertake
3773 A thing like death to chide away this shame,
3774 That cop'st with death himself to 'scape from it;
3775 And if thou darest, I'll give thee remedy.
3776 
3777 JULIET
3778 O, bid me leap, rather than marry Paris,
3779 From off the battlements of any tower,
3780 Or walk in thievish ways, or bid me lurk
3781 Where serpents are. Chain me with roaring bears,
3782 Or hide me nightly in a charnel house,
3783 O'ercovered quite with dead men's rattling bones,
3784 With reeky shanks and yellow chapless skulls.
3785 Or bid me go into a new-made grave
3786 And hide me with a dead man in his shroud
3787 (Things that to hear them told have made me
3788 tremble),
3789 And I will do it without fear or doubt,
3790 To live an unstained wife to my sweet love.
3791 
3792 FRIAR LAWRENCE
3793 Hold, then. Go home; be merry; give consent
3794 To marry Paris. Wednesday is tomorrow.
3795 Tomorrow night look that thou lie alone;
3796 Let not the Nurse lie with thee in thy chamber.
3797 [Holding out a vial.]
3798 Take thou this vial, being then in bed,
3799 And this distilling liquor drink thou off;
3800 When presently through all thy veins shall run
3801 A cold and drowsy humor; for no pulse
3802 Shall keep his native progress, but surcease.
3803 No warmth, no breath shall testify thou livest.
3804 The roses in thy lips and cheeks shall fade
3805 To paly ashes, thy eyes' windows fall
3806 Like death when he shuts up the day of life.
3807 Each part, deprived of supple government,
3808 Shall, stiff and stark and cold, appear like death,
3809 And in this borrowed likeness of shrunk death
3810 Thou shalt continue two and forty hours
3811 And then awake as from a pleasant sleep.
3812 Now, when the bridegroom in the morning comes
3813 To rouse thee from thy bed, there art thou dead.
3814 Then, as the manner of our country is,
3815 In thy best robes uncovered on the bier
3816 Thou shalt be borne to that same ancient vault
3817 Where all the kindred of the Capulets lie.
3818 In the meantime, against thou shalt awake,
3819 Shall Romeo by my letters know our drift,
3820 And hither shall he come, and he and I
3821 Will watch thy waking, and that very night
3822 Shall Romeo bear thee hence to Mantua.
3823 And this shall free thee from this present shame,
3824 If no inconstant toy nor womanish fear
3825 Abate thy valor in the acting it.
3826 
3827 JULIET
3828 Give me, give me! O, tell not me of fear!
3829 
3830 FRIAR LAWRENCE, [giving Juliet the vial]
3831 Hold, get you gone. Be strong and prosperous
3832 In this resolve. I'll send a friar with speed
3833 To Mantua with my letters to thy lord.
3834 
3835 JULIET
3836 Love give me strength, and strength shall help
3837 afford.
3838 Farewell, dear father.
3839 [They exit in different directions.]
3840 
3841 Scene 2
3842 =======
3843 [Enter Father Capulet, Mother, Nurse, and Servingmen,
3844 two or three.]
3845 
3846 
3847 CAPULET
3848 So many guests invite as here are writ.
3849 [One or two of the Servingmen exit
3850 with Capulet's list.]
3851 Sirrah, go hire me twenty cunning cooks.
3852 
3853 SERVINGMAN  You shall have none ill, sir, for I'll try if
3854 they can lick their fingers.
3855 
3856 CAPULET  How canst thou try them so?
3857 
3858 SERVINGMAN  Marry, sir, 'tis an ill cook that cannot lick
3859 his own fingers. Therefore he that cannot lick his
3860 fingers goes not with me.
3861 
3862 CAPULET  Go, begone.	[Servingman exits.]
3863 We shall be much unfurnished for this time.--
3864 What, is my daughter gone to Friar Lawrence?
3865 
3866 NURSE  Ay, forsooth.
3867 
3868 CAPULET
3869 Well, he may chance to do some good on her.
3870 A peevish self-willed harlotry it is.
3871 
3872 [Enter Juliet.]
3873 
3874 
3875 NURSE
3876 See where she comes from shrift with merry look.
3877 
3878 CAPULET
3879 How now, my headstrong, where have you been
3880 gadding?
3881 
3882 JULIET
3883 Where I have learned me to repent the sin
3884 Of disobedient opposition
3885 To you and your behests, and am enjoined
3886 By holy Lawrence to fall prostrate here	[Kneeling.]
3887 To beg your pardon. Pardon, I beseech you.
3888 Henceforward I am ever ruled by you.
3889 
3890 CAPULET
3891 Send for the County. Go tell him of this.
3892 I'll have this knot knit up tomorrow morning.
3893 
3894 JULIET
3895 I met the youthful lord at Lawrence' cell
3896 And gave him what becomed love I might,
3897 Not stepping o'er the bounds of modesty.
3898 
3899 CAPULET
3900 Why, I am glad on 't. This is well. Stand up.
3901 [Juliet rises.]
3902 This is as 't should be.--Let me see the County.
3903 Ay, marry, go, I say, and fetch him hither.--
3904 Now, afore God, this reverend holy friar,
3905 All our whole city is much bound to him.
3906 
3907 JULIET
3908 Nurse, will you go with me into my closet
3909 To help me sort such needful ornaments
3910 As you think fit to furnish me tomorrow?
3911 
3912 LADY CAPULET
3913 No, not till Thursday. There is time enough.
3914 
3915 CAPULET
3916 Go, nurse. Go with her. We'll to church tomorrow.
3917 [Juliet and the Nurse exit.]
3918 
3919 LADY CAPULET
3920 We shall be short in our provision.
3921 'Tis now near night.
3922 
3923 CAPULET  Tush, I will stir about,
3924 And all things shall be well, I warrant thee, wife.
3925 Go thou to Juliet. Help to deck up her.
3926 I'll not to bed tonight. Let me alone.
3927 I'll play the housewife for this once.--What ho!--
3928 They are all forth. Well, I will walk myself
3929 To County Paris, to prepare up him
3930 Against tomorrow. My heart is wondrous light
3931 Since this same wayward girl is so reclaimed.
3932 [They exit.]
3933 
3934 Scene 3
3935 =======
3936 [Enter Juliet and Nurse.]
3937 
3938 
3939 JULIET
3940 Ay, those attires are best. But, gentle nurse,
3941 I pray thee leave me to myself tonight,
3942 For I have need of many orisons
3943 To move the heavens to smile upon my state,
3944 Which, well thou knowest, is cross and full of sin.
3945 
3946 [Enter Lady Capulet.]
3947 
3948 
3949 LADY CAPULET
3950 What, are you busy, ho? Need you my help?
3951 
3952 JULIET
3953 No, madam, we have culled such necessaries
3954 As are behooveful for our state tomorrow.
3955 So please you, let me now be left alone,
3956 And let the Nurse this night sit up with you,
3957 For I am sure you have your hands full all
3958 In this so sudden business.
3959 
3960 LADY CAPULET  Good night.
3961 Get thee to bed and rest, for thou hast need.
3962 [Lady Capulet and the Nurse exit.]
3963 
3964 JULIET
3965 Farewell.--God knows when we shall meet again.
3966 I have a faint cold fear thrills through my veins
3967 That almost freezes up the heat of life.
3968 I'll call them back again to comfort me.--
3969 Nurse!--What should she do here?
3970 My dismal scene I needs must act alone.
3971 Come, vial.	[She takes out the vial.]
3972 What if this mixture do not work at all?
3973 Shall I be married then tomorrow morning?
3974 [She takes out her knife
3975 and puts it down beside her.]
3976 No, no, this shall forbid it. Lie thou there.
3977 What if it be a poison which the Friar
3978 Subtly hath ministered to have me dead,
3979 Lest in this marriage he should be dishonored
3980 Because he married me before to Romeo?
3981 I fear it is. And yet methinks it should not,
3982 For he hath still been tried a holy man.
3983 How if, when I am laid into the tomb,
3984 I wake before the time that Romeo
3985 Come to redeem me? There's a fearful point.
3986 Shall I not then be stifled in the vault,
3987 To whose foul mouth no healthsome air breathes in,
3988 And there die strangled ere my Romeo comes?
3989 Or, if I live, is it not very like
3990 The horrible conceit of death and night,
3991 Together with the terror of the place--
3992 As in a vault, an ancient receptacle
3993 Where for this many hundred years the bones
3994 Of all my buried ancestors are packed;
3995 Where bloody Tybalt, yet but green in earth,
3996 Lies fest'ring in his shroud; where, as they say,
3997 At some hours in the night spirits resort--
3998 Alack, alack, is it not like that I,
3999 So early waking, what with loathsome smells,
4000 And shrieks like mandrakes torn out of the earth,
4001 That living mortals, hearing them, run mad--
4002 O, if I wake, shall I not be distraught,
4003 Environed with all these hideous fears,
4004 And madly play with my forefathers' joints,
4005 And pluck the mangled Tybalt from his shroud,
4006 And, in this rage, with some great kinsman's bone,
4007 As with a club, dash out my desp'rate brains?
4008 O look, methinks I see my cousin's ghost
4009 Seeking out Romeo that did spit his body
4010 Upon a rapier's point! Stay, Tybalt, stay!
4011 Romeo, Romeo, Romeo! Here's drink. I drink to
4012 thee.	[She drinks and falls upon her bed
4013 within the curtains.]
4014 
4015 Scene 4
4016 =======
4017 [Enter Lady Capulet and Nurse.]
4018 
4019 
4020 LADY CAPULET
4021 Hold, take these keys, and fetch more spices, nurse.
4022 
4023 NURSE
4024 They call for dates and quinces in the pastry.
4025 
4026 [Enter old Capulet.]
4027 
4028 
4029 CAPULET
4030 Come, stir, stir, stir! The second cock hath crowed.
4031 The curfew bell hath rung. 'Tis three o'clock.--
4032 Look to the baked meats, good Angelica.
4033 Spare not for cost.
4034 
4035 NURSE  Go, you cot-quean, go,
4036 Get you to bed. Faith, you'll be sick tomorrow
4037 For this night's watching.
4038 
4039 CAPULET
4040 No, not a whit. What, I have watched ere now
4041 All night for lesser cause, and ne'er been sick.
4042 
4043 LADY CAPULET
4044 Ay, you have been a mouse-hunt in your time,
4045 But I will watch you from such watching now.
4046 [Lady Capulet and Nurse exit.]
4047 
4048 CAPULET
4049 A jealous hood, a jealous hood!
4050 
4051 [Enter three or four Servingmen with spits and logs
4052 and baskets.]
4053 
4054 Now fellow,
4055 What is there?
4056 
4057 FIRST SERVINGMAN
4058 Things for the cook, sir, but I know not what.
4059 
4060 CAPULET
4061 Make haste, make haste.	[First Servingman exits.]
4062 Sirrah, fetch drier logs.
4063 Call Peter. He will show thee where they are.
4064 
4065 SECOND SERVINGMAN
4066 I have a head, sir, that will find out logs
4067 And never trouble Peter for the matter.
4068 
4069 CAPULET
4070 Mass, and well said. A merry whoreson, ha!
4071 Thou shalt be loggerhead.
4072 [Second Servingman exits.]
4073 Good faith, 'tis day.
4074 The County will be here with music straight,
4075 [Play music.]
4076 For so he said he would. I hear him near.--
4077 Nurse!--Wife! What ho!--What, nurse, I say!
4078 
4079 [Enter Nurse.]
4080 
4081 Go waken Juliet. Go and trim her up.
4082 I'll go and chat with Paris. Hie, make haste,
4083 Make haste. The bridegroom he is come already.
4084 Make haste, I say.
4085 [He exits.]
4086 
4087 Scene 5
4088 =======
4089 
4090 NURSE, [approaching the bed]
4091 Mistress! What, mistress! Juliet!--Fast, I warrant
4092 her, she--
4093 Why, lamb, why, lady! Fie, you slugabed!
4094 Why, love, I say! Madam! Sweetheart! Why, bride!--
4095 What, not a word?--You take your pennyworths
4096 now.
4097 Sleep for a week, for the next night, I warrant,
4098 The County Paris hath set up his rest
4099 That you shall rest but little.--God forgive me,
4100 Marry, and amen! How sound is she asleep!
4101 I needs must wake her.--Madam, madam, madam!
4102 Ay, let the County take you in your bed,
4103 He'll fright you up, i' faith.--Will it not be?
4104 [She opens the bed's curtains.]
4105 What, dressed, and in your clothes, and down
4106 again?
4107 I must needs wake you. Lady, lady, lady!--
4108 Alas, alas! Help, help! My lady's dead.--
4109 O, weraday, that ever I was born!--
4110 Some aqua vitae, ho!--My lord! My lady!
4111 
4112 [Enter Lady Capulet.]
4113 
4114 
4115 LADY CAPULET
4116 What noise is here?
4117 
4118 NURSE  O lamentable day!
4119 
4120 LADY CAPULET
4121 What is the matter?
4122 
4123 NURSE  Look, look!--O heavy day!
4124 
4125 LADY CAPULET
4126 O me! O me! My child, my only life,
4127 Revive, look up, or I will die with thee.
4128 Help, help! Call help.
4129 
4130 [Enter Capulet.]
4131 
4132 
4133 CAPULET
4134 For shame, bring Juliet forth. Her lord is come.
4135 
4136 NURSE
4137 She's dead, deceased. She's dead, alack the day!
4138 
4139 LADY CAPULET
4140 Alack the day, she's dead, she's dead, she's dead.
4141 
4142 CAPULET
4143 Ha, let me see her! Out, alas, she's cold.
4144 Her blood is settled, and her joints are stiff.
4145 Life and these lips have long been separated.
4146 Death lies on her like an untimely frost
4147 Upon the sweetest flower of all the field.
4148 
4149 NURSE
4150 O lamentable day!
4151 
4152 LADY CAPULET  O woeful time!
4153 
4154 CAPULET
4155 Death, that hath ta'en her hence to make me wail,
4156 Ties up my tongue and will not let me speak.
4157 
4158 [Enter Friar Lawrence and the County Paris, with
4159 Musicians.]
4160 
4161 
4162 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4163 Come, is the bride ready to go to church?
4164 
4165 CAPULET
4166 Ready to go, but never to return.--
4167 O son, the night before thy wedding day
4168 Hath Death lain with thy wife. There she lies,
4169 Flower as she was, deflowered by him.
4170 Death is my son-in-law; Death is my heir.
4171 My daughter he hath wedded. I will die
4172 And leave him all. Life, living, all is Death's.
4173 
4174 PARIS
4175 Have I thought long to see this morning's face,
4176 And doth it give me such a sight as this?
4177 
4178 LADY CAPULET
4179 Accursed, unhappy, wretched, hateful day!
4180 Most miserable hour that e'er time saw
4181 In lasting labor of his pilgrimage!
4182 But one, poor one, one poor and loving child,
4183 But one thing to rejoice and solace in,
4184 And cruel death hath catched it from my sight!
4185 
4186 NURSE
4187 O woe, O woeful, woeful, woeful day!
4188 Most lamentable day, most woeful day
4189 That ever, ever I did yet behold!
4190 O day, O day, O day, O hateful day!
4191 Never was seen so black a day as this!
4192 O woeful day, O woeful day!
4193 
4194 PARIS
4195 Beguiled, divorced, wronged, spited, slain!
4196 Most detestable death, by thee beguiled,
4197 By cruel, cruel thee quite overthrown!
4198 O love! O life! Not life, but love in death!
4199 
4200 CAPULET
4201 Despised, distressed, hated, martyred, killed!
4202 Uncomfortable time, why cam'st thou now
4203 To murder, murder our solemnity?
4204 O child! O child! My soul and not my child!
4205 Dead art thou! Alack, my child is dead,
4206 And with my child my joys are buried.
4207 
4208 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4209 Peace, ho, for shame! Confusion's cure lives not
4210 In these confusions. Heaven and yourself
4211 Had part in this fair maid. Now heaven hath all,
4212 And all the better is it for the maid.
4213 Your part in her you could not keep from death,
4214 But heaven keeps his part in eternal life.
4215 The most you sought was her promotion,
4216 For 'twas your heaven she should be advanced;
4217 And weep you now, seeing she is advanced
4218 Above the clouds, as high as heaven itself?
4219 O, in this love you love your child so ill
4220 That you run mad, seeing that she is well.
4221 She's not well married that lives married long,
4222 But she's best married that dies married young.
4223 Dry up your tears, and stick your rosemary
4224 On this fair corse, and, as the custom is,
4225 And in her best array, bear her to church,
4226 For though fond nature bids us all lament,
4227 Yet nature's tears are reason's merriment.
4228 
4229 CAPULET
4230 All things that we ordained festival
4231 Turn from their office to black funeral:
4232 Our instruments to melancholy bells,
4233 Our wedding cheer to a sad burial feast,
4234 Our solemn hymns to sullen dirges change,
4235 Our bridal flowers serve for a buried corse,
4236 And all things change them to the contrary.
4237 
4238 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4239 Sir, go you in, and, madam, go with him,
4240 And go, Sir Paris. Everyone prepare
4241 To follow this fair corse unto her grave.
4242 The heavens do lour upon you for some ill.
4243 Move them no more by crossing their high will.
4244 [All but the Nurse and the Musicians exit.]
4245 
4246 FIRST MUSICIAN
4247 Faith, we may put up our pipes and be gone.
4248 
4249 NURSE
4250 Honest good fellows, ah, put up, put up,
4251 For, well you know, this is a pitiful case.
4252 
4253 FIRST MUSICIAN
4254 Ay, by my troth, the case may be amended.
4255 [Nurse exits.]
4256 
4257 [Enter Peter.]
4258 
4259 
4260 PETER  Musicians, O musicians, "Heart's ease,"
4261 "Heart's ease." O, an you will have me live, play
4262 "Heart's ease."
4263 
4264 FIRST MUSICIAN  Why "Heart's ease?"
4265 
4266 PETER  O musicians, because my heart itself plays "My
4267 heart is full." O, play me some merry dump to
4268 comfort me.
4269 
4270 FIRST MUSICIAN  Not a dump, we. 'Tis no time to play
4271 now.
4272 
4273 PETER  You will not then?
4274 
4275 FIRST MUSICIAN  No.
4276 
4277 PETER  I will then give it you soundly.
4278 
4279 FIRST MUSICIAN  What will you give us?
4280 
4281 PETER  No money, on my faith, but the gleek. I will give
4282 you the minstrel.
4283 
4284 FIRST MUSICIAN  Then will I give you the
4285 serving-creature.
4286 
4287 PETER  Then will I lay the serving-creature's dagger on
4288 your pate. I will carry no crochets. I'll re you, I'll fa
4289 you. Do you note me?
4290 
4291 FIRST MUSICIAN  An you re us and fa us, you note us.
4292 
4293 SECOND MUSICIAN  Pray you, put up your dagger and
4294 put out your wit.
4295 
4296 PETER  Then have at you with my wit. I will dry-beat
4297 you with an iron wit, and put up my iron dagger.
4298 Answer me like men.
4299 [Sings.]	When griping griefs the heart doth wound
4300 	And doleful dumps the mind oppress,
4301 	Then music with her silver sound--
4302 Why "silver sound"? Why "music with her silver
4303 sound"? What say you, Simon Catling?
4304 
4305 FIRST MUSICIAN  Marry, sir, because silver hath a
4306 sweet sound.
4307 
4308 PETER  Prates.--What say you, Hugh Rebeck?
4309 
4310 SECOND MUSICIAN  I say "silver sound" because musicians
4311 sound for silver.
4312 
4313 PETER  Prates too.--What say you, James Soundpost?
4314 
4315 THIRD MUSICIAN  Faith, I know not what to say.
4316 
4317 PETER  O, I cry you mercy. You are the singer. I will say
4318 for you. It is "music with her silver sound" because
4319 musicians have no gold for sounding:
4320 [Sings.]	Then music with her silver sound
4321 	With speedy help doth lend redress.
4322 [He exits.]
4323 
4324 FIRST MUSICIAN  What a pestilent knave is this same!
4325 
4326 SECOND MUSICIAN  Hang him, Jack. Come, we'll in
4327 here, tarry for the mourners, and stay dinner.
4328 [They exit.]
4329

4331 ACT 5
4332 =====
4333 
4334 Scene 1
4335 =======
4336 [Enter Romeo.]
4337 
4338 
4339 ROMEO
4340 If I may trust the flattering truth of sleep,
4341 My dreams presage some joyful news at hand.
4342 My bosom's lord sits lightly in his throne,
4343 And all this day an unaccustomed spirit
4344 Lifts me above the ground with cheerful thoughts.
4345 I dreamt my lady came and found me dead
4346 (Strange dream that gives a dead man leave to
4347 think!)
4348 And breathed such life with kisses in my lips
4349 That I revived and was an emperor.
4350 Ah me, how sweet is love itself possessed
4351 When but love's shadows are so rich in joy!
4352 
4353 [Enter Romeo's man Balthasar, in riding boots.]
4354 
4355 News from Verona!--How now, Balthasar?
4356 Dost thou not bring me letters from the Friar?
4357 How doth my lady? Is my father well?
4358 How doth my Juliet? That I ask again,
4359 For nothing can be ill if she be well.
4360 
4361 BALTHASAR
4362 Then she is well and nothing can be ill.
4363 Her body sleeps in Capels' monument,
4364 And her immortal part with angels lives.
4365 I saw her laid low in her kindred's vault
4366 And presently took post to tell it you.
4367 O, pardon me for bringing these ill news,
4368 Since you did leave it for my office, sir.
4369 
4370 ROMEO
4371 Is it e'en so?--Then I deny you, stars!--
4372 Thou knowest my lodging. Get me ink and paper,
4373 And hire post-horses. I will hence tonight.
4374 
4375 BALTHASAR
4376 I do beseech you, sir, have patience.
4377 Your looks are pale and wild and do import
4378 Some misadventure.
4379 
4380 ROMEO  Tush, thou art deceived.
4381 Leave me, and do the thing I bid thee do.
4382 Hast thou no letters to me from the Friar?
4383 
4384 BALTHASAR
4385 No, my good lord.
4386 
4387 ROMEO  No matter. Get thee gone,
4388 And hire those horses. I'll be with thee straight.
4389 [Balthasar exits.]
4390 Well, Juliet, I will lie with thee tonight.
4391 Let's see for means. O mischief, thou art swift
4392 To enter in the thoughts of desperate men.
4393 I do remember an apothecary
4394 (And hereabouts he dwells) which late I noted
4395 In tattered weeds, with overwhelming brows,
4396 Culling of simples. Meager were his looks.
4397 Sharp misery had worn him to the bones.
4398 And in his needy shop a tortoise hung,
4399 An alligator stuffed, and other skins
4400 Of ill-shaped fishes; and about his shelves,
4401 A beggarly account of empty boxes,
4402 Green earthen pots, bladders, and musty seeds,
4403 Remnants of packthread, and old cakes of roses
4404 Were thinly scattered to make up a show.
4405 Noting this penury, to myself I said
4406 "An if a man did need a poison now,
4407 Whose sale is present death in Mantua,
4408 Here lives a caitiff wretch would sell it him."
4409 O, this same thought did but forerun my need,
4410 And this same needy man must sell it me.
4411 As I remember, this should be the house.
4412 Being holiday, the beggar's shop is shut.--
4413 What ho, Apothecary!
4414 
4415 [Enter Apothecary.]
4416 
4417 
4418 APOTHECARY  Who calls so loud?
4419 
4420 ROMEO
4421 Come hither, man. I see that thou art poor.
4422 [He offers money.]
4423 Hold, there is forty ducats. Let me have
4424 A dram of poison, such soon-speeding gear
4425 As will disperse itself through all the veins,
4426 That the life-weary taker may fall dead,
4427 And that the trunk may be discharged of breath
4428 As violently as hasty powder fired
4429 Doth hurry from the fatal cannon's womb.
4430 
4431 APOTHECARY
4432 Such mortal drugs I have, but Mantua's law
4433 Is death to any he that utters them.
4434 
4435 ROMEO
4436 Art thou so bare and full of wretchedness,
4437 And fearest to die? Famine is in thy cheeks,
4438 Need and oppression starveth in thy eyes,
4439 Contempt and beggary hangs upon thy back.
4440 The world is not thy friend, nor the world's law.
4441 The world affords no law to make thee rich.
4442 Then be not poor, but break it, and take this.
4443 
4444 APOTHECARY
4445 My poverty, but not my will, consents.
4446 
4447 ROMEO
4448 I pay thy poverty and not thy will.
4449 
4450 APOTHECARY, [giving him the poison]
4451 Put this in any liquid thing you will
4452 And drink it off, and if you had the strength
4453 Of twenty men, it would dispatch you straight.
4454 
4455 ROMEO, [handing him the money]
4456 There is thy gold, worse poison to men's souls,
4457 Doing more murder in this loathsome world
4458 Than these poor compounds that thou mayst not
4459 sell.
4460 I sell thee poison; thou hast sold me none.
4461 Farewell, buy food, and get thyself in flesh.
4462 [Apothecary exits.]
4463 Come, cordial and not poison, go with me
4464 To Juliet's grave, for there must I use thee.
4465 [He exits.]
4466 
4467 Scene 2
4468 =======
4469 [Enter Friar John.]
4470 
4471 
4472 FRIAR JOHN
4473 Holy Franciscan friar, brother, ho!
4474 
4475 [Enter Friar Lawrence.]
4476 
4477 
4478 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4479 This same should be the voice of Friar John.--
4480 Welcome from Mantua. What says Romeo?
4481 Or, if his mind be writ, give me his letter.
4482 
4483 FRIAR JOHN
4484 Going to find a barefoot brother out,
4485 One of our order, to associate me,
4486 Here in this city visiting the sick,
4487 And finding him, the searchers of the town,
4488 Suspecting that we both were in a house
4489 Where the infectious pestilence did reign,
4490 Sealed up the doors and would not let us forth,
4491 So that my speed to Mantua there was stayed.
4492 
4493 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4494 Who bare my letter, then, to Romeo?
4495 
4496 FRIAR JOHN
4497 I could not send it--here it is again--
4498 [Returning the letter.]
4499 Nor get a messenger to bring it thee,
4500 So fearful were they of infection.
4501 
4502 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4503 Unhappy fortune! By my brotherhood,
4504 The letter was not nice but full of charge,
4505 Of dear import, and the neglecting it
4506 May do much danger. Friar John, go hence.
4507 Get me an iron crow and bring it straight
4508 Unto my cell.
4509 
4510 FRIAR JOHN
4511 Brother, I'll go and bring it thee.	[He exits.]
4512 
4513 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4514 Now must I to the monument alone.
4515 Within this three hours will fair Juliet wake.
4516 She will beshrew me much that Romeo
4517 Hath had no notice of these accidents.
4518 But I will write again to Mantua,
4519 And keep her at my cell till Romeo come.
4520 Poor living corse, closed in a dead man's tomb!
4521 [He exits.]
4522 
4523 Scene 3
4524 =======
4525 [Enter Paris and his Page.]
4526 
4527 
4528 PARIS
4529 Give me thy torch, boy. Hence and stand aloof.
4530 Yet put it out, for I would not be seen.
4531 Under yond yew trees lay thee all along,
4532 Holding thy ear close to the hollow ground.
4533 So shall no foot upon the churchyard tread
4534 (Being loose, unfirm, with digging up of graves)
4535 But thou shalt hear it. Whistle then to me
4536 As signal that thou hearest something approach.
4537 Give me those flowers. Do as I bid thee. Go.
4538 
4539 PAGE, [aside]
4540 I am almost afraid to stand alone
4541 Here in the churchyard. Yet I will adventure.
4542 [He moves away from Paris.]
4543 
4544 PARIS, [scattering flowers]
4545 Sweet flower, with flowers thy bridal bed I strew
4546 (O woe, thy canopy is dust and stones!)
4547 Which with sweet water nightly I will dew,
4548 Or, wanting that, with tears distilled by moans.
4549 The obsequies that I for thee will keep
4550 Nightly shall be to strew thy grave and weep.
4551 [Page whistles.]
4552 The boy gives warning something doth approach.
4553 What cursed foot wanders this way tonight,
4554 To cross my obsequies and true love's rite?
4555 What, with a torch? Muffle me, night, awhile.
4556 [He steps aside.]
4557 
4558 [Enter Romeo and Balthasar.]
4559 
4560 
4561 ROMEO
4562 Give me that mattock and the wrenching iron.
4563 Hold, take this letter. Early in the morning
4564 See thou deliver it to my lord and father.
4565 Give me the light. Upon thy life I charge thee,
4566 Whate'er thou hearest or seest, stand all aloof
4567 And do not interrupt me in my course.
4568 Why I descend into this bed of death
4569 Is partly to behold my lady's face,
4570 But chiefly to take thence from her dead finger
4571 A precious ring, a ring that I must use
4572 In dear employment. Therefore hence, begone.
4573 But, if thou, jealous, dost return to pry
4574 In what I farther shall intend to do,
4575 By heaven, I will tear thee joint by joint
4576 And strew this hungry churchyard with thy limbs.
4577 The time and my intents are savage-wild,
4578 More fierce and more inexorable far
4579 Than empty tigers or the roaring sea.
4580 
4581 BALTHASAR
4582 I will be gone, sir, and not trouble you.
4583 
4584 ROMEO
4585 So shalt thou show me friendship. Take thou that.
4586 [Giving money.]
4587 Live and be prosperous, and farewell, good fellow.
4588 
4589 BALTHASAR, [aside]
4590 For all this same, I'll hide me hereabout.
4591 His looks I fear, and his intents I doubt.
4592 [He steps aside.]
4593 
4594 ROMEO, [beginning to force open the tomb]
4595 Thou detestable maw, thou womb of death,
4596 Gorged with the dearest morsel of the earth,
4597 Thus I enforce thy rotten jaws to open,
4598 And in despite I'll cram thee with more food.
4599 
4600 PARIS
4601 This is that banished haughty Montague
4602 That murdered my love's cousin, with which grief
4603 It is supposed the fair creature died,
4604 And here is come to do some villainous shame
4605 To the dead bodies. I will apprehend him.
4606 [Stepping forward.]
4607 Stop thy unhallowed toil, vile Montague.
4608 Can vengeance be pursued further than death?
4609 Condemned villain, I do apprehend thee.
4610 Obey and go with me, for thou must die.
4611 
4612 ROMEO
4613 I must indeed, and therefore came I hither.
4614 Good gentle youth, tempt not a desp'rate man.
4615 Fly hence and leave me. Think upon these gone.
4616 Let them affright thee. I beseech thee, youth,
4617 Put not another sin upon my head
4618 By urging me to fury. O, begone!
4619 By heaven, I love thee better than myself,
4620 For I come hither armed against myself.
4621 Stay not, begone, live, and hereafter say
4622 A madman's mercy bid thee run away.
4623 
4624 PARIS
4625 I do defy thy commination
4626 And apprehend thee for a felon here.
4627 
4628 ROMEO
4629 Wilt thou provoke me? Then have at thee, boy!
4630 [They draw and fight.]
4631 
4632 PAGE
4633 O Lord, they fight! I will go call the watch.
4634 [He exits.]
4635 
4636 PARIS
4637 O, I am slain! If thou be merciful,
4638 Open the tomb; lay me with Juliet.	[He dies.]
4639 
4640 ROMEO
4641 In faith, I will.--Let me peruse this face.
4642 Mercutio's kinsman, noble County Paris!
4643 What said my man when my betossed soul
4644 Did not attend him as we rode? I think
4645 He told me Paris should have married Juliet.
4646 Said he not so? Or did I dream it so?
4647 Or am I mad, hearing him talk of Juliet,
4648 To think it was so?--O, give me thy hand,
4649 One writ with me in sour misfortune's book!
4650 I'll bury thee in a triumphant grave.--
4651 [He opens the tomb.]
4652 A grave? O, no. A lantern, slaughtered youth,
4653 For here lies Juliet, and her beauty makes
4654 This vault a feasting presence full of light.--
4655 Death, lie thou there, by a dead man interred.
4656 [Laying Paris in the tomb.]
4657 How oft when men are at the point of death
4658 Have they been merry, which their keepers call
4659 A light'ning before death! O, how may I
4660 Call this a light'ning?--O my love, my wife,
4661 Death, that hath sucked the honey of thy breath,
4662 Hath had no power yet upon thy beauty.
4663 Thou art not conquered. Beauty's ensign yet
4664 Is crimson in thy lips and in thy cheeks,
4665 And death's pale flag is not advanced there.--
4666 Tybalt, liest thou there in thy bloody sheet?
4667 O, what more favor can I do to thee
4668 Than with that hand that cut thy youth in twain
4669 To sunder his that was thine enemy?
4670 Forgive me, cousin.--Ah, dear Juliet,
4671 Why art thou yet so fair? Shall I believe
4672 That unsubstantial death is amorous,
4673 And that the lean abhorred monster keeps
4674 Thee here in dark to be his paramour?
4675 For fear of that I still will stay with thee
4676 And never from this palace of dim night
4677 Depart again. Here, here will I remain
4678 With worms that are thy chambermaids. O, here
4679 Will I set up my everlasting rest
4680 And shake the yoke of inauspicious stars
4681 From this world-wearied flesh! Eyes, look your last.
4682 Arms, take your last embrace. And, lips, O, you
4683 The doors of breath, seal with a righteous kiss
4684 A dateless bargain to engrossing death.
4685 [Kissing Juliet.]
4686 Come, bitter conduct, come, unsavory guide!
4687 Thou desperate pilot, now at once run on
4688 The dashing rocks thy seasick weary bark!
4689 Here's to my love. [Drinking.] O true apothecary,
4690 Thy drugs are quick. Thus with a kiss I die.
4691 [He dies.]
4692 
4693 [Enter Friar Lawrence with lantern, crow, and spade.]
4694 
4695 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4696 Saint Francis be my speed! How oft tonight
4697 Have my old feet stumbled at graves!--Who's there?
4698 
4699 BALTHASAR
4700 Here's one, a friend, and one that knows you well.
4701 
4702 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4703 Bliss be upon you. Tell me, good my friend,
4704 What torch is yond that vainly lends his light
4705 To grubs and eyeless skulls? As I discern,
4706 It burneth in the Capels' monument.
4707 
4708 BALTHASAR
4709 It doth so, holy sir, and there's my master,
4710 One that you love.
4711 
4712 FRIAR LAWRENCE  Who is it?
4713 
4714 BALTHASAR  Romeo.
4715 
4716 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4717 How long hath he been there?
4718 
4719 BALTHASAR  Full half an hour.
4720 
4721 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4722 Go with me to the vault.
4723 
4724 BALTHASAR  I dare not, sir.
4725 My master knows not but I am gone hence,
4726 And fearfully did menace me with death
4727 If I did stay to look on his intents.
4728 
4729 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4730 Stay, then. I'll go alone. Fear comes upon me.
4731 O, much I fear some ill unthrifty thing.
4732 
4733 BALTHASAR
4734 As I did sleep under this yew tree here,
4735 I dreamt my master and another fought,
4736 And that my master slew him.
4737 
4738 FRIAR LAWRENCE, [moving toward the tomb]
4739 Romeo!--
4740 Alack, alack, what blood is this which stains
4741 The stony entrance of this sepulcher?
4742 What mean these masterless and gory swords
4743 To lie discolored by this place of peace?
4744 Romeo! O, pale! Who else? What, Paris too?
4745 And steeped in blood? Ah, what an unkind hour
4746 Is guilty of this lamentable chance!
4747 The lady stirs.
4748 
4749 JULIET
4750 O comfortable friar, where is my lord?
4751 I do remember well where I should be,
4752 And there I am. Where is my Romeo?
4753 
4754 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4755 I hear some noise.--Lady, come from that nest
4756 Of death, contagion, and unnatural sleep.
4757 A greater power than we can contradict
4758 Hath thwarted our intents. Come, come away.
4759 Thy husband in thy bosom there lies dead,
4760 And Paris, too. Come, I'll dispose of thee
4761 Among a sisterhood of holy nuns.
4762 Stay not to question, for the watch is coming.
4763 Come, go, good Juliet. I dare no longer stay.
4764 
4765 JULIET
4766 Go, get thee hence, for I will not away.
4767 [He exits.]
4768 What's here? A cup closed in my true love's hand?
4769 Poison, I see, hath been his timeless end.--
4770 O churl, drunk all, and left no friendly drop
4771 To help me after! I will kiss thy lips.
4772 Haply some poison yet doth hang on them,
4773 To make me die with a restorative.	[She kisses him.]
4774 Thy lips are warm!
4775 
4776 [Enter Paris's Page and Watch.]
4777 
4778 
4779 FIRST WATCH  Lead, boy. Which way?
4780 
4781 JULIET
4782 Yea, noise? Then I'll be brief. O, happy dagger,
4783 This is thy sheath. There rust, and let me die.
4784 [She takes Romeo's dagger, stabs herself, and dies.]
4785 
4786 PAGE
4787 This is the place, there where the torch doth burn.
4788 
4789 FIRST WATCH
4790 The ground is bloody.--Search about the
4791 churchyard.
4792 Go, some of you; whoe'er you find, attach.
4793 [Some watchmen exit.]
4794 Pitiful sight! Here lies the County slain,
4795 And Juliet bleeding, warm, and newly dead,
4796 Who here hath lain this two days buried.--
4797 Go, tell the Prince. Run to the Capulets.
4798 Raise up the Montagues. Some others search.
4799 [Others exit.]
4800 We see the ground whereon these woes do lie,
4801 But the true ground of all these piteous woes
4802 We cannot without circumstance descry.
4803 
4804 [Enter Watchmen with Romeo's man Balthasar.]
4805 
4806 
4807 SECOND WATCH
4808 Here's Romeo's man. We found him in the
4809 churchyard.
4810 
4811 FIRST WATCH
4812 Hold him in safety till the Prince come hither.
4813 
4814 [Enter Friar Lawrence and another Watchman.]
4815 
4816 
4817 THIRD WATCH
4818 Here is a friar that trembles, sighs, and weeps.
4819 We took this mattock and this spade from him
4820 As he was coming from this churchyard's side.
4821 
4822 FIRST WATCH
4823 A great suspicion. Stay the Friar too.
4824 
4825 [Enter the Prince with Attendants.]
4826 
4827 
4828 PRINCE
4829 What misadventure is so early up
4830 That calls our person from our morning rest?
4831 
4832 [Enter Capulet and Lady Capulet.]
4833 
4834 
4835 CAPULET
4836 What should it be that is so shrieked abroad?
4837 
4838 LADY CAPULET
4839 O, the people in the street cry "Romeo,"
4840 Some "Juliet," and some "Paris," and all run
4841 With open outcry toward our monument.
4842 
4843 PRINCE
4844 What fear is this which startles in our ears?
4845 
4846 FIRST WATCH
4847 Sovereign, here lies the County Paris slain,
4848 And Romeo dead, and Juliet, dead before,
4849 Warm and new killed.
4850 
4851 PRINCE
4852 Search, seek, and know how this foul murder
4853 comes.
4854 
4855 FIRST WATCH
4856 Here is a friar, and slaughtered Romeo's man,
4857 With instruments upon them fit to open
4858 These dead men's tombs.
4859 
4860 CAPULET
4861 O heavens! O wife, look how our daughter bleeds!
4862 This dagger hath mista'en, for, lo, his house
4863 Is empty on the back of Montague,
4864 And it mis-sheathed in my daughter's bosom.
4865 
4866 LADY CAPULET
4867 O me, this sight of death is as a bell
4868 That warns my old age to a sepulcher.
4869 
4870 [Enter Montague.]
4871 
4872 
4873 PRINCE
4874 Come, Montague, for thou art early up
4875 To see thy son and heir now early down.
4876 
4877 MONTAGUE
4878 Alas, my liege, my wife is dead tonight.
4879 Grief of my son's exile hath stopped her breath.
4880 What further woe conspires against mine age?
4881 
4882 PRINCE  Look, and thou shalt see.
4883 
4884 MONTAGUE, [seeing Romeo dead]
4885 O thou untaught! What manners is in this,
4886 To press before thy father to a grave?
4887 
4888 PRINCE
4889 Seal up the mouth of outrage for awhile,
4890 Till we can clear these ambiguities
4891 And know their spring, their head, their true
4892 descent,
4893 And then will I be general of your woes
4894 And lead you even to death. Meantime forbear,
4895 And let mischance be slave to patience.--
4896 Bring forth the parties of suspicion.
4897 
4898 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4899 I am the greatest, able to do least,
4900 Yet most suspected, as the time and place
4901 Doth make against me, of this direful murder.
4902 And here I stand, both to impeach and purge
4903 Myself condemned and myself excused.
4904 
4905 PRINCE
4906 Then say at once what thou dost know in this.
4907 
4908 FRIAR LAWRENCE
4909 I will be brief, for my short date of breath
4910 Is not so long as is a tedious tale.
4911 Romeo, there dead, was husband to that Juliet,
4912 And she, there dead, that Romeo's faithful wife.
4913 I married them, and their stol'n marriage day
4914 Was Tybalt's doomsday, whose untimely death
4915 Banished the new-made bridegroom from this city,
4916 For whom, and not for Tybalt, Juliet pined.
4917 You, to remove that siege of grief from her,
4918 Betrothed and would have married her perforce
4919 To County Paris. Then comes she to me,
4920 And with wild looks bid me devise some mean
4921 To rid her from this second marriage,
4922 Or in my cell there would she kill herself.
4923 Then gave I her (so tutored by my art)
4924 A sleeping potion, which so took effect
4925 As I intended, for it wrought on her
4926 The form of death. Meantime I writ to Romeo
4927 That he should hither come as this dire night
4928 To help to take her from her borrowed grave,
4929 Being the time the potion's force should cease.
4930 But he which bore my letter, Friar John,
4931 Was stayed by accident, and yesternight
4932 Returned my letter back. Then all alone
4933 At the prefixed hour of her waking
4934 Came I to take her from her kindred's vault,
4935 Meaning to keep her closely at my cell
4936 Till I conveniently could send to Romeo.
4937 But when I came, some minute ere the time
4938 Of her awakening, here untimely lay
4939 The noble Paris and true Romeo dead.
4940 She wakes, and I entreated her come forth
4941 And bear this work of heaven with patience.
4942 But then a noise did scare me from the tomb,
4943 And she, too desperate, would not go with me
4944 But, as it seems, did violence on herself.
4945 All this I know, and to the marriage
4946 Her nurse is privy. And if aught in this
4947 Miscarried by my fault, let my old life
4948 Be sacrificed some hour before his time
4949 Unto the rigor of severest law.
4950 
4951 PRINCE
4952 We still have known thee for a holy man.--
4953 Where's Romeo's man? What can he say to this?
4954 
4955 BALTHASAR
4956 I brought my master news of Juliet's death,
4957 And then in post he came from Mantua
4958 To this same place, to this same monument.
4959 This letter he early bid me give his father
4960 And threatened me with death, going in the vault,
4961 If I departed not and left him there.
4962 
4963 PRINCE
4964 Give me the letter. I will look on it.--
4965 [He takes Romeo's letter.]
4966 Where is the County's page, that raised the
4967 watch?--
4968 Sirrah, what made your master in this place?
4969 
4970 PAGE
4971 He came with flowers to strew his lady's grave
4972 And bid me stand aloof, and so I did.
4973 Anon comes one with light to ope the tomb,
4974 And by and by my master drew on him,
4975 And then I ran away to call the watch.
4976 
4977 PRINCE
4978 This letter doth make good the Friar's words,
4979 Their course of love, the tidings of her death;
4980 And here he writes that he did buy a poison
4981 Of a poor 'pothecary, and therewithal
4982 Came to this vault to die and lie with Juliet.
4983 Where be these enemies?--Capulet, Montague,
4984 See what a scourge is laid upon your hate,
4985 That heaven finds means to kill your joys with love,
4986 And I, for winking at your discords too,
4987 Have lost a brace of kinsmen. All are punished.
4988 
4989 CAPULET
4990 O brother Montague, give me thy hand.
4991 This is my daughter's jointure, for no more
4992 Can I demand.
4993 
4994 MONTAGUE  But I can give thee more,
4995 For I will ray her statue in pure gold,
4996 That whiles Verona by that name is known,
4997 There shall no figure at such rate be set
4998 As that of true and faithful Juliet.
4999 
5000 CAPULET
5001 As rich shall Romeo's by his lady's lie,
5002 Poor sacrifices of our enmity.
5003 
5004 PRINCE
5005 A glooming peace this morning with it brings.
5006 The sun for sorrow will not show his head.
5007 Go hence to have more talk of these sad things.
5008 Some shall be pardoned, and some punished.
5009 For never was a story of more woe
5010 Than this of Juliet and her Romeo.
5011 [All exit.]
